Therapeutic bronchoscopy
### Question  ###
A 64 year old man with stage IIIB mediastinal large B cell lymphoma and a 45 pack year smoking history presents with progressive stridor and orthopnea. He is on apixaban for atrial fibrillation (last dose 24 hours ago). CT chest with 1 mm slices shows ~80% lower tracheal narrowing from a smooth, crescentic, purely extrinsic mass effect; there is no bronchus sign or endoluminal tissue. On flexible bronchoscopy under moderate sedation, the mucosa is intact with a long, crescentic indentation extending to the main carina; distal airways are visible but critically narrowed. His dyspnea score is 8/10; SpO₂ is 92% on 2 L/min nasal cannula. He needs urgent palliation to allow systemic therapy next week. Which is the next best step to restore airway patency today?
 ### Options### 
(a) Nd:YAG laser photoresection
(b) Argon plasma coagulation of the indentation
(c) Airway stenting across the distal trachea/carina after gentle balloon dilation
(d) Electrocautery snare resection of the indentation
(e) Same session photodynamic therapy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Pure extrinsic compression is not relieved by ablative tools; airway stenting after careful dilation is preferred (choice C correct). Laser, electrocautery, and APC act on intraluminal tissue; without a mass to debulk, they do not restore caliber and can injure normal mucosa. Evidence syntheses and guidelines emphasize stenting—often bifurcated if the carina is involved—for extrinsic or mixed lesions, with mechanical dilation immediately beforehand as needed. Ernst et al. and ACCP guidance describe thermal tools as ineffective for purely extrinsic lesions, whereas stents rapidly palliate dyspnea and support subsequent oncologic therapy. Ost et al. show therapeutic bronchoscopy improves symptoms and quality of life when the chosen modality matches the mechanism of obstruction. 
Pearl: Match the tool to the pathology—ablation for endoluminal disease; scaffolding for extrinsic collapse.
Why others are wrong: Laser (A) and electrocautery (D) require tissue to cut/coagulate and risk perforation when fired at normal wall; APC (B) provides superficial coagulation and will not lift an externally flattened lumen; PDT (E) has a delayed effect and does not relieve extrinsic compression acutely.
###References###
1.	Ernst A, Feller Kopman D, Becker HD, Mehta AC. Central airway obstruction. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2004;169:1278 97. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15132964/
2.	Ernst A, Silvestri GA, Johnstone D. Interventional pulmonary procedures: guidelines. Chest. 2003;123:1693 717. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12740280/
3.	Ost DE, et al. Therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant central airway obstruction: success & impact. Chest. 2015;147:1282 98. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25321361/
________________________________________
### Question  ###
A 58 year old woman with a friable, oozing tracheal squamous carcinoma is intubated with a laser safe tube. She is on volume controlled ventilation (tidal volume 6 mL/kg, PEEP 5 cm H₂O) with FiO₂ 0.70 for baseline PaO₂ 62 mm Hg. You plan short pulse Nd:YAG laser to coagulate the bleeding, then mechanical debulking. The anesthesiologist asks about ventilator adjustments during lasing. Which immediate step best reduces catastrophic complications while allowing treatment?
 ### Options### 
(a) Increase PEEP to 10 cm H₂O to prevent derecruitment during firing
(b) Switch to spontaneous ventilation to lower airway pressures
(c) Pack the trachea with dry gauze around the tumor
(d) Raise FiO₂ to 1.0 between pulses to maintain reserve
(e) Reduce and maintain FiO₂ at the lowest feasible level (ideally ≤0.40–0.50) during lasing
###Discussion###
Bottom line: The single most important step before firing is to lower and maintain FiO₂ as low as feasible—ideally ≤0.40–0.50 (choice E correct) to mitigate airway fire. Classic safety series and consensus precautions for bronchoscopic Nd:YAG emphasize oxygen limitation, short pulses, and careful beam orientation; fires and tube ignition are strongly associated with high FiO₂. Shapshay & Beamis and Dumon et al. detail these fire prevention principles; Casey highlights practical steps to avert endotracheal fires. 
Pearl: Before any thermal energy in the airway, say out loud: “What’s the FiO₂?”
Why others are wrong: Raising PEEP (A) or changing mode (B) does not address ignition risk and may destabilize gas exchange; packing with gauze (C) introduces flammable material; “topping off” with 100% oxygen between pulses (D) increases fire risk and can be forgotten when firing resumes, a known pitfall.
###References###
1.	Dumon JF, et al. Principles for safety in Nd:YAG laser bronchology. Chest. 1984;86:163 8. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/6734303/
2.	Shapshay SM, Beamis JF. Safety precautions for bronchoscopic Nd:YAG laser surgery. Otolaryngol Head Neck Surg. 1986;94:175 80. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/3951110/
3.	Casey KR. Preventing endotracheal fires. Chest. 1987;91:637. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/3829762/

### Question  ###
A 71 year old man with metastatic renal cell carcinoma and recurrent hemoptysis undergoes flexible bronchoscopy. A vascular, oozing endobronchial lesion narrows the right main bronchus (60% area). He is on 3 L/min oxygen (SpO₂ 93%). You plan argon plasma coagulation (APC) for hemostasis and to prep for mechanical debulking. The APC unit has probes of 1.5 mm and 2.3 mm and flow settings from 0.3 to 1.8 L/min. Which technique most reduces the risk of life threatening gas embolism while achieving coagulation?
 ### Options### 
(a) Use high argon flow (≥2 L/min) to clear blood and improve plasma stability
(b) Keep the probe non contact (≈1–3 mm), ensure the target is the nearest tissue, use short bursts, and the lowest effective flow (≤~1 L/min)
(c) Press the probe tip against the tumor to prevent gas tracking
(d) Occlude the distal airway with the bronchoscope to trap gas and increase dwell time
(e) Insufflate CO₂ through the scope to “dilute” any argon
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Non contact technique with the lowest effective argon flow (≤~1 L/min) and short bursts (choice B correct) mitigates intravascular gas entry, the key mechanism of APC embolism. Animal and mechanistic work demonstrate that higher flow elevates intraluminal pressure and promotes gas migration into damaged vessels; keeping the lesion as the nearest surface, staying 1–3 mm off tissue, and painting in brief activations reduces risk while preserving hemostasis. Folch et al. clarified the pathophysiology; Feller Kopman et al. showed gas emboli to the heart during bronchoscopic ablation under higher flow conditions; practical technique articles echo flow minimization and non contact use. 
Pitfall: Contacting tissue with the APC probe increases local pressure and raises embolism risk.
Why others are wrong: High flow (A) worsens pressure; contact activation (C) injects gas; airway occlusion (D) raises distal pressure; adding CO₂ (E) does not address the mechanism and may worsen pressure dynamics.
###References###
1.	Folch EE, et al. Argon plasma coagulation: mechanism of gas embolism. Respiration. 2021. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33591756/
2.	Feller Kopman D, et al. Gas flow during bronchoscopic ablation causes gas emboli. Chest. 2008;133:892 6. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/18263679/
3.	Tremblay A, Marquette CH. Endobronchial electrocautery & APC: a practical approach. Can Respir J. 2004;11:305 10. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15510248/
________________________________________
### Question  ###
A 69 year old man with a 30 pack year history presents with dyspnea and oozing endobronchial tumor in the distal trachea. You are deciding between Nd:YAG and CO₂ lasers to combine with mechanical debulking. CT shows a 2 cm polypoid lesion; distal airway is visible. You want deep coagulation for hemostasis and to shrink tumor bulk before forceps extraction. Which statement best reflects tissue–laser interactions relevant to this case?
 ### Options### 
(a) CO₂ lasers penetrate deeper into tissue than Nd:YAG
(b) Argon (blue green) lasers have poor hemoglobin absorption and limited hemostasis
(c) Nd:YAG functions primarily as a surface photoablation tool with minimal scatter
(d) Nd:YAG produces volumetric heating with deeper coagulation than CO₂, which provides shallow, precise cutting
(e) All airway lasers can be delivered through flexible quartz fibers
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Nd:YAG scatters and heats tissue volumetrically, enabling deeper coagulation, whereas CO₂ is highly surface absorbed and cuts precisely with shallow penetration (choice D correct). Classic studies and reviews detail wavelength dependent absorption: CO₂ (10,600 nm) is strongly absorbed by water with ~sub mm penetration and precise cutting; Nd:YAG (1060 nm) has deeper penetration with greater scatter for coagulation/hemostasis; argon/KTP wavelengths are avidly absorbed by hemoglobin for photocoagulation. CO₂ generally cannot be transmitted through standard flexible quartz fibers, requiring an articulating arm and rigid bronchoscopy, while Nd:YAG uses flexible fibers. These properties guide tool selection when hemostasis depth matters. 
Pearl: Pick Nd:YAG when you need deep coagulation; pick CO₂ when you need shallow, precise cuts.
Why others are wrong: (A) reverses depth; (B) argon has strong Hb absorption; (C) Nd:YAG is not surface limited; (E) CO₂ is not fiber delivered in typical systems.
###References###
1.	Bolliger CT, Sutedja TG, Strausz J, Freitag L. Therapeutic bronchoscopy with immediate effect. Eur Respir J. 2006;27:1258 71. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16772308/
2.	Shapshay SM. Laser applications in trachea and bronchi. Laryngoscope. 1987;97(Suppl):1 26. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/3586811/
________________________________________
### Question  ###
A 73 year old community hospital considers adding an ablative modality to palliate malignant central airway obstruction. Their case mix is mainly polypoid intraluminal tumors; rigid bronchoscopy is available; budget is tight. They ask which investment yields effective immediate recanalization with lower acquisition and maintenance costs. What should you recommend?
 ### Options### 
(a) Electrocautery using the hospital’s standard high frequency generator with bronchoscopic accessories
(b) Nd:YAG laser tower with cooling, multiple fibers, and safety infrastructure
(c) KTP laser platform for photocoagulation
(d) CO₂ laser with articulating arm for rigid bronchoscopy
(e) Visible spectrum argon laser as a standalone tool
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Electrocautery achieves similar near term clinical results to laser for many intraluminal lesions at substantially lower cost (choice A correct). Cost effectiveness analyses and series show electrosurgery can obviate the need for laser in most cases with high technical success and symptom relief. Boxem et al. found electrocautery cost effective versus Nd:YAG. Coulter & Mehta described successful palliation with electrosurgery reducing laser use. Reviews corroborate comparable outcomes when operators are experienced. 
Pearl: In many hospitals, electrosurgery = laser like results for palliation at a fraction of the cost.
Why others are wrong: Laser platforms (B–D) require expensive consoles, fibers, safety eyewear, and maintenance, without a guaranteed efficacy advantage for typical intraluminal tumors; a visible argon laser (E) alone lacks robust debulking power and is largely supplanted by APC for non contact coagulation.
###References###
1.	Boxem T, et al. Nd:YAG vs electrocautery—cost effectiveness. Chest. 1999;116:1108 12. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10559089/
2.	Coulter TD, Mehta AC. Impact of endobronchial electrosurgery on laser need. Chest. 2000;118:516 21. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10936139/
3.	Bolliger CT, et al. Therapeutic bronchoscopy with immediate effect. Eur Respir J. 2006;27:1258 71. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16772308/

### Question  ###
A 62 year old man with squamous cell lung cancer (active smoker, 50 pack years) has recurrent small volume hemoptysis and exertional dyspnea. CT shows eccentric endotracheal soft tissue with patent distal airway. Bronchoscopy reveals a friable, oozing plaque along the anterior tracheal wall; FiO₂ during the case is 0.40. You want rapid hemostasis and superficial devitalization, then mechanical extraction. Which tool is best as the initial energy modality?
 ### Options### 
(a) Cryotherapy probe
(b) Nd:YAG laser alone
(c) Argon plasma coagulation
(d) Cold saline lavage alone
(e) Balloon tamponade without ablation
###Discussion###
Bottom line: APC provides rapid, superficial non contact coagulation well suited for oozing mucosal tumors (choice C correct). Prospective and large series reported immediate control of hemoptysis and significant reduction in endoluminal obstruction when APC is combined with mechanical debulking. Morice et al. showed immediate hemoptysis control with APC and sizable lumen gain; Reichle et al. reported prompt hemostasis in >99% of cases with APC. The non contact, millimeter depth injury profile makes APC efficient for mucosal oozing in a moderate FiO₂ environment. 
Pearl: APC is for paint on hemostasis—shallow and fast.
Why others are wrong: Cryotherapy (A) is safe in high FiO₂ but has delayed effects; Nd:YAG (B) can control bleeding but is less efficient for diffuse oozing and demands stricter fire precautions; cold saline (D) and tamponade (E) are supportive measures, not definitive therapy for recurrent bleeding.
###References###
1.	Morice RC, et al. Endobronchial APC for hemoptysis/obstruction. Chest. 2001;119:781 7. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/11243965/
2.	Reichle G, et al. APC in bronchology. Pneumologie. 2000;54:508 16. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/11103359/

### Question  ###
A 68 year old woman with prior sleeve resection develops tight granulation at the bronchial anastomosis. She is ventilated for pneumonia; FiO₂ cannot be decreased below 0.80 without desaturation to 84%. Urgent immediate recanalization is not required today, but you want to treat endoluminal tissue safely. Which modality is the best choice now?
 ### Options### 
(a) Nd:YAG laser
(b) Cryotherapy
(c) Argon plasma coagulation
(d) Electrocautery
(e) Photodynamic therapy this session
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Cryotherapy is safest when FiO₂ must remain high, as it does not support combustion (choice B correct). Reviews comparing thermal modalities emphasize that all “hot” tools (laser, APC, EC) require FiO₂ reduction to mitigate fire, whereas cryotherapy can be applied without altering oxygen delivery—an advantage in unstable hypoxemia. Its tradeoff is delayed cytotoxicity; pairing with mechanical debulking can accelerate lumen gain when needed. 
Pearl: When FiO₂ can’t come down, the “cold” tool (cryo) comes out.
Why others are wrong: Laser (A), APC (C), and EC (D) require FiO₂ reduction to ≤~0.40–0.50 during firing; PDT (E) is delayed and not a same session solution for urgent obstruction.
###References###
1.	Bolliger CT, et al. Therapeutic bronchoscopy with immediate effect. Eur Respir J. 2006;27:1258 71. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16772308/
2.	Rosell A, Stratakos G. Therapeutic bronchoscopy for central airway diseases. Eur Respir Rev. 2020;29:190178. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/32047056/

### Question  ###
A 55 year old man with a silicone tracheal stent for malignant obstruction returns with tumor overgrowth near the proximal stent margin. He is mildly dyspneic; FiO₂ is 0.30. You plan to ablate tumor, then re evaluate stent need. Which strategy best minimizes catastrophic complications?
 ### Options### 
(a) Proceed with low power Nd:YAG (20 W) while leaving the stent in place
(b) Cover the exposed stent surface with dry gauze to shield it during lasing
(c) Switch to an argon or KTP laser, which are “safer” for silicone
(d) Fire perpendicular to the stent to shorten pulse time
(e) Remove the stent if feasible before lasing and/or use non thermal/mechanical debulking
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Remove or avoid firing around airway stents—especially silicone—because even low power can ignite or damage stents (choice E correct). In vitro and clinical reports show silicone stents ignite with Nd:YAG at low power densities; metallic stents can be damaged by several lasers. Safety recommendations include removing stents before laser or choosing non thermal/mechanical techniques. Scherer et al. documented silicone stent ignition; Witt and Hautmann demonstrated laser damage to metallic stents; Dalupang et al. delimited unsafe power ranges. 
Pitfall: “Low power” does not mean “safe” on silicone—fires have occurred at low settings.
Why others are wrong: Leaving the stent in place with “low power” (A) is unsafe; gauze (B) is flammable; changing wavelength (C) does not eliminate risk; perpendicular firing (D) can worsen damage and penetration.
###References###
1.	Scherer TA, et al. Nd:YAG laser ignition of silicone stents. Chest. 2000;117:1449 54. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10807829/
2.	Witt C, et al. Nd:YAG laser & tracheobronchial metallic stents (in vitro). Lasers Surg Med. 1997;20:51 5. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/9041461/
3.	Hautmann H, Huber RM. Laser resistance of expandable metal stents. Lasers Surg Med. 2001;29:70 2. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/11494277/
4.	Dalupang JJ, Shanks TG, Colt HG. Nd:YAG damage to metal & silicone stents. Chest. 2001;120:934 40. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/11591576/

### Question  ###
A 47 year old man with a pedunculated tracheal tumor (CT shows clear distal trachea) will undergo Nd:YAG assisted debulking under general anesthesia through a rigid bronchoscope. The team asks how to fire to minimize perforation while maintaining hemostasis. Which technique is best?
 ### Options### 
(a) Aim parallel to the airway wall, keep the fiber ≈5–10 mm from tissue, start at low power with short pulses, and work circumferentially from the periphery inward
(b) Aim perpendicular to the wall for faster penetration and vaporization
(c) Keep FiO₂ at 1.0 to maintain oxygen reserve during firing
(d) Use long (>1 s) pulses at high power for rapid vaporization
(e) Keep the fiber tip flush with the bronchoscope tip for stability
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Fire parallel to the wall, start low with short pulses, and work from periphery to center (choice A correct). Classic safety guidance for Nd:YAG in airway procedures stresses beam orientation and pulse strategy to avoid deep penetration and perforation while maintaining coagulation; Dumon et al. and Shapshay & Beamis specify aiming parallel, short pulses, and periphery to center technique. FiO₂ should be minimized, not maximized, during firing. Keeping the fiber beyond the scope tip lowers scope damage/fire risk. 
Pearl: “Parallel, peripheral, short pulse” is the default for safe YAG firing.
Why others are wrong: Perpendicular firing (B) increases perforation risk; FiO₂ 1.0 (C) raises fire risk; long, high power pulses (D) have unpredictable depth; fiber flush with the scope (E) risks scope ignition/damage.
###References###
1.	Dumon JF, et al. Safety in Nd:YAG laser bronchology. Chest. 1984;86:163 8. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/6734303/
2.	Shapshay SM, Beamis JF. Laser safety precautions. Otolaryngol Head Neck Surg. 1986;94:175 80. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/3951110/
3.	Lee P, Kupeli E, Mehta AC. Therapeutic bronchoscopy in lung cancer. Clin Chest Med. 2002;23:241 56. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12122967/

### Question  ###
A 66 year old man with NSCLC undergoes therapeutic bronchoscopy for mixed tracheobronchial obstruction. Your quality committee asks which modality specific complications have been associated in multicenter registry data to guide consent ###Discussion###s. Which pattern is most consistent?
 ### Options### 
(a) Electrocautery → more hemorrhage; APC → respiratory failure
(b) Laser → gas embolism; cryotherapy → fire risk
(c) APC → airway perforation more than laser
(d) APC → bleeding requiring intervention; electrocautery → unexpected respiratory failure
(e) No modality specific associations were identified
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In AQuIRE data, APC use was associated with bleeding requiring intervention, and electrocautery with unexpected respiratory failure (choice D correct). The overall complication rate was low, but modality specific associations were detected after adjustment. These findings inform risk ###Discussion### rather than mandate tool avoidance. Ost et al. (success/impact and complications papers) provide the registry based evidence. Ernst et al. reported overall risk adjusted morbidity/mortality across therapeutic bronchoscopic procedures in an earlier multi institutional database. 
Pearl: Even with low overall risk, discuss modality specific patterns—especially APC linked bleeding.
Why others are wrong: (A) reverses associations; (B) gas embolism has been reported with APC and other gas flow systems, and cryotherapy does not carry fire risk; (C) perforation is not the key APC signal; (E) is incorrect—associations were reported.
###References###
1.	Ost DE, et al. Complications after therapeutic bronchoscopy (AQuIRE). Chest. 2015;148:450 71. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25846475/
2.	Ost DE, et al. Success & impact of therapeutic bronchoscopy. Chest. 2015;147:1282 98. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25321361/
3.	Ernst A, et al. Risk adjusted morbidity/mortality analysis. Chest. 2008;134:514 9. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/18682461/

### Question  ###
A 67 year old man with stage IV esophageal adenocarcinoma (30 pack years) has 3 weeks of progressive stridor and cough with liquids. CT (1 mm slices) shows 75% narrowing of the distal trachea and carina by circumferential extrinsic compression; no endoluminal tissue is seen. Bronchoscopy confirms smooth mucosa with a long crescentic indentation; distal right and left mainstem bronchi are visible. He is not a surgical candidate; systemic therapy will start next week. He needs immediate palliation to breathe and prevent aspiration. What is the next best step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Nd:YAG laser photoresection of the indentation
(b) Cryotherapy alone
(c) Bifurcated (Y shaped) airway stenting across the carina after gentle dilation
(d) Photodynamic therapy this session
(e) External beam radiation as the sole immediate intervention
###Discussion###
Bottom line: For pure extrinsic malignant carinal compression, a bifurcated airway stent after dilation provides rapid, effective palliation (choice C correct). Silicone or metallic Y stents restore caliber at the distal trachea and both main bronchi and can improve cough/aspiration when the esophagus is involved. Dumon Y stent series and subsequent cohorts report prompt symptom relief with acceptable safety; broader CAO literature emphasizes stenting for extrinsic or mixed disease, reserving ablation for intraluminal tumor. Radiation and PDT are delayed therapies and not suitable as sole immediate measures. 
Pearl: Extrinsic = scaffold; intraluminal = debulk.
Why others are wrong: Laser (A) and cryo (B) act on endoluminal tissue only; PDT (D) has delayed effects; radiation alone (E) will not open the airway now.
###References###
1.	Dutau H, et al. Dumon Y stent for malignant disease involving the carina. Chest. 2004;126:951 8. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15364766/
2.	Ernst A, et al. Central airway obstruction. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2004;169:1278 97. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15132964/
3.	Ost DE, et al. Therapeutic bronchoscopy outcomes. Chest. 2015;147:1282 98. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25321361/
________________________________________
### Question  ###
A 72 year old woman with squamous cell carcinoma compressing and distorting the carina requires palliative bifurcation stenting. She is dyspneic at rest (SpO₂ 90% on 3 L/min). CT shows severe angulation of the left main bronchus and an irregular carinal spur. You have access to covered nitinol self expanding Y stents and silicone Dumon Y stents. Which device is most likely to have higher immediate first attempt implantation success in this malignant carinal anatomy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Self expanding covered nitinol Y stent
(b) Silicone Dumon Y stent
(c) Dynamic (hybrid) Y stent with posterior membrane
(d) Two straight silicone stents side by side
(e) Hourglass straight silicone stent
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Self expanding metallic Y stents have higher first attempt deployment success in malignant carinal disease compared with silicone Y stents (choice A correct). A single center comparison (Lachkar et al.) found higher immediate technical success with metallic Y stents and similar early/late adverse events; multicenter experiences (Madan; Qiao) support feasibility/safety of integrated self expanding Y stents in distorted carinas. Silicone Y stents (B) work well but require more manipulation and may fail on first attempt. Hybrid “dynamic” stents (C) are not typically first line for highly distorted malignant carinas. Two straight stents (D) or an hourglass stent (E) do not reliably treat true carinal bifurcation disease. 
Pearl: When the carina is irregular and angulated, self expanding Y stents seat more predictably.
###References###
1.	Lachkar S, et al. Metallic vs silicone Y stents for malignant carina lesions. Respir Med Res. 2020;78:100767. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33220482/
2.	Madan K, et al. Multicenter experience with self expanding metallic Y stents. J Bronchology Interv Pulmonol. 2016;23:29 38. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26744897/
3.	Qiao Y, et al. Integrated self expanding Y stent for carinal stenosis. Radiol Med. 2016;121:744 50. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26892131/

### Question  ###
A 68 year old man with advanced esophageal cancer has severe cough with liquids and repeated aspiration pneumonias. An uncovered esophageal stent placed last week did not stop leakage; CT shows a 6 mm tracheoesophageal fistula at the carina with partial protrusion of the esophageal stent into the airway. Bronchoscopy confirms a large TEF. He is not fit for surgery. What is the next best step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Replace the esophageal stent with a larger esophageal stent only
(b) Photodynamic therapy of the fistula tract
(c) External beam radiation alone
(d) Add a silicone airway Y stent to complement the esophageal stent (“double stenting”)
(e) Observation with thickened liquids
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In malignant TEF involving the carina, “double stenting” with a silicone Y airway stent in addition to the esophageal stent improves sealing and ventilation (choice D correct). Airway stenting prevents esophageal stent protrusion and helps cover the fistula; series document improved symptoms and aspiration control when esophageal stenting alone fails. Freitag et al. first reported airway/double stenting for malignant TEF; more recent small series (Wang et al.) describe Y silicone tracheal stents successfully sealing esophagorespiratory fistulas related to esophageal stents. Upsizing the esophageal stent (A) alone often fails; PDT (B) and radiation (C) are delayed and rarely seal large TEFs promptly; observation (E) is unsafe. 
Pearl: If an esophageal stent doesn’t seal a malignant TEF, add an airway stent—not a bigger esophageal stent.
###References###
1.	Freitag L, et al. Airway/double stenting for malignant esophago tracheal fistulas. Chest. 1996;110:1155 60. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/8915210/
2.	Wang Q, et al. Y silicone tracheal stent for malignant esophageal stent ERF. J Thorac Dis. 2024;16:948 59. Publisher: https://jtd.amegroups.org/article/view/86298/html
3.	Dutau H, et al. Dumon Y stent series. Chest. 2004;126:951 8. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15364766/

### Question  ###
A 46 year old woman with complex post tracheostomy tracheal stenosis (short, rigid segment with cartilage involvement) has recurrence after two balloon dilations. She is a potential surgical candidate but needs months to optimize nutrition and wean steroids. You must temporize the airway without compromising future surgery. Which option best fits?
 ### Options### 
(a) Uncovered self expanding metal stent as a “permanent” solution
(b) Removable silicone stent
(c) Fully covered metal stent as permanent therapy
(d) Repeat dilations only until surgery
(e) Photodynamic therapy of the stenosis
###Discussion###
Bottom line: When benign central airway stenosis requires stenting, a removable silicone stent preserves future surgical options (choice B correct). Silicone stents can be repositioned/removed and are preferred to uncovered metal stents, which embed and are difficult or hazardous to remove; even fully covered metal stents are generally avoided for long term benign disease. Series in benign stenosis (e.g., Martinez Ballarin; Brichet) showed silicone stent tolerance and acceptable outcomes as a bridge to definitive therapy. Repeated dilation alone (D) has high recurrence in complex lesions; PDT (E) does not remodel fixed fibrostenosis. 
Pearl: In benign disease, use what you can remove—silicone.
###References###
1.	Martinez Ballarin JL, et al. Silicone stents in benign stenoses. Chest. 1996;110:626 9. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/8697853/
2.	Brichet A, et al. Management of post intubation tracheal stenoses. Eur Respir J. 1999;13:888 93. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10362070/

### Question  ###
A 61 year old man with a silicone tracheobronchial stent placed for malignant CAO presents with fever and productive cough 6 weeks later. CT shows tree in bud opacities in the right middle lobe; bronchoscopy reveals thick secretions within the stent. He asks about infection risk after stenting and whether he should have been on preventive antibiotics. Which statement is most accurate?
 ### Options### 
(a) Stent associated infection is rare (<1%)
(b) Fungal ball is the most common infectious presentation
(c) Routine antibiotic prophylaxis prevents most infections
(d) Any bacterial colonization mandates permanent stent removal
(e) Infections occur in a meaningful minority (~1 in 5), with pneumonia most common
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Infections after airway stenting occur in a substantial minority (~19%), and pneumonia is the most common syndrome (choice E correct). A systematic review of 23 studies (501 patients) reported infection in about one fifth of stented patients; pneumonia dominated, followed by bronchial infection and lung abscess/fungus ball. Colonization is common and does not mandate automatic stent removal; management is individualized (airway toilet, exchange if obstructed). There is no evidence that routine prophylactic antibiotics prevent these events. Noppen et al. documented bacterial colonization after stenting. 
Pearl: Expect infections after stenting—optimize secretion management and surveillance rather than routine prophylaxis.
Why others are wrong: (A) underestimates risk; (B) fungus ball is less common than pneumonia; (C) prophylaxis is unsupported; (D) colonization alone is not an indication for permanent removal.
###References###
1.	Agrafiotis M, Siempos II, Falagas ME. Infections related to airway stenting. Respiration. 2009;78:69 74. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19147910/
2.	Noppen M, et al. Bacterial colonization after stenting. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 1999;160:672 7. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10430733/

### Question  ###
A 59 year old lung transplant recipient on tacrolimus and prednisone requires a bronchial stent for anastomotic stenosis. A separate 59 year old immunocompetent man undergoes the same procedure for post tuberculosis stenosis. Which factor is associated with less exuberant granulation tissue formation within the stent?
 ### Options### 
(a) Chronic immunosuppression
(b) Undersizing the stent to reduce wall contact
(c) Using an uncovered metal stent to “ventilate” the mucosa
(d) Excess stent length across normal airway
(e) High dynamic friction from a loose fitting stent
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Immunosuppression is associated with less in stent granulation tissue (choice A correct). In observational work, immunosuppressed patients developed less granulation than immunocompetent controls. Histologic studies of noncovered metal stents show basal membrane destruction and microvascular erosion leading to papillomatous granulation; uncovered designs do not prevent hyperproliferation. Undersizing, excessive length, or loose fit increase focal pressure and friction—mechanisms driving granulation and restenosis. 
Pearl: Fit matters—tight enough to prevent motion, not so tight that it crushes mucosa.
###References###
1.	Shlomi D, et al. Immunosuppression and stent granulation. Laryngoscope. 2008;118:1383 8. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/18401282/
2.	Grewe PH, et al. Reaction of tracheobronchial wall to noncovered metal stents. Chest. 2005;128:986 90. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16100177/

### Question  ###
Your fluoroscopy suite is down. A 63 year old man with malignant carinal stenosis needs a self expanding metallic Y stent today; he is stable on a laryngeal mask with conscious sedation. The interventional team asks if the case must be canceled without fluoroscopy/guidewires. What is the best answer based on published experience?
 ### Options### 
(a) Fluoroscopy is mandatory; without it, Y stenting is contraindicated
(b) Only general anesthesia and rigid bronchoscopy are acceptable
(c) Guidewires are essential and the only safe approach
(d) Laryngeal mask with conscious sedation is standard when fluoroscopy is absent
(e) Experienced teams can place bifurcated self expanding metallic Y stents under direct bronchoscopic visualization alone
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Direct bronchoscopic implantation of integrated self expanding metallic Y stents without fluoroscopy/guidewires is feasible in experienced hands (choice E correct). Case series describe successful Y stent deployment using bronchoscopic visualization alone; while many centers rely on fluoroscopy and guidewires, they are not absolute requirements in all settings. Other reports describe placement under fluoroscopy via laryngeal mask and sedation, highlighting technique flexibility. Canceling solely for lack of fluoroscopy is not mandatory when expertise and appropriate devices are available. 
Pearl: For integrated Y stents, visualization only implantation is an option in skilled centers.
###References###
1.	Özdemir C, et al. Bifurcated metallic Y stents without fluoroscopy/guidewires. Multidiscip Respir Med. 2016;11:15. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27252612/

### Question  ###
A 62 year old man develops a symptomatic bronchopleural fistula (BPF) 2 weeks after right lower lobectomy for NSCLC. He has fever, air leak through the chest tube, and purulent sputum. He is not a candidate for immediate surgery due to sepsis and poor nutrition. Bronchoscopy localizes the fistula to the right lower lobe stump within 1 cm of the bronchus intermedius. Which bronchoscopic option has published support for sealing BPFs in this setting?
 ### Options### 
(a) Immediate external beam radiation to the stump
(b) Nd:YAG laser to “scar” the fistula edges
(c) Oral antibiotics and observation only
(d) Modified Dumon silicone stent configured to occlude the bronchial stump
(e) Esophageal stent replacement
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Customized/modified Dumon silicone stents configured to occlude the involved bronchus or stump can successfully seal postoperative BPFs (choice D correct). Case reports/series describe creating an occlusive configuration or branch on a silicone stent to block the stump and stop the air leak, with clinical improvement when surgery is not immediately possible. Radiation (A) does not close airway fistulas; thermal coagulation (B) risks enlarging the defect; observation alone (C) is unsafe; esophageal stents (E) treat esophageal—not bronchial—leaks. 
Pearl: Silicone can be shaped to the problem—consider customized occlusion for BPF.
###References###

### Question  ###
A 54 year old bilateral lung transplant recipient develops bilateral anastomotic stenoses close to the secondary carinas. Serial balloon dilations provide only short lived relief. The team has failed to maintain patency with straight stents. He is clinically stable but dyspneic on exertion. What strategy is most consistent with published experience?
 ### Options### 
(a) Avoid any stenting; repeat balloon dilations indefinitely
(b) Place a long uncovered metal stent spanning the carina to both anastomoses
(c) Use bifurcated silicone stents (custom Y or “Borgne” variants) tailored to the anastomotic sites
(d) Prolonged antibiotics and chest physiotherapy alone
(e) Endobronchial brachytherapy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Bifurcated silicone stents customized to the anatomy (e.g., Y configurations) are effective for post transplant anastomotic complications near secondary carinas (choice C correct). Ten year experiences and earlier series report favorable outcomes with bifurcated silicone stents in this setting; they are removable and accommodate evolving anatomy. Repeated dilation alone (A) often fails; uncovered metal stents (B) embed and complicate revision; supportive care (D) is inadequate; brachytherapy (E) has no role in benign anastomotic stenosis. 
Pearl: In transplant anastomotic disease, removable bifurcated silicone buys time and preserves options.

### Question  ###
During planning for a silicone Y stent across the main carina in a 61 year old man with left sided lung cancer, you anticipate the right upper lobe (RUL) orifice will be covered by the tracheal limb. The right upper lobe is well perfused on perfusion scan; loss of its ventilation would worsen dyspnea. What bronchoscopic strategy can preserve RUL ventilation?
 ### Options### 
(a) Accept occlusion because silicone Y stents cannot ventilate side branches
(b) On site customization—fenestrate the stent or add a branch (“Borgne Y”) to maintain RUL flow
(c) Use an uncovered metallic Y stent to allow air to pass through the mesh
(d) Place a second parallel straight stent beside the Y stent
(e) Refer for resection/anastomosis because only surgery can preserve RUL ventilation
###Discussion###
Bottom line: On site silicone stent customization (fenestration or adding a branch—Borgne Y) can preserve ventilation to covered lobar orifices (choice B correct). Practical techniques describe creating a side hole or adding a branch to a silicone stent to ventilate a lobar orifice that would otherwise be occluded by a Y stent; dedicated designs exist for right primary carina disease. Uncovered mesh (C) risks tumor/granulation ingrowth and does not reliably direct flow. A parallel straight stent (D) does not solve the covered ostium. Surgery (E) is not the only solution and is often unsuitable in malignant disease. 
Pearl: Silicone is modifiable—fenestrate or branch to ventilate a covered lobe.
###References###
1.	Breen DP, Dutau H. On site customization of silicone stents. Respiration. 2009;77:447 53. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19246823/
2.	Oki M, Saka H. Dedicated bifurcated silicone stent for right primary carina stenosis. Chest. 2013;144:450 5. PubMed: https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23392434/
### Question  ###
A 62 year old woman with COPD (FEV₁ 68% predicted) and a 40 pack year smoking history is found to have a 15 mm solid right upper lobe nodule on 1 mm thin slice CT. A “bronchus sign” is present leading into RB1. PET CT is avid without extrathoracic disease. She is not on anticoagulation and has no hemoptysis. You plan a bronchoscopic first approach and have fluoroscopy, radial probe EBUS (rEBUS), and virtual bronchoscopic navigation (VBN) available. Your goal is to maximize diagnostic yield for this ≤20 mm peripheral lesion while minimizing the need for a second procedure. Which initial bronchoscopic strategy is best?
 ### Options### 
(a) Thin bronchoscope (≈4.0 mm) with rEBUS and guide sheath under fluoroscopy
(b) Ultrathin bronchoscope (≈3.0–3.4 mm, 1.7 mm channel) with rEBUS + VBN under fluoroscopy
(c) Standard bronchoscope (≥4.6 mm) with fluoroscopy alone
(d) Ultrathin bronchoscope under fluoroscopy only (no rEBUS/VBN)
(e) CT guided transthoracic needle aspiration instead of bronchoscopy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Use the ultrathin bronchoscope (UTB) with rEBUS + VBN under fluoroscopy—choice (b) correct. Two randomized trials showed higher diagnostic yield for UTB compared with thin bronchoscopes when both arms used multimodality guidance; the advantage was greatest for lesions <20 mm and when a CT bronchus sign was present, exactly like this case. The 2015 RCT (median lesion ~19 mm) found UTB with rEBUS/VBN/fluoroscopy outperformed thin bronchoscopy with guide sheath; the 2019 RCT confirmed superiority across important subgroups. These data support starting with UTB when the path to the lesion is visible and advanced guidance is available, reducing repeat procedures and downstream costs. (a) Thin bronchoscope with rEBUS/guide sheath is reasonable but inferior to UTB in RCTs for small targets with a bronchus sign. (c) Fluoroscopy alone misses 3 D spatial relationships and underperforms image confirmed localization. (d) UTB without rEBUS/VBN forfeits the major yield gain of multimodality confirmation. (e) TTNA is not wrong in general, but the ### Question  ### asks for the initial bronchoscopic strategy; moreover, TTNA exposes the patient to a different complication profile and does not leverage the favorable bronchus sign. Pearl: For ≤20 mm nodules with a bronchus sign, UTB plus rEBUS (ideally with VBN) consistently shifts the odds toward a diagnosis in one session. 
###References###
1.	Oki M, et al.  with multimodal devices for peripheral pulmonary lesions: a randomized trial. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2015;192:468 476. PubMed
2.	Oki M, et al. Use of an ultrathin vs thin bronchoscope for peripheral pulmonary lesions: a randomized trial. Chest. 2019;156:954 964. PubMed
3.	Asano F, et al. Virtual bronchoscopic navigation combined with : a randomized clinical trial. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2013;188:327 333. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 68 year old man with metastatic squamous cell carcinoma presents with stridor and severe dyspnea from distal tracheal obstruction (~80% occlusion) on CT. He has 30 pack years, CAD with an ejection fraction of 45%, and apixaban held 48 h ago. You schedule urgent tumor debulking using thermal tools. In the OR you have rigid and flexible bronchoscopes, Nd:YAG or diode laser, APC, and stents. Anesthesia asks whether to proceed in the endoscopy suite under deep sedation with flexible bronchoscopy or in the operating room under general anesthesia with rigid bronchoscopy. Oxygen needs have been 4 L/min by nasal cannula; you anticipate needing to limit oxidizer concentration during laser activation. Which approach best aligns with lower complication risk and safer airway/fire control?
 ### Options### 
(a) Moderate sedation with flexible bronchoscopy in an endoscopy suite
(b) Spontaneous ventilation with high inspired oxygen to prevent desaturation
(c) General anesthesia with rigid bronchoscopy and an experienced team in the OR
(d) Deep sedation with flexible bronchoscopy to avoid intubation
(e) Avoid preoxygenation entirely to reduce fire risk
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Proceed with general anesthesia and rigid bronchoscopy in an experienced OR team—choice (c) correct. Large multicenter data in malignant central airway obstruction (MCAO) show higher complication risk when therapeutic bronchoscopy is performed under moderate sedation compared with general anesthesia, independent of other factors. Rigid bronchoscopy affords secure ventilation, suction, tamponade, and the ability to combine laser with mechanical coring and stenting in a controlled field, which is vital for high grade obstruction and bleeding risk. Oxygen should be minimized during laser activation to reduce airway fire risk, but preoxygenation and airway control remain standard safety practices; specialized laser resistant ETTs add protection when endotracheal intubation is used. (a) and (d) increase risk in complex MCAO by limiting ventilatory control, suction, and tool deployment. (b) High FiO₂ during lasing raises fire risk. (e) Skipping preoxygenation jeopardizes safety; the goal is limiting oxidizer concentration during laser activation, not abandoning preoxygenation. Pearl: In MCAO requiring thermal debulking, rigid bronchoscopy under general anesthesia consistently improves control of ventilation, bleeding, and fire risk compared with sedated flexible bronchoscopy. 
###References###
1.	Ost DE, et al. Complications following therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant central airway obstruction: results of the AQuIRE registry. Chest. 2015;148:450 471. PubMed
2.	Ost DE, et al. Therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant central airway obstruction: success rates and impact on dyspnea and quality of life. Chest. 2015;147:1282 1298. PubMed
3.	Roy S. Development of an improved laser resistant endotracheal tube. Laryngoscope. 2024;134(S7). Publisher

### Question  ###
A 59 year old man undergoes rigid bronchoscopy with Nd:YAG laser for a hypervascular left main bronchus tumor while on controlled positive pressure ventilation. FiO₂ is kept low during laser activation. Mid case he develops abrupt hypotension, ST segment changes, and loss of end tidal CO₂; a systemic air embolism is suspected and the case is aborted. For future procedures, which preventive measure most directly targets the mechanism believed to cause systemic air embolism during bronchoscopic Nd:YAG operations?
 ### Options### 
(a) Briefly increasing FiO₂ before each laser pulse to ensure oxygen reserve
(b) Firing the beam perpendicular to the airway wall to shorten exposure time
(c) Using contact mode lasing with high gas coolant flow to clear smoke rapidly
(d) Limiting gas coolant flow and avoiding direct tissue contact of gas cooled fibers
(e) Placing the patient in steep Trendelenburg for the entire case
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Reduce entrainment of gas into injured vessels by minimizing pressurized gas at the tissue interface—choice (d) correct. Experimental and clinical observations during bronchoscopic Nd:YAG procedures implicate high pressure gas streams (from coolant flow) and tissue disruption as promoters of intravascular gas entry; limiting coolant flow and avoiding direct tissue contact of gas cooled fibers were recommended preventive strategies. These interventions directly address the pathogenesis rather than downstream consequences. (a) Raising FiO₂ does not prevent embolism and can increase airway fire risk during lasing. (b) Perpendicular firing risks deeper transmural injury, which may increase vascular disruption. (c) High gas coolant flow augments the driving pressure for gas entrainment, counter to the preventive goal. (e) Trendelenburg positioning is occasionally used as part of management after embolism is suspected, not primary prevention. Pitfall: Confusing air embolism prevention with general hypoxemia prevention—airway oxidizer management is about fire risk, whereas embolism prevention is about pressurized gas/tissue interaction. 
###References###
1.	Tellides G, et al. Pathogenesis of systemic air embolism during bronchoscopic Nd:YAG laser operations. Ann Thorac Surg. 1998;65:930 934. PubMed
2.	Ost DE, et al. Complications following therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant central airway obstruction (AQuIRE). Chest. 2015;148:450 471. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 72 year old woman with a centrally obstructing endobronchial carcinoma presents with 300 mL hemoptysis and progressive dyspnea. She is hemodynamically stable, not anticoagulated, and has an SpO₂ of 92% on 2 L/min O₂. CT shows a right main bronchus endoluminal mass with no major vessel invasion; the airway is ~70% occluded. You have access to rigid bronchoscopy, Nd:YAG/diode and CO₂ lasers, cryotherapy, APC, and stents. Oxygen can be reduced during thermal activation. Your priority is immediate hemostasis and airway control with the option to debulk and stent if needed. What is the best immediate endoscopic plan?
 ### Options### 
(a) Outpatient flexible bronchoscopy with argon plasma coagulation under moderate sedation
(b) Photodynamic therapy
(c) Cryotherapy via flexible bronchoscopy
(d) CO₂ laser via flexible bronchoscopy under moderate sedation
(e) Rigid bronchoscopy under general anesthesia with Nd:YAG laser coagulation and large bore suction available
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Rigid bronchoscopy with Nd:YAG laser in the OR is preferred for brisk central bleeding—choice (e) correct. Large series of Nd:YAG bronchoscopy demonstrate rapid coagulation of vascular endobronchial tumors with high technical success restoring airway patency; rigid bronchoscopy enables secure airway control, effective suction, tamponade, and combined mechanical debulking or stenting in one session. In MCAO registries and guideline based care, symptom relief and improved ventilation frequently follow such multimodality therapy. (a) Sedated flexible bronchoscopy limits suction and tool deployment and was associated with higher complication rates than general anesthesia in AQuIRE. (b) Photodynamic therapy lacks immediate hemostatic effect and is inappropriate for active bleeding. (c) Cryotherapy is likewise delayed in effect and not first line for active hemorrhage. (d) CO₂ laser offers precise cutting but limited hemostasis compared with Nd:YAG; flexible, sedated conditions are suboptimal for heavy bleeding. Pearl: In active central airway hemorrhage, rigid bronchoscopy plus a deep penetrating coagulative laser (e.g., Nd:YAG or diode) provides the fastest route to hemostasis and airway control. 
###References###
1.	Cavaliere S, et al. Nd:YAG laser bronchoscopy: 1,396 applications. Chest. 1988;94:15 21. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 67 year old man with emphysema (DLCO 55%) has a 12 mm lateral basal segment (RB9) nodule on 0.75 mm CT with a clear bronchus sign. PET CT is avid. You plan to sample bronchoscopically rather than TTNA due to bleb predominant COPD. In the lab you have fluoroscopy, rEBUS, VBN, a thin bronchoscope (≈4.0 mm, 2.0 mm channel), and an ultrathin bronchoscope (UTB; ≈3.0 mm outer diameter with a 1.7 mm channel) compatible with rEBUS. Which equipment selection most improves access to subsegmental bronchi and real time “within lesion” localization for this small peripheral target?
 ### Options### 
(a) Ultrathin bronchoscope (≈3.0 mm, 1.7 mm channel) with rEBUS
(b) Standard bronchoscope (≥4.8 mm) with 2.0 mm channel and forceps
(c) Thin bronchoscope (≈4.0 mm) with guide sheath only
(d) CT guided transthoracic needle aspiration
(e) Standard bronchoscope with fluoroscopy alone
###Discussion###
Bottom line: The UTB with rEBUS best combines distal reach and image confirmation—choice (a) correct. UTBs (≈3.0 mm) can routinely reach fifth generation bronchi, beyond the limits of 4.0 mm thin scopes, and the 1.7 mm channel accommodates rEBUS for in lesion confirmation. Randomized trials and comparative studies show higher yields for UTB—especially for ≤20 mm lesions—when paired with rEBUS (±VBN/fluoroscopy) versus larger scopes. The ability to navigate the bronchus sign into subsegmental branches and prove “within lesion” position is decisive for a 12 mm RB9 nodule. (b) A standard scope lacks the reach; (e) fluoroscopy alone cannot confirm 3 D contact. (c) A guide sheath without the distal reach of UTB may still stall proximal to RB9. (d) TTNA is a separate pathway with a different risk profile; the ### Question  ### asks which bronchoscopic selection best enhances access and localization. Pearl: If rEBUS cannot get into small targets with a thin scope, switching to a UTB often turns an “adjacent to” view into a “within lesion” view and raises yield.

### Question  ###
A 55 year old woman with dyspnea has (1) a thin, circumferential, web like subglottic stenosis after prior intubation and (2) a separate bulky, friable, vascular endobronchial tumor in the bronchus intermedius. You plan endoscopic therapy in staged sessions and can use CO₂, Nd:YAG, or KTP lasers along with rigid dilation and forceps. You will limit oxidizer concentration during lasing. Which pairing of laser to tissue property is most appropriate for these two very different problems?
 ### Options### 
(a) CO₂ laser for deep coagulation of bulky tumor; Nd:YAG for precise cutting of thin web
(b) Nd:YAG for rapid vaporization of superficial web; KTP for deep coagulation of bulky tumor
(c) CO₂ for precise cutting with shallow penetration (web); Nd:YAG for deep coagulation/vaporization of bulky tumor
(d) Argon laser for thick tumor; holmium:YAG for deep hemostasis
(e) Excimer laser for major tumor hemostasis
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Match CO₂ to precise, shallow cutting and Nd:YAG to deep coagulation—choice (c) correct. Early comparative clinical experience shows CO₂ provides high absorption with minimal scatter, enabling precise incisions through thin webs with limited thermal spread—ideal for mucosal sparing radial cuts before gentle dilation. Nd:YAG (1064 nm) penetrates more deeply with strong coagulative effect (and vaporization at higher power density), better suited to debulk and devascularize bulky, vascular tumors before mechanical coring. (a) Inverts each laser’s principal strength. (b) Nd:YAG is not favored for superficial web cutting due to deeper thermal effects; KTP is shallower but not the preferred coagulator for grossly vascular intrathoracic tumors. (d) The options misattribute effects and are not supported as first line combinations for these use cases. (e) Excimer lasers have poor coagulative capability and are not appropriate for brisk tumor hemostasis. Pearl: Use CO₂ to “release” a thin web; use Nd:YAG (or a comparable coagulative diode) to devascularize bulky tumor before mechanical debulking. 
###References###
1.	Shapshay SM, et al. Endoscopic treatment of tracheobronchial malignancy: experience with Nd:YAG and CO₂ lasers. Otolaryngol Head Neck Surg. 1985;93:205 210. PubMed
2.	Ramser ER, Beamis JF. Laser bronchoscopy. Clin Chest Med. 1995;16:415 426. PubMed
3.	Cavaliere S, et al. Chest. 1988;94:15 21. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 58 year old woman with a 22 mm left lower lobe peripheral adenocarcinoma (solid component) needs comprehensive next generation sequencing (DNA and RNA). Thin slice CT shows a bronchus sign. During bronchoscopy you can use UTB (1.7 mm channel), rEBUS, fluoroscopy, and a 1.1 mm cryoprobe. You aim to maximize tissue adequacy while maintaining safety. What is the best sampling plan?
 ### Options### 
(a) 1.5 mm forceps only with a few passes to avoid bleeding
(b) Forceps plus rEBUS guided UTB cryobiopsy using a 1.1 mm cryoprobe
(c) Forceps plus pTBNA if rEBUS shows an “adjacent to” view
(d) Brush cytology only to avoid complications
(e) BAL from the segmental airway for cell block NGS
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Combine forceps with rEBUS guided UTB cryobiopsy—choice (b) correct. Prospective and observational data using a 1.1 mm cryoprobe through UTB show larger, less crushed tissue and higher overall diagnostic yield than forceps alone, with acceptable safety in ≤30 mm peripheral lesions. Importantly for this case, multiple series report successful comprehensive NGS from 1.1 mm cryobiopsy specimens, addressing the common limitation of small forceps biopsies obtained through 1.7 mm channels. Forceps passes still add value (architecture, hemostasis between cryo bites), so a combined approach is advantageous. (a) Forceps alone risks inadequate material for broad NGS panels. (c) Adding pTBNA can help “adjacent to” views but may still limit tissue volume and fragmentation for multiplex testing. (d) Brushings alone underperform for histology and molecular yield. (e) BAL cell blocks are often insufficient for full NGS panels in solid peripheral lesions. Pearl: When comprehensive genomics matters, 1.1 mm UTB cryobiopsy plus forceps is a practical way to upsize tissue without abandoning a bronchoscopic approach.

### Question  ###
A 41 year old man, previously intubated for trauma, now has exertional dyspnea and biphasic stridor. CT and bronchoscopy reveal a short, thin, circumferential subglottic/tracheal web (~8 mm length) without cartilage loss or malacia. He is not on anticoagulation. You plan endoscopic therapy under general anesthesia with rigid dilation available, and you can use Nd:YAG, KTP, and CO₂ lasers. Your goal is durable airway patency while preserving mucosa and minimizing restenosis. Which endoscopic technique is best?
 ### Options### 
(a) Circumferential CO₂ laser ablation of the stenotic ring
(b) Balloon dilation alone
(c) Immediate silicone stent as first line therapy
(d) Mucosal sparing radial laser incisions followed by gentle dilation
(e) Cryotherapy alone to shrink the fibrotic ring
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Perform mucosal sparing radial laser incisions with gentle dilation—choice (d) correct. In simple, web like post intubation stenosis, series describing Nd:YAG (and other lasers) used for radial incisions followed by controlled dilation report good long term patency and symptom relief, with many patients requiring only a few sessions. The key is releasing the fibrotic ring while preserving as much normal mucosa as possible to limit scarring. (a) Circumferential ablation sacrifices mucosa, increasing thermal injury and restenosis. (b) Dilation alone often provides transient relief with higher recurrence. (c) First line stenting is generally reserved for complex stenoses (length >1 cm, cartilage involvement); stents add migration, granulation, and infection risks. (e) Cryotherapy lacks the immediate mechanical release needed for a tight web. Pearl: For short, web like benign stenoses, cut radially (1–3 incisions) and then dilate—avoid circumferential mucosal loss. 
###References###
1.	Mehta AC, et al. Concentric tracheal and subglottic stenosis: mucosal sparing Nd:YAG followed by gentle dilatation. Chest. 1993;104:673 677. PubMed
2.	Galluccio G, et al. Interventional endoscopy in benign tracheal stenoses: long term results. Eur J Cardiothorac Surg. 2009;35:429 433. PubMed
3.	Baugnée PE, et al. Endoscopic treatment of post intubation tracheal stenosis (58 cases). Rev Mal Respir. 1995;12:585 592. PubMed

### Question  ###
During thin scope bronchoscopy for a 17 mm lateral segment (RB4) nodule with a bronchus sign, you repeatedly obtain only an “adjacent to” rEBUS image and cannot advance the probe into the lesion despite guide sheath manipulation under fluoroscopy. PET CT is avid; the patient prefers to avoid TTNA if possible due to prior pneumothorax after pleural procedures. You still have time and equipment available in the same session: an ultrathin bronchoscope (UTB; 3.0 mm outer diameter, 1.7 mm channel) and rEBUS. What is the best next step to improve bronchoscopic yield today?
 ### Options### 
(a) Switch to larger forceps on the thin bronchoscope
(b) Keep the thin scope and add a guide sheath only
(c) Abort and refer for CT guided transthoracic needle biopsy
(d) Obtain PET CT before further sampling
(e) Switch to the UTB, advance more distally, and attempt rEBUS UT localization
###Discussion###
Bottom line: When thin scope rEBUS can’t enter the lesion, switching to UTB in the same session often converts “adjacent to” to “within lesion”—choice (e) correct. Prospective and retrospective series show that adding UTB on a need basis after a failed thin scope attempt allowed rEBUS to be advanced into the lesion in ~55–60% of cases and significantly increased diagnostic yield compared with staying with the thin scope. The UTB’s smaller diameter improves angulation and distal access along the bronchus sign, enabling in lesion confirmation before sampling. (a) Larger forceps don’t fix the reach problem. (b) A guide sheath without better distal access still stalls proximal to the lesion. (c) TTNA is reasonable overall but prematurely abandons a viable bronchoscopic salvage step that may avoid pneumothorax prone transthoracic sampling. (d) PET CT has already been done and does not improve intraprocedural localization. Pearl: In a single session, “thin to ultrathin” escalation is a practical rescue that materially raises the odds of diagnosis without changing modality. 
###References###
1.	Nishii Y, et al. Use of ultrathin bronchoscope on a need basis improves diagnostic yield of difficult lesions. Front Med (Lausanne). 2020;7:588048. PubMed
2.	Oki M, et al. Value of adding  to thin bronchoscopy: a multicentre prospective study. Respirology. 2023;28:152 158. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 70 year old man with severe dyspnea from malignant central airway obstruction (MCAO) due to squamous cell carcinoma undergoes therapeutic bronchoscopy with multimodality debulking and stenting under general anesthesia. He asks what benefits to expect and how risky the procedure is. You review his ASA class (III), note no active infection, and plan to limit oxidizer concentration during any laser activation. Which statement best reflects contemporary outcomes data he should hear before consent?
 ### Options### 
(a) Technical success is high and many patients experience improved dyspnea and health related quality of life, though symptomatic benefit is not universal
(b) Complications occur in >20% of cases at experienced centers
(c) Moderate sedation is safer than general anesthesia for these procedures
(d) Laser monotherapy usually obviates the need for oncologic adjuvant therapies
(e) Rigid bronchoscopy is associated with worse outcomes than flexible bronchoscopy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Emphasize high technical success with meaningful—though not guaranteed—symptomatic gains and a low overall complication rate—choice (a) correct. In the AQuIRE registry and other multicenter studies of therapeutic bronchoscopy for MCAO, technical success was high, and a substantial portion of patients had improved dyspnea and HRQOL after treatment. Complication rates were in the single digits overall, varied by center, and were higher with moderate sedation than with general anesthesia after adjustment—supporting robust airway control for complex cases. Long term work demonstrates improvements in quality adjusted survival when airway patency is restored, situating endoscopic therapy as a key palliative or bridging component—not a substitute for oncologic care. (b) Overstates risk; published rates at experienced centers are far lower. (c) Contradicted by AQuIRE, which found higher complications with moderate sedation. (d) Most patients still need radiation, systemic therapy, or surgery when feasible. (e) Rigid bronchoscopy is a cornerstone of successful outcomes in complex MCAO rather than a detriment. Pearl: Set expectations: airway reopening is usually feasible and often relieves dyspnea, but benefits vary and endoscopic therapy complements—not replaces—oncologic treatment. 
###References###
1.	Ost DE, et al. Therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant central airway obstruction: success rates and impact on dyspnea and QOL. Chest. 2015;147:1282 1298. PubMed
2.	Ost DE, et al. Complications following therapeutic bronchoscopy (AQuIRE). Chest. 2015;148:450 471. PubMed
3.	Ong P, et al. Long term quality adjusted survival after therapeutic bronchoscopy for MCAO. Thorax. 2019;74:141 156. PubMed
### Question  ###
A 64 year old man with previously irradiated esophageal squamous cell carcinoma is intubated in the ICU for progressive dyspnea (volume control ventilation, FiO₂ 0.80, PEEP 8 cm H₂O). Thin section CT (1.0 mm) shows a 2.8 cm exophytic mass occupying ~85% of the proximal tracheal lumen with additional extrinsic impingement; the distal trachea and carina are patent without clear vascular invasion. OR and rigid equipment are immediately available. Which immediate bronchoscopic plan best restores and maintains airway patency while minimizing risk in this setting?
 ### Options### 
(a) Flexible argon plasma coagulation under moderate sedation to shrink the tumor
(B) Nd:YAG laser photoresection under rigid bronchoscopy at current FiO₂
(C) High dose rate endobronchial brachytherapy as single session palliation
(D) Rigid bronchoscopy with mechanical coring plus microdebrider debulking, proceeding to airway stenting if significant extrinsic compression persists
(E) Photodynamic therapy now with debridement planned in 48–72 hours
Correct answer: D
Explanation
Choice D is correct. In life threatening mixed CAO with high FiO₂, immediate non thermal debulking is safest and most effective. Rigid bronchoscopy allows large bore suction and mechanical coring; adding a microdebrider rapidly removes exophytic tumor while avoiding airway fire and permitting continued oxygenation. Prospective/retrospective series demonstrate technical success and low complication rates for microdebrider debulking in central airways. If significant residual extrinsic compression remains after intraluminal clearance, stenting during the same session maintains luminal caliber against external forces and improves durability. 
Casal and colleagues and Lunn et al. support microdebrider safety/effectiveness, and Saad et al. report long term outcomes with airway stents placed bronchoscopically. Nd:YAG laser requires FiO₂ ≤ 0.40 to mitigate fire risk and is not appropriate at FiO₂ 0.80; APC is likewise thermal and inadequate for bulky tumor. PDT and endobronchial brachytherapy act with delay and are inappropriate for emergent stabilization. Additionally, registry data associate complex therapeutic bronchoscopy under moderate sedation with higher complication rates than general anesthesia, reinforcing the need for a controlled OR approach. Pearl: In ventilated, high FiO₂ patients with mixed obstruction, pair rigid non thermal debulking with stenting when an extrinsic component persists. 

###References###
Casal RF, Iribarren J, Eapen G, et al. Safety and effectiveness of microdebrider bronchoscopy for the management of central airway obstruction. Respirology. 2013;18:1011 1015. 
Lunn W, Garland R, Ashiku S, et al. Microdebrider bronchoscopy: a new tool for the interventional bronchoscopist. Ann Thorac Surg. 2005;80:1485 1488. 
Saad CP, Murthy S, Krizmanich G, Mehta AC. Self expandable metallic airway stents and flexible bronchoscopy: long term outcomes analysis. Chest. 2003;124:1993 1999. 
Khemasuwan D, Mehta AC, Wang KP. Past, present, and future of endobronchial laser photoresection. J Thorac Dis. 2015;7:S380 S388. 
Ost DE, Ernst A, Grosu HB, et al. Complications following therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant central airway obstruction: AQuIRE registry. Chest. 2015;148:450 457.

### Question  ###
A 71 year old woman with unresectable NSCLC has complete occlusion of the left main bronchus on bronchoscopy. Thin section CT (1.0 mm) suggests a short, intraluminal mass, but distal patency is unclear due to collapse. She is stable on 3 L/min oxygen (SpO₂ 93%). You plan to place an airway stent if a usable distal lumen exists. During this same procedure, which maneuver best informs stent sizing and feasibility while providing immediate safety?
 ### Options### 
(a) Deploy a self expanding metallic stent sized to radiographic length without further assessment
(B) Perform short, controlled balloon dilation across the occlusion to transiently reveal the distal airway and gauge effective length/diameter
(C) Administer porfimer sodium for photodynamic therapy and schedule debridement in 48–72 hours
(D) Apply Nd:YAG laser first to “char” the lesion and then measure after sloughing
(E) Place a brachytherapy catheter to open the airway over weeks
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Choice B is correct. Endoscopic balloon dilation provides immediate lumen gain and can be used diagnostically to uncover the distal airway, confirm ventilability, and calibrate stent length/diameter before committing to prosthesis placement—particularly when CT cannot define distal patency. Hautmann et al. reported rapid stabilization and symptom relief with bronchoscopic balloon dilation in malignant CAO, and contemporary reviews emphasize its role both therapeutically and in planning downstream stenting. Pearl: A brief dilation to “look beyond” a complete occlusion can prevent mis sizing or unnecessary stenting. PDT and brachytherapy are delayed effect modalities and cannot answer the intraprocedural ### Question  ###; “blind” stent deployment risks poor expansion, migration, or covering an unusable distal airway; laser first is unnecessary and potentially hazardous when the distal target is unknown. The chapter’s algorithm and summary table depict dilation and stenting workflows for malignant CAO. 

###References###
Hautmann H, Gamarra F, Pfeifer KJ, Huber RM. Fiberoptic bronchoscopic balloon dilatation in malignant tracheobronchial disease: indications and results. Chest. 2001;120:43 49. 
Powers RE, Schwalk AJ. Overview of malignant central airway obstruction. Mediastinum. 2023;7:32. Publisher

### Question  ###
A 58 year old man with inoperable squamous cell lung cancer has had three successful debulking procedures over 9 months (laser once, microdebrider twice). He completed external beam radiotherapy 6 months ago. He re presents with recurrent endobronchial disease causing ~50% narrowing at the right main bronchus, mild dyspnea, and no hemoptysis; oxygenation is stable. He asks for a strategy that might reduce the frequency of repeat bronchoscopies rather than immediate relief today. Which management is most appropriate now?
 ### Options### 
(a) Repeat laser photoresection as sole therapy during this visit
(B) Broad field argon plasma coagulation as sole therapy
(C) Freeze–thaw cryotherapy as sole therapy
(D) Observation with return if symptoms worsen
(E) Plan high dose rate endobronchial brachytherapy as an adjunct for recurrent endobronchial disease
Correct answer: E
Explanation
Choice E is correct. Endobronchial brachytherapy (EBBT) delivers a localized, high dose radiation via an afterloading catheter and has delayed effects over weeks. Evidence based guidance from ASTRO and the American Brachytherapy Society does not support routine EBBT as sole palliation of airway obstruction; however, in selected, non emergent cases with recurrent endobronchial disease after prior modalities, EBBT may be offered as an adjunct to prolong local control and reduce the need for repeated bronchoscopic debulking, provided life expectancy is sufficient. Laser/APC/cryo as stand alone repeat debulking would offer immediate but typically short lived benefit in this relapse pattern; observation ignores ongoing local progression. Pitfall: Using EBBT for acute obstruction is inappropriate—it does not provide immediate airway opening. Chapter figures summarize the delayed effect role of EBBT relative to immediate debulking tools. 

###References###
Rodrigues G, Videtic G, Sur R, et al. ASTRO evidence based guideline on palliative thoracic radiotherapy in lung cancer. Pract Radiat Oncol. 2011;1:60 71. 
Stewart A, Parashar B, Patel M, et al. American Brachytherapy Society consensus guidelines for thoracic brachytherapy for lung cancer. Brachytherapy. 2016;15:1 11.

### Question  ###
A 62 year old woman with a 2.5 cm exophytic mid tracheal tumor and visible distal lumen on thin section CT (1.0 mm) is planned for therapeutic bronchoscopy. She is hemodynamically stable on FiO₂ 0.35. There is no radiographic vascular invasion. Which immediate bronchoscopic approach best achieves effective debulking and hemostasis today?
 ### Options### 
(a) Rigid bronchoscopy with Nd:YAG laser photoresection after maintaining FiO₂ ≤ 0.40
(B) Broad field APC alone via flexible bronchoscopy
(C) Microdebrider debulking alone via rigid bronchoscopy
(D) Endobronchial brachytherapy in a single fraction
(E) Photodynamic therapy with planned debridement in 48–72 hours
Correct answer: A
Explanation
Choice A is correct. For short, intraluminal, exophytic lesions with a visible distal lumen, Nd:YAG laser photoresection under rigid bronchoscopy provides rapid debulking plus coagulation for hemostasis and is most effective when FiO₂ is kept ≤ 0.40 to reduce airway fire risk. Foundational series and contemporary reviews detail high immediate success in this phenotype. Microdebrider debulking (C) is a strong alternative but is generally favored when FiO₂ cannot be reduced or when the lesion’s vascularity limits safe thermal use. APC (B) offers superficial coagulation/hemostasis but is less efficient for bulk tumor removal. EBBT (D) and PDT (E) are delayed effect therapies and are inappropriate for immediate recanalization. Pearl: Laser works best for central, exophytic tumors <~4 cm with a visible distal lumen and oxygen titrated below 40%. The chapter’s tables/figures align these selection criteria for laser versus other modalities. 

###References###
Dumon JF, Reboud E, Garbe L, et al. Treatment of tracheobronchial lesions by laser photoresection. Chest. 1982;81:278 284. 
Cavaliere S, Foccoli P, Farina PL. Nd:YAG laser bronchoscopy: five year experience with 1,396 applications. Chest. 1988;94:15 21. 
Khemasuwan D, Mehta AC, Wang KP. Endobronchial laser photoresection. J Thorac Dis. 2015;7:S380 S388.

### Question  ###
A 69 year old man with CKD (eGFR 28 mL/min/1.73 m²), ischemic cardiomyopathy, and COPD (ASA class IV) has a 70% endoluminal left main bronchus obstruction. He is scheduled for therapeutic bronchoscopy today. Which procedural setting/anesthetic strategy is most likely to minimize complications for this high risk, therapeutic CAO intervention?
 ### Options### 
(a) Flexible bronchoscopy with moderate sedation in the endoscopy suite
(B) Flexible bronchoscopy with high flow nasal cannula but no anesthesiologist
(C) General anesthesia with rigid bronchoscopy in the operating room
(D) Flexible bronchoscopy with paralytics administered by the bronchoscopist
(E) Topical anesthesia alone at the bedside
Correct answer: C
Explanation
Choice C is correct. In the multicenter AQuIRE registry, moderate sedation and urgent/emergent procedures were independently associated with higher complication rates during therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant CAO. Complex debulking is safer with general anesthesia in a controlled OR and—with appropriate expertise—often rigid bronchoscopy to secure the airway, accommodate large bore suction, and enable multimodality therapy. Performing high risk interventions without an anesthesiologist or with only topical anesthesia is inappropriate; paralytics without full anesthetic support and definitive airway control are unsafe. Pearl: For complex therapeutic CAO, avoid moderate sedation—use GA in the OR with rigid capability to reduce complications. The chapter emphasizes this risk signal and depicts workflows accordingly. 

###References###
Ost DE, Ernst A, Grosu HB, et al. Complications following therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant central airway obstruction: AQuIRE registry. Chest. 2015;148:450 457.

### Question  ###
A 55 year old woman on high flow nasal cannula (FiO₂ 0.70) has a near occlusive, cherry red, highly vascular polypoid tumor in the proximal left main bronchus; CT shows no extrinsic compression and a patent distal airway. You have rigid equipment and cryo tools. Which immediate bronchoscopic approach is most appropriate?
 ### Options### 
(a) Cryoextraction (cryo recanalization) via rigid bronchoscopy
(B) Nd:YAG laser photoresection at current FiO₂
(C) APC coagulation of the tumor surface
(D) High dose rate endobronchial brachytherapy today
(E) Photodynamic therapy with debridement in 48–72 hours
Correct answer: A
Explanation
Choice A is correct. Cryoextraction removes tumor immediately by freezing tissue to the probe and extracting it en bloc/piecemeal. It is non thermal and avoids airway fire risk when FiO₂ is high, making it ideal for vascular carcinoid type lesions in urgent settings. Hetzel et al. first described rapid recanalization with cryoextraction; subsequent series outline technical refinements and key success factors. Laser (B) and APC (C) are thermal and unsafe at FiO₂ 0.70. Brachytherapy (D) and PDT (E) have delayed effects and are unsuitable for emergent airway relief. Pearl: In hypoxemic patients who cannot tolerate FiO₂ reduction, non thermal tools (cryoextraction or microdebrider) are preferred for immediate recanalization. Chapter summaries list cryotherapy’s roles and cautions, including its utility in highly vascular tumors. 

###References###
Hetzel M, Hetzel J, Schumann C, et al. Cryorecanalization: immediate management of acute airway obstruction. J Thorac Cardiovasc Surg. 2004;127:1427 1431. 
Yılmaz A, Aktaş Z, Alici IO, et al. Cryorecanalization: keys to success. Surg Endosc. 2012;26:2969 2974. 
Maiwand MO, Homasson JP. Cryotherapy for tracheobronchial disorders. Clin Chest Med. 1995;16:427 443.

### Question  ###
A 65 year old man with a friable right main bronchus tumor undergoes rigid mechanical coring and suction debulking. After clearing the lumen, there is diffuse tumor surface oozing without a discrete arterial bleeder. FiO₂ has been reduced to 0.35 and he remains stable. Which immediate bronchoscopic step provides the best hemostasis?
 ### Options### 
(a) Freeze–thaw cryotherapy across the oozing mucosa
(B) Further balloon dilation of the treated segment
(C) Photodynamic therapy with debridement in 48–72 hours
(D) Argon plasma coagulation over the bleeding tumor surface
(E) High dose rate endobronchial brachytherapy
Correct answer: D
Explanation
Choice D is correct. Argon plasma coagulation (APC) delivers non contact monopolar current via ionized argon to achieve controlled surface coagulation over a broad area—well suited for diffuse oozing after debulking. Early series showed reliable hemostasis and symptomatic improvement with APC for malignant airway lesions. Freeze–thaw cryotherapy (A) has delayed tissue necrosis and is slower for acute hemostasis; balloon dilation (B) does not address bleeding; PDT (C) and brachytherapy (E) act over days to weeks and are not hemostatic for immediate control. Pearl: When FiO₂ is ≤ 0.40 and bleeding is superficial/diffuse, APC offers efficient, non contact hemostasis after debulking. The chapter’s modality tables situate APC’s hemostatic niche relative to other tools. 

###References###
Morice RC, Ece T, Ece F, Keus L. Endobronchial argon plasma coagulation for hemoptysis and neoplastic airway obstruction. Chest. 2001;119:781 787. 
Crosta C, Spaggiari L, De Stefano A, et al. Endoscopic argon plasma coagulation for palliative treatment of malignant airway obstructions. Lung Cancer. 2001;33:75 80.
