### Question  ###
A 59 year old man declines surgery for a 1.2 cm superficial endobronchial lesion at a segmental orifice (third order bronchus). He is stable, wants a non emergent bronchoscopic option that can treat more distal mucosa. CT shows no vascular invasion; lung is perfused. Which strategy best fits his goals?
 ### Options### 
(a) Nd:YAG laser photoresection today in the OR
(B) APC alone during flexible bronchoscopy
(C) Endobronchial brachytherapy as sole therapy
(D) Microdebrider debulking alone
(E) Photodynamic therapy (porfimer sodium) with planned debridement at 48–72 hours
Correct answer: E
Explanation
Choice E is correct. Photodynamic therapy (PDT)—systemic photosensitizer followed by bronchoscopic light activation—produces delayed tumor necrosis and can reach more distal airways with penetration on the order of millimeters. Series in unresectable endobronchial disease report palliation and local control when followed by scheduled debridement at 48–72 hours to clear sloughed tissue. Laser (A) is excellent for central exophytic tumors but is less suited for distal, flat mucosal disease and is not necessary emergently here. APC (B) provides superficial coagulation and hemostasis but lacks photosensitizer mediated cytotoxicity over depth. Brachytherapy (C) is not recommended as routine monotherapy for palliation, and microdebrider (D) targets bulk tumor rather than distal mucosal spread. Pitfall: After PDT, plan early bronchoscopy to evacuate debris and mitigate edema related obstruction. The chapter’s key points outline PDT’s depth, timing, and adverse effects. 

###References###
Moghissi K, Dixon K, Stringer M, et al. Bronchoscopic photodynamic therapy in advanced unresectable lung cancer: experience of 100 cases. Eur J Cardiothorac Surg. 1999;15:1 6. 
McCaughan JS Jr, Hawley PC, Brown DG, et al. Effect of light dose on photodynamic destruction of endobronchial tumors. Ann Thorac Surg. 1992;54:705 711. 
Stewart A, Parashar B, Patel M, et al. American Brachytherapy Society consensus guidelines. Brachytherapy. 2016;15:1 11.

### Question  ###
A 66 year old man has complete left upper lobe atelectasis from an obstructing endobronchial tumor at the left main origin. CT angiography shows near occlusion of the corresponding left pulmonary artery branch from tumor encasement. He requests bronchoscopy to “re open the airway.” Which pre procedural finding most predicts limited symptomatic benefit from bronchoscopic recanalization despite technical success?
 ### Options### 
(a) Short segment, purely intraluminal obstruction
(B) Absent/critically reduced perfusion to the target lung region on imaging
(C) Patent distal bronchi beyond the lesion
(D) ASA class II status and good performance status
(E) Grossly visible distal lumen at bronchoscopy
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Choice B is correct. Functional benefit after airway recanalization depends not only on luminal patency but also on regional perfusion. When the pulmonary arterial supply to the affected region is occluded, restoring airflow adds dead space with little symptomatic gain. Physiologic studies demonstrate changes in ventilation–perfusion after endobronchial laser treatment, and outcomes data consistently link success to factors such as pure endoluminal disease and patent distal airways, while other features (e.g., renal failure, high ASA class, left main disease) predict lower success—none of which supersede the central issue of absent perfusion in this vignette. Pearl: Assess perfusion when deciding whether recanalization will translate into clinical benefit. Chapter text and figures emphasize integrating imaging (including vascular structures) into selection for bronchoscopic therapy. 

###References###
George PJ, Clarke G, Tolfree S, et al. Changes in regional ventilation and perfusion of the lung after endoscopic laser treatment. Thorax. 1990;45:248 253. 
Ost DE, Ernst A, Grosu HB, et al. Therapeutic bronchoscopy for malignant CAO: success rates and impact on dyspnea and quality of life. Chest. 2015;147:1282 1298. 
Verma A, Goh SK, Tai DYH, et al. Outcome differences in recanalized malignant CAO: endoluminal vs extrinsic. Lasers Med Sci. 2019;34:955 962.

### Question  ###
A 63 year old woman with papillary thyroid carcinoma presents with progressive dyspnea. CT (1.0 mm) shows a 2.5 cm pure extrinsic tracheal compression with intact mucosa; bronchoscopy confirms minimal endoluminal tissue. She is stable on 2 L/min oxygen. Which immediate bronchoscopic strategy best addresses the obstruction today?
 ### Options### 
(a) APC across the compressed mucosa
(B) Nd:YAG laser photoresection of the compressed segment
(C) Balloon dilation followed by airway stent placement to scaffold the segment
(D) Freeze–thaw cryotherapy to the compressed wall
(E) Photodynamic therapy with delayed debridement
Correct answer: C
Explanation
Choice C is correct. For extrinsic compression, immediate lumen gain with balloon dilation followed by airway stenting provides the most reliable relief by countering external pressure; dilation alone is transient. Series support rapid symptomatic improvement with balloon dilation and durable patency when a stent is placed, especially when the mucosa is intact and obstruction length is short. Thermal debulking tools (APC, laser) target intraluminal tissue and offer no scaffolding function; cryotherapy likewise targets tissue rather than external forces; PDT is delayed effect and unsuitable for immediate relief. Pearl: After dilation for extrinsic disease, don’t stop—place a stent to prevent recoil and early re obstruction. The chapter’s algorithm and summary tables show this sequence for extrinsic CAO. 

###References###

### Question  ###
A 60 year old man with central squamous cell carcinoma and a permanent pacemaker presents with mild dyspnea and hemoptysis. CT shows a 2 cm endoluminal lesion in the right main bronchus; FiO₂ is titratable to 0.35. You plan immediate bronchoscopic hemostasis and debulking. Which approach best balances efficacy and device safety today?
 ### Options### 
(a) Nd:YAG laser photoresection under rigid bronchoscopy with FiO₂ ≤ 0.40
(B) APC via flexible bronchoscopy at current FiO₂
(C) Microdebrider debulking alone via rigid bronchoscopy
(D) Endobronchial brachytherapy as sole therapy
(E) Photodynamic therapy with debridement at 48–72 hours
Correct answer: A
Explanation
Choice A is correct. With FiO₂ titratable ≤ 0.40, Nd:YAG laser photoresection provides immediate debulking and effective hemostasis for a short, exophytic endoluminal tumor in a main bronchus. Contemporary reviews describe laser as effective and note practical advantages, including lack of interference with cardiac implanted devices—relevant in this patient with a pacemaker. APC (B) is superficial and thermal, less efficient for bulk removal; microdebrider (C) can debulk but offers less coagulation control for bleeding; brachytherapy (D) and PDT (E) are delayed effect modalities and not appropriate for immediate hemostasis. Pearl: When FiO₂ can be reduced safely and the lesion is exophytic with a visible distal lumen, laser offers the best blend of rapid debulking and hemostasis—even in patients with pacemakers. The chapter’s laser selection criteria (lesion length, visibility, FiO₂ threshold) are summarized in its figures. 

###References###
Khemasuwan D, Mehta AC, Wang KP. Endobronchial laser photoresection. J Thorac Dis. 2015;7:S380 S388. 
Cavaliere S, Foccoli P, Farina PL. Nd:YAG laser bronchoscopy: five year experience. Chest. 1988;94:15 21. 
BLVR
### Question  ###
A 67-year-old man with severe COPD (60-pack-year ex-smoker) has dyspnea despite triple inhaler therapy and completed pulmonary rehabilitation. Resting vitals: BP 128/76 mm Hg, HR 88/min, SpO₂ 93% on room air. Tests: FEV₁ 32% predicted, RV 178% predicted, DLCO 24% predicted. Transthoracic echo estimates RVSP ~55 mm Hg; thin-section CT (1.0 mm) shows heterogeneous upper-lobe–predominant emphysema without bronchiectasis. He is scheduled for endobronchial valve (EBV) evaluation under general anesthesia (volume-controlled ventilation, FiO₂ 0.4). What is the single best next step before proceeding?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Proceed directly with EBV placement in the most destroyed lobe
•	(B) Obtain right-heart catheterization to confirm and stage pulmonary hypertension
•	(C) Select lower-lobe coil therapy because DLCO is low
•	(D) Defer all interventions and repeat spirometry after 6 months
•	(E) Perform thermal vapor ablation instead of EBV because emphysema is heterogeneous
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: In candidates with suspected pulmonary hypertension on echocardiography, confirm hemodynamics before bronchoscopic lung volume reduction—making choice B correct. An expert panel recommends that significant pulmonary hypertension is a relative/functional contraindication to BLVR and that abnormal echo findings (e.g., RVSP ≥~50 mm Hg) warrant invasive confirmation and risk stratification prior to EBV or other BLVR therapies. Randomized EBV trials demonstrating benefit (LIBERATE, TRANSFORM) excluded significant pulmonary hypertension, so confirming hemodynamics aligns the patient with trial-like selection and safety【Criner 2018; Kemp 2017】. The chapter’s tables of outcomes and complications also emphasize selection aligned with trial criteria. 

Why others are incorrect: (A) Proceeding without clarifying pulmonary pressures ignores selection guidance and may increase risk. (C) Coils are not chosen primarily because of DLCO; they also carry pneumonia/pneumothorax risks and selection is not driven by low DLCO alone【Sciurba 2016; Deslée 2016】. (D) Watchful waiting offers no diagnostic clarification and delays effective therapy. (E) Thermal vapor ablation evidence applies mainly to upper-lobe heterogeneous disease but does not bypass the need to evaluate suspected pulmonary hypertension【Herth 2016】.
Pearl: Echo-suggested pulmonary hypertension should be confirmed by right-heart catheterization before BLVR consideration.
###References###
1.	Herth FJF, et al. Endoscopic lung volume reduction: expert panel recommendation—update 2019. Respiration. 2019;97:548–557. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30836374/
2.	Criner GJ, et al. LIBERATE trial. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151–1164. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/
3.	Kemp SV, et al. TRANSFORM trial. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2017;196:1535–1543. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28885054/
4.	Herth FJF, et al. STEP-UP 6-mo results. Lancet Respir Med. 2016;4:185–193. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26899390/
5.	Sciurba FC, et al. RENEW trial. JAMA. 2016;315:2178–2189. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27179849/

### Question  ###
A 64-year-old woman with severe emphysema is evaluated for EBV therapy. Thin-section CT (≤1.5 mm) shows RUL-predominant destruction with fissure completeness of 82% between RUL and RML. Platelets 210 K/µL, INR 1.0. Planned bronchoscopy under moderate sedation (FiO₂ 0.28) is scheduled. What is the best strategy to determine EBV eligibility for the RUL?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Proceed with EBV insertion; >80% fissure completeness assures no collateral ventilation
•	(B) Perform intra-procedural Chartis balloon occlusion testing of the RUL
•	(C) Abandon valves and select coils because fissure completeness is <95%
•	(D) Obtain a perfusion scan only; valves work best in the most perfused lobe
•	(E) Place valves in both RUL and RML in the same session without additional testing
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: When CT fissure completeness is intermediate (80–94%), intra-procedural Chartis testing best clarifies collateral ventilation—making choice B correct【Herth 2013; Koster 2016】. Chartis assessment predicts target-lobe volume reduction and clinical response by detecting collateral ventilation; it is specifically recommended when fissure integrity is incomplete on quantitative CT. In contrast, (A) assumes absent collateral ventilation at 82%, which is not reliable. (C) Coils do not require absent collateral ventilation, but abandoning EBV without Chartis may deny a potentially effective therapy【Koster 2016】. (D) Perfusion helps with lobe selection, not CV assessment【Argula 2013】. (E) Treating two lobes on the right without confirming CV risks failure and complications.
Pitfall: Equating “>80% fissure” with “no CV.” Use Chartis when 80–94%.
###References###
1.	Herth FJF, et al. Radiological and clinical outcomes using Chartis. Eur Respir J. 2013;41:302–308. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/22556025/
2.	Koster TD, et al. Combining diagnostic tools maximizes prediction. Respiration. 2016;92:150–157. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27577190/
3.	Argula RG, et al. Perfusion impacts exercise response to valves. Chest. 2013;144:1578–1586. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23828481/

### Question  ###
A 71-year-old man develops an immediate post-procedural pneumothorax after successful left upper-lobe EBV placement. A 16F chest tube is inserted on −20 cm H₂O suction. On day 8, he has a persistent air leak but remains hemodynamically stable on 2 L/min O₂. What is the most appropriate next step?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Add a second chest tube
•	(B) Remove one endobronchial valve from the treated lobe
•	(C) Transition suction to water seal and discharge
•	(D) Perform endobronchial blood patch through the chest tube
•	(E) Place endobronchial coils in the ipsilateral lower lobe to reduce air leak
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: After EBV-related pneumothorax with air leak persisting ≥7 days, remove one valve as per expert algorithm—making choice B correct【Valipour 2014】. Recommendations emphasize prompt chest-tube drainage; if an air leak persists at day 7, sequential valve removal is advised, with removal of all valves if the leak continues 48 hours after the first removal【Valipour 2014】. (A) A second tube is reserved for large/loculated spaces or inadequate drainage, not routine persistent leak. (C) Water seal/discharge ignores the protocolized escalation at day 7. (D) Blood patching is not first-line in this scenario. (E) Coils have no role in managing an acute EBV-related air leak.
Pearl: EBV-pneumothorax management is protocolized: tube, observe, remove valves at day 7 if still leaking.
###References###
1.	Valipour A, et al. Expert statement on pneumothorax mechanisms and management after valve therapy. Respiration. 2014;87:513–521. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24777292/

### Question  ###
A 62-year-old woman with heterogeneous emphysema is evaluated for target-lobe selection. Quantitative CT shows similar destruction in LUL and LLL; HRCT fissures are complete. Perfusion scintigraphy shows LUL perfusion 8% vs LLL 22% of total. She is scheduled for EBV under general anesthesia (FiO₂ 0.4). Which approach best aligns with evidence?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Treat the more perfused lobe (LLL) to maximize recruitment
•	(B) Treat the less perfused lobe (LUL) to avoid V/Q mismatch
•	(C) Treat both LUL and LLL in one session to ensure lobar collapse
•	(D) Avoid valves; choose coils because perfusion is discordant
•	(E) Use thermal vapor ablation because it targets segments
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: When fissures are complete and two lobes are similarly destroyed, preferentially target the less perfused diseased lobe to minimize post-procedural V/Q mismatch—making choice B correct. In a cohort receiving EBVs, lower baseline perfusion in the treated lobe correlated with better exercise responses, supporting avoidance of high-perfusion lobes when alternatives exist【Argula 2013】. (A) Targeting a high-perfusion lobe risks hypoxemia from abrupt perfusion shifts. (C) Bilobar EBV on one side is not standard and increases risk. (D) Coils are not indicated solely due to perfusion patterns. (E) Thermal vapor ablation evidence applies mainly to upper-lobe heterogeneous disease but does not supersede EBVs in this fissure-complete patient【Herth 2016】.
Pearl: Perfusion scintigraphy can refine EBV target selection—avoid highly perfused lobes when options exist.
###References###
1.	Argula RG, et al. Chest. 2013;144:1578–1586. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23828481/
2.	Herth FJF, et al. STEP-UP trial (6-mo). Lancet Respir Med. 2016;4:185–193. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26899390/

### Question  ###
A 68-year-old man with severe homogeneous emphysema, complete fissures on QCT, and absent collateral ventilation by Chartis undergoes pre-procedure planning. Which statement best reflects trial-level expectations with EBV therapy?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) No proven benefit in homogeneous disease; EBVs help only in heterogeneous disease
•	(B) EBVs improve quality of life but not lung function when CV is absent
•	(C) Clinically meaningful gains in FEV₁, RV, and symptoms are achievable when CV is absent
•	(D) Benefits occur only if both upper lobes are treated in one session
•	(E) Outcomes are inferior to coils in homogeneous disease with hyperinflation
Correct answer: C
Explanation
Bottom line: In patients with homogeneous emphysema without collateral ventilation, EBVs can produce clinically meaningful improvements—making choice C correct. The IMPACT randomized trial demonstrated significant FEV₁, RV, and symptom improvements in homogeneous disease when CV was excluded and lobar occlusion achieved【Valipour 2016】. (A) and (B) contradict these results. (D) Single-lobe treatment is standard; bilateral same-session treatment is not required by evidence. (E) Coils have mixed benefit with higher pneumonia rates and did not clearly outperform in homogeneous disease in RCTs【Sciurba 2016; Deslée 2016】.
Pearl: Absence of collateral ventilation—not heterogeneity alone—drives EBV success.
###References###

3.	Deslée G, et al. REVOLENS. JAMA. 2016;315:175–184. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26757466/

### Question  ###
A 70-year-old woman with severe heterogeneous emphysema (FEV₁ 28% pred, RV 195% pred) and complete fissures has no collateral ventilation by Chartis. Under general anesthesia (pressure-control, FiO₂ 0.5), she undergoes Zephyr EBV placement in the RUL. Which outcome profile is most consistent with multicenter randomized trials?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Improvements in FEV₁, RV, 6-min walk distance, and dyspnea, with pneumothorax as the most frequent serious adverse event
•	(B) Transient FEV₁ improvement without change in 6-min walk or RV; lowest pneumothorax risk among BLVR options
•	(C) No functional benefit unless both upper lobes are occluded; pneumothorax rare
•	(D) Quality-of-life gains only in lower-lobe treatment; pneumothorax uncommon
•	(E) Similar benefits to coils but with higher pneumonia rates than coils
Correct answer: A
Explanation
Bottom line: EBV RCTs (LIBERATE/TRANSFORM) consistently show clinically meaningful improvements in FEV₁, RV, walk distance, and dyspnea—with pneumothorax the most frequent serious adverse event—making choice A correct【Criner 2018; Kemp 2017】. (B) and (D) understate efficacy demonstrated across outcomes. (C) Bilateral same-session upper-lobe treatment is not required. (E) Coils have different risk profiles (notably more pneumonia), and trials did not show EBVs having higher pneumonia rates than coils【Sciurba 2016】.
Pearl: Expect benefit across physiology, exercise capacity, and symptoms after successful EBV—counsel about early pneumothorax risk.

1.	Criner GJ, et al. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151–1164. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/

### Question  ###
A 66-year-old man with hyperinflation (RV 210% pred) and severe emphysema has incomplete interlobar fissures on QCT and persistent collateral ventilation on Chartis. He is on apixaban for atrial fibrillation (held 48 h pre-procedure). Which bronchoscopic strategy remains reasonable based on randomized data?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Zephyr EBVs to the most destroyed lobe despite collateral ventilation
•	(B) Endobronchial coils targeting one lobe per lung in staged procedures
•	(C) Thermal vapor ablation of multiple segments in one session regardless of distribution
•	(D) Lung sealant to block CV and induce collapse in both upper lobes
•	(E) No bronchoscopic option is evidence-based when CV is present
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: Coils act by parenchymal tensioning/compression and do not require absence of collateral ventilation; staged bilateral coil therapy is evidence-based—making choice B correct【Deslée 2016; Sciurba 2016】. (A) EBVs depend on absent CV for efficacy. (C) Thermal vapor ablation has randomized data mainly for upper-lobe heterogeneous disease and is not generally applied “regardless of distribution”【Herth 2016】. (D) Lung sealant showed efficacy but with high severe adverse event rates and is not widely adopted【Come 2015】. (E) Coils and segmental vapor ablation remain options in selected patients with CV.
Pearl: When CV precludes EBVs, consider coils; their mechanism is independent of fissure integrity.

1.	Deslée G, et al. REVOLENS. JAMA. 2016;315:175–184. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26757466/

4.	Come CE, et al. Lung sealant vs medical therapy. Eur Respir J. 2015;46:651–662. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25837041/

### Question  ###
A 72-year-old woman with upper-lobe–predominant heterogeneous emphysema has persistent dyspnea after rehab. She declines valves. HRCT shows severe RUL and LUL destruction; fissures are variable. You plan segment-level therapy in staged sessions (FiO₂ 0.35). Which statement best reflects data for bronchoscopic thermal vapor ablation?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Evidence is limited to case series; no randomized data exist
•	(B) Randomized data support segmental dosing with improvements in FEV₁, RV, and dyspnea at 6–12 months
•	(C) Benefits occur only when both upper lobes are treated in a single session
•	(D) Serious adverse events are dominated by pneumothorax
•	(E) Therapy requires absent collateral ventilation by Chartis testing
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: The multicenter STEP-UP randomized trial supports segmental thermal vapor ablation with clinically meaningful improvements in FEV₁, RV, and symptoms at 6–12 months—making choice B correct【Herth 2016; Shah 2016】. (A) is incorrect—randomized data exist. (C) STEP-UP used selective, sequential treatment, not mandatory single-session bilobar therapy. (D) The most common serious adverse events were COPD exacerbations rather than pneumothorax【Herth 2016】. (E) Vapor ablation does not require absent collateral ventilation.
Pearl: Thermal vapor ablation offers segment-level reduction with benefits shown in upper-lobe heterogeneous emphysema.

### Question  ###
A 65-year-old woman undergoes successful left upper-lobe EBV placement (FiO₂ 0.4). A chest radiograph at 24 hours shows complete LUL atelectasis without pneumothorax. She asks what this means for her long-term outlook. What is the best evidence-based counseling?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Lobar atelectasis predicts worse survival due to shunt
•	(B) Atelectasis has no relation to survival; only FEV₁ change matters
•	(C) Lobar atelectasis after valves has been associated with improved survival in observational cohorts
•	(D) Atelectasis predicts higher pneumonia risk but no functional gain
•	(E) Atelectasis implies valve failure and need for removal
Correct answer: C
Explanation
Bottom line: Observational data link post-valve lobar atelectasis with better survival—making choice C correct. Early and later cohorts found an association between valve-induced atelectasis and improved survival compared with no atelectasis【Hopkinson 2011; Gompelmann 2019】. These findings support atelectasis as a favorable marker of effective target-lobe occlusion rather than harm. (A), (B), (D), and (E) are inconsistent with these data.
Pearl: After EBV, lobar atelectasis usually indicates effective occlusion and has been associated with better survival.
###References###
1.	Hopkinson NS, et al. Atelectasis & survival after BLVR. Eur Respir J. 2011;37:1346–1351. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20947683/
2.	Gompelmann D, et al. Survival after valve therapy. Respiration. 2019;97:145–152. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30227420/

### Question  ###
A 69-year-old man with severe heterogeneous emphysema is considering the Spiration Valve System (SVS). HRCT shows ≥90% fissure completeness in the target lobe; Chartis shows no CV. He asks what to expect and what risks predominate. Which statement best reflects trial data?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) SVS improves CT volume only; no functional or QOL changes
•	(B) SVS improves FEV₁ and symptoms, with COPD exacerbation the commonest adverse event; pneumothorax occurs in a minority
•	(C) SVS yields similar benefit only when two lobes are occluded
•	(D) SVS is contraindicated if fissures are ≥90% complete
•	(E) SVS has higher pneumonia rates than coils in RCTs
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: In randomized trials of SVS (REACH, EMPROVE), patients experienced significant improvements in FEV₁ and health status; COPD exacerbation was the most frequent adverse event, and pneumothorax occurred in a notable minority—making choice B correct【Li 2019; Criner 2019】. (A) contradicts both trials. (C) Bilobar occlusion is not required. (D) Fissure completeness ≥90% is part of selection, not a contraindication. (E) Head-to-head coil comparisons are lacking; RCTs of coils show higher pneumonia in coil arms versus controls, not a direct comparison to SVS【Sciurba 2016】.
Pearl: For SVS, set expectations: functional gains with COPD exacerbations and pneumothorax as key monitored risks.
###References###
1.	Li S, et al. REACH trial (SVS vs SoC). Respiration. 2019;97:416–427. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30554211/
2.	Criner GJ, et al. EMPROVE trial. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2019;200:1354–1362. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/31365298/
3.	Sciurba FC, et al. RENEW (coils). JAMA. 2016;315:2178–2189. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27179849/
### Question  ###
A 62 year old woman (40 pack year ex smoker) with severe COPD has dyspnea despite dual bronchodilators and completed pulmonary rehabilitation. Spirometry shows FEV₁ 32% predicted; RV 190% predicted. Dlco is 18% predicted. Thin section CT (1.0 mm) demonstrates heterogeneous, upper lobe–predominant emphysema. Quantitative analysis shows complete right major fissure. During bronchoscopy under general anesthesia, Chartis balloon occlusion of the right upper lobe demonstrates expiratory flow that decays to zero (no collateral ventilation). Perfusion scintigraphy shows RUL 10%, RML 13%, RLL 30%, LUL 20%, LLL 27%. Echocardiography estimates RVSP 44 mm Hg. She asks whether to proceed with bronchoscopic lung volume reduction (BLVR). What is the most appropriate recommendation?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Proceed with Zephyr endobronchial valves to the right upper lobe
•	(B) Proceed with Spiration valves to the right upper lobe based on fissure analysis
•	(C) Stage bilateral endobronchial coils beginning with the right upper lobe
•	(D) Segmental thermal vapor ablation of the right upper lobe
•	(E) Do not proceed with BLVR given very low Dlco; discuss lung transplantation evaluation
Correct answer: E
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice E is correct. The 2019 expert panel recommends against endoscopic lung volume reduction when Dlco is markedly reduced (≈<20% predicted) because gas exchange reserve is inadequate and periprocedural risk may be unacceptable; such patients should be evaluated for advanced therapies including transplantation rather than BLVR. This holds even when other selection features (no collateral ventilation, favorable perfusion distribution) are optimal. 
Evidence from Zephyr RCTs (LIBERATE/STELVIO/TRANSFORM) supports EBV benefits in appropriately selected candidates, but these trials excluded patients with severely reduced Dlco, aligning with the panel’s guidance. 10–13
Why the others are wrong: A–B) Although fissure integrity and absent collateral ventilation predict EBV success, very low Dlco is an exclusionary factor in selection recommendations. 3,10–13 C) Coils can work even with collateral ventilation, but severe diffusion impairment still argues against BLVR candidacy. 16,27–30 D) Thermal vapor ablation RCTs targeted upper lobe heterogeneous disease but did not establish safety in patients with Dlco this low; the expert panel still prioritizes gas exchange thresholds in candidacy decisions. 18,31
Pitfall: Do not let a “perfect target lobe” (no CV, low perfusion) override a systemic contraindication like Dlco ≈<20%—gas exchange reserve governs candidacy.
###References###
•	Herth FJF, Slebos DJ, Criner GJ, et al. Endoscopic lung volume reduction—expert panel update 2019. Respiration. 2019;97:548 557. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30836374/
•	Marchetti N, Criner GJ. Surgical approaches to emphysema (LVRS, bullectomy, transplant). Semin Respir Crit Care Med. 2015;36:592 608. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26238644/
•	Criner GJ, Sue R, Wright S, et al. LIBERATE RCT. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151 1164. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/

### Question  ###
A 68 year old man (55 pack year former smoker) with severe hyperinflation seeks BLVR. Thin section CT (1.0 mm) shows heterogeneous emphysema affecting the left upper lobe (LUL) and right lower lobe (RLL); fissures appear complete. Under general anesthesia, Chartis occlusion shows absent collateral ventilation in both LUL and RLL. Quantitative perfusion scintigraphy demonstrates: LUL 9%, LLL 25%, RUL 18%, RML 16%, RLL 32%. Which single target selection is most likely to maximize 6 minute walk improvement while minimizing V/Q mismatch?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Right upper lobe with endobronchial valves
•	(B) Left upper lobe with endobronchial valves
•	(C) Right lower lobe with endobronchial valves
•	(D) Left lower lobe with endobronchial valves
•	(E) No valve therapy because both targets are collateral ventilation negative
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice B is correct. When more than one CV negative lobe is eligible, targeting the lobe with the lowest baseline perfusion predicts larger gains in exercise capacity and reduces the risk of V/Q derangement. In this patient, the LUL (9% perfusion) is the lowest perfused CV negative lobe and thus the preferred target for EBV therapy. Argula et al. showed that lower target lobe perfusion correlated with better 6MWT response after EBVs, supporting perfusion guided selection among otherwise suitable lobes. 5 EBV RCTs (TRANSFORM/LIBERATE) confirm that when CV is absent, lobar collapse yields clinically meaningful improvements across physiologic and patient reported outcomes. 10,11 
Why the others are wrong: A) RUL has higher perfusion than LUL, increasing V/Q disturbance risk if collapsed. C) RLL has high perfusion (32%); collapsing it risks larger perfusion loss consequences. D) LLL is not the primary disease site and is more perfused than LUL. E) Dismissing EBVs ignores the favorable physiology (no CV) and the ability to optimize target selection via perfusion.
Pearl: Among EBV eligible lobes, choose the most diseased lobe with the least perfusion to optimize exercise response and minimize V/Q mismatch.
###References###
•	Argula RG, Strange C, Ramakrishnan V, Goldin J. Baseline perfusion predicts EBV exercise response. Chest. 2013;144:1578 1586. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23828481/
•	Kemp SV, Slebos DJ, Kirk A, et al. TRANSFORM RCT. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2017;196:1535 1543. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28885054/
•	Criner GJ, Sue R, Wright S, et al. LIBERATE RCT. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151 1164. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/

### Question  ###
A 70 year old man underwent Zephyr EBV placement to the left upper lobe for heterogeneous emphysema. Within 12 hours he developed a moderate pneumothorax requiring a large bore chest tube. On day 6, a continuous air leak persisted despite appropriate tube position, so one valve was removed. On day 8, the air leak remains brisk and he is clinically stable on 2 L/min O₂. Chest radiograph shows an apical pneumothorax similar in size to prior films. What is the most appropriate next step?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Increase chest tube suction and continue observation
•	(B) Autologous blood patch pleurodesis through the existing tube
•	(C) Remove the remaining valves from the treated lobe
•	(D) Proceed directly to video assisted thoracoscopic repair
•	(E) Insert an additional apical pigtail catheter
Correct answer: C
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice C is correct. In the persistent air leak algorithm after EBV related pneumothorax, ongoing leak despite optimal drainage and removal of one valve should prompt removal of the remaining valves in the treated lobe to reverse the atelectatic shear forces and allow fissural/parenchymal defects to close. Valipour et al. proposed—and subsequent programs adopted—this stepwise approach (drain → observe → remove one valve → if leak persists, remove all valves → consider surgery only if necessary). 21 This strategy reflects pooled trial/registry experience from Zephyr RCTs where most pneumothoraces resolve without surgery but some require valve explantation to stop the leak. 10,11 
Why the others are wrong: A) More suction rarely changes a persistent leak driven by ongoing lobar collapse mechanics. B) Blood patch pleurodesis can be adjunctive but is not first line before completing valve removal in this context. D) Surgery is reserved for refractory cases after appropriate bronchoscopic steps. E) Adding a second drain addresses malposition or loculation, not the underlying continued air leak from the treated lobe.
Pearl: In post EBV pneumothorax with a persistent leak, complete valve removal—before surgery—is often the decisive step.
###References###
•	Valipour A, Slebos DJ, de Oliveira HG, et al. Expert statement on pneumothorax after endoscopic valve therapy. Respiration. 2014;87:513 521. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24777292/
•	Criner GJ, Sue R, Wright S, et al. LIBERATE RCT. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151 1164. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/
•	Kemp SV, Slebos DJ, Kirk A, et al. TRANSFORM RCT. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2017;196:1535 1543. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28885054/

### Question  ###
A 59 year old woman with severe upper lobe–predominant emphysema (FEV₁ 29% predicted, RV 175%) is not improving on maximal inhaled therapy. Thin section CT (1.0 mm) shows incomplete upper lobe fissures bilaterally. During bronchoscopy under general anesthesia, Chartis occlusion of the right upper lobe shows persistent, non declining flow (collateral ventilation present). She asks for a bronchoscopic option that can target only the most destroyed segments of the upper lobes rather than the whole lobe. Which intervention best fits her goals and has randomized trial evidence in this phenotype?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Zephyr endobronchial valves to the right upper lobe
•	(B) Spiration Valve System to the right upper lobe
•	(C) Lobar endobronchial coils distributed in the right upper lobe
•	(D) Segmental thermal vapor ablation of selected upper lobe segments
•	(E) Continue medical therapy alone because BLVR requires absent collateral ventilation
Correct answer: D
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice D is correct. Thermal vapor ablation delivers heated water vapor to selected segments, producing localized scarring and volume loss. The STEP UP randomized trial in upper lobe–predominant heterogeneous emphysema demonstrated clinically meaningful improvements in FEV₁, RV, and dyspnea versus standard care using a segmental treatment strategy; its mechanism does not rely on the absence of collateral ventilation. 18,31 In contrast, one way valves require fissural integrity/no CV to achieve lobar collapse, so options A and B are poor  ### Options###  here. 10–13 Coils (C) do not require absent CV and have RCT evidence, but they are lobe level compression devices rather than a segment level therapy tailored to only the most destroyed segments—the goal the patient prefers. 27–30 
Why the others are wrong: A–B) Valves depend on lobar isolation; CV predicts non response. C) Coils are plausible but do not fulfill the patient’s request for segmental treatment. E) Not true—non valve BLVR (vapor ablation; also sealant in trials) can work despite CV.
Pearl: Match mechanism to anatomy: segment level disease in CV positive upper lobes favors vapor ablation rather than valves.
###References###
•	Herth FJ, Valipour A, Shah PL, et al. STEP UP randomized trial of thermal vapor ablation. Lancet Respir Med. 2016;4:185 193. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26899390/

•	Deslée G, Mal H, Dutau H, et al. REVOLENS (coils) RCT. JAMA. 2016;315:175 184. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26757466/

### Question  ###
A 65 year old man with heterogeneous emphysema is referred to your center, which does not have Chartis. Thin section CT (1.25 mm) shows severe disease in the right upper lobe; quantitative analysis estimates right major fissure completeness at 88%. He wants a bronchoscopic option with the best evidence base. What is the most appropriate next step?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Refer to a center for Chartis guided evaluation to determine candidacy for Zephyr EBVs
•	(B) Proceed with Spiration Valve System placement based on the current CT alone
•	(C) Defer all BLVR because Chartis is unavailable at your center
•	(D) Treat the right upper lobe first with coils, then consider valves if needed
•	(E) Offer lower lobe thermal vapor ablation based on current imaging
Correct answer: A
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice A is correct. For Zephyr EBVs, pivotal trials used Chartis to confirm absent interlobar collateral ventilation as a prerequisite for benefit; a center with Chartis can determine true candidacy despite borderline fissure completeness on CT. 10–13 In contrast, randomized trials of the Spiration Valve System (SVS) required CT fissure completeness ≳90% for selection; proceeding with SVS at 88% would not reflect its trial based selection strategy. 26,32 Thus the most evidence consistent path is referral for Chartis to assess CV; if negative, EBV therapy can be considered in line with Zephyr RCTs. 
Why the others are wrong: B) SVS trials (REACH/EMPROVE) hinged on ≥~90% fissure completeness; at 88%, candidacy is uncertain and not evidence based. 26,32 C) Lack of Chartis locally should not preclude EBV consideration—refer. D) Coils are an option in CV positive/incomplete fissure settings, but they are not the first step when a Chartis guided EBV pathway may be suitable. 27–30 E) Thermal vapor ablation evidence is for upper lobe–predominant disease; the ### Question  ### asks for the best next selection/evaluation step, not to retarget a different lobe. 18
Pearl: SVS relied on CT fissure thresholds, whereas Zephyr trials relied on Chartis—use the selection method that matches the device’s evidence base.
###References###
•	Criner GJ, Sue R, Wright S, et al. LIBERATE RCT (Chartis guided Zephyr). Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151 1164. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/
•	Li S, Wang G, Wang C, et al. REACH RCT (SVS; fissure ≥90%). Respiration. 2019;97:416 427. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30554211/
•	Criner GJ, Delage A, Voelker K, et al. EMPROVE RCT (SVS). Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2019;200:1354 1362. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/31365298/
### Question  ###
A 64-year old woman with severe COPD (45 pack year former smoker) has persistent dyspnea despite triple inhaled therapy and pulmonary rehabilitation. Spirometry shows FEV₁ 27% predicted with RV 180% predicted. Thin section HRCT (1.0 mm) demonstrates heterogeneous, upper lobe–predominant emphysema with an intact right major fissure on quantitative analysis. During bronchoscopy under general anesthesia, Chartis balloon occlusion of the right upper lobe shows expiratory flow that declines to zero over time. She asks what feature best predicts whether she will achieve lobar atelectasis and meaningful clinical benefit after endobronchial valve placement. Which variable is most predictive?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Absence of interlobar collateral ventilation confirmed by Chartis assessment
•	(B) Presence of a CT “bronchus sign” in the target lobe
•	(C) Peripheral blood eosinophil count ≥300/µL
•	(D) Resting PaO₂ ≥60 mm Hg on room air
•	(E) Baseline 6 minute walk distance ≥350 m
Correct answer: A
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice A is correct. Randomized trials and mechanistic studies show that absence of interlobar collateral ventilation (CV)—demonstrated by Chartis or by complete fissures—predicts lobar atelectasis and the largest gains in FEV₁, exercise capacity, and quality of life after endobronchial valve (EBV) therapy. 
Evidence from STELVIO (NEJM) demonstrated substantial benefit only when CV was absent; TRANSFORM and LIBERATE confirmed clinically meaningful improvements in CV negative targets, while the earlier VENT trial’s modest effects were explained in part by untreated CV. Chartis guided selection improves the likelihood of complete lobar occlusion and volume reduction, and combining fissure analysis with Chartis maximizes prediction of response. 
Why the others are wrong: B) The “bronchus sign” predicts peripheral nodule sampling, not BLVR response (not a selection variable in BLVR RCTs). C) Peripheral eosinophilia influences inhaled steroid responsiveness/asthma traits, not EBV efficacy in emphysema trials. D) Resting PaO₂ helps risk stratify but has not been shown to determine EBV benefit. E) Baseline 6 minute walk distance is an outcome and safety parameter, not the primary determinant of achieving lobar collapse; responders are driven by CV status. Across trials, the consistent signal is that no CV targets respond; CV positive targets do not. 
Pearl: EBV success hinges on physiology (no collateral ventilation) more than on absolute emphysema burden—confirm with Chartis and/or fissure completeness before committing to valves.
###References###
•	Klooster K, ten Hacken NH, Hartman JE, et al. Endobronchial valves for emphysema without interlobar collateral ventilation. N Engl J Med. 2015;373:2325 2335. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26650153/
•	Herth FJF, Eberhardt R, Gompelmann D, et al. Radiological and clinical outcomes of using Chartis to plan endobronchial valve treatment. Eur Respir J. 2013;41:302 308. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/22556025/
•	Koster TD, van Rikxoort EM, Huebner RH, et al. Predicting lung volume reduction after EBV therapy using combined diagnostic tools. Respiration. 2016;92:150 157. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27577190/
•	Sciurba FC, Ernst A, Herth FJ, et al. A randomized study of endobronchial valves for advanced emphysema (VENT). N Engl J Med. 2010;363:1233 1244. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20860505/

### Question  ###
A 70 year old man with heterogeneous upper lobe–predominant emphysema underwent Zephyr EBV placement to the right upper lobe yesterday. He developed an immediate pneumothorax requiring a 24 Fr chest tube to −20 cm H₂O suction. On hospital day 7, he remains hemodynamically stable but has a continuous air leak. Chest radiograph shows a small, persistent apical pneumothorax. What is the most appropriate next step?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Continue the current suction regimen and reassess in 72 hours
•	(B) Clamp the chest tube to test for auto pleurodesis
•	(C) Insert an additional apical pigtail catheter
•	(D) Remove one endobronchial valve from the treated lobe
•	(E) Proceed directly to video assisted thoracoscopic bullectomy
Correct answer: D
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice D is correct. Expert consensus on post EBV pneumothorax recommends sequential removal of valves when a significant air leak persists despite chest drainage—typically beginning after a week of continuous leak—because removing a valve reduces the atelectatic shear driving the leak and facilitates closure. This algorithm arose from pooled experience across EBV trials (e.g., LIBERATE/TRANSFORM) in which pneumothorax clustered early and usually resolved with chest drainage; persistent leaks occasionally required valve removal. 
Why the others are wrong: A) Passive observation beyond a week with an ongoing leak is unlikely to change trajectory and prolongs hospitalization. B) Tube clamping with an active air leak risks tension physiology. C) Adding another drain treats recurrence or malposition, not an established persistent air leak driven by lobar collapse mechanics. E) Surgery is rarely needed when evidence based bronchoscopic steps (including staged valve removal) are followed. 
Pitfall: Delaying valve removal in a persistent post EBV air leak can unnecessarily extend chest tube days and hospital stay; follow the stepwise algorithm before surgical referral.
###References###
•	Valipour A, Slebos DJ, de Oliveira HG, et al. Expert statement: pneumothorax associated with endoscopic valve therapy—mechanisms and treatment algorithm. Respiration. 2014;87:513 521. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24777292/
•	Criner GJ, Sue R, Wright S, et al. LIBERATE: Zephyr EBV in heterogeneous emphysema. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151 1164. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/
•	Kemp SV, Slebos DJ, Kirk A, et al. TRANSFORM randomized trial of Zephyr EBV. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2017;196:1535 1543. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28885054/

### Question  ###
A 67 year old man (50 pack year ex smoker) with severe dyspnea and hyperinflation is evaluated for BLVR. HRCT shows heterogeneous emphysema in both right upper and right lower lobes; fissures are complete. Chartis shows absent CV in both lobes. Quantitative perfusion scintigraphy demonstrates relative perfusion: RUL 8%, RML 12%, RLL 35%, LUL 18%, LLL 27%. To maximize exercise improvement and minimize V/Q mismatch, which target would you choose for valve treatment?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Right lower lobe
•	(B) Left upper lobe
•	(C) Left lower lobe
•	(D) Right middle lobe
•	(E) Right upper lobe
Correct answer: E
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice E is correct. When multiple EBV eligible lobes are available, targeting the lobe with the lowest baseline perfusion (here, the right upper lobe at 8%) is associated with greater gains in exercise performance and less V/Q disruption than collapsing a more highly perfused lobe. In a study correlating baseline regional perfusion with outcomes after EBV, lower target lobe perfusion predicted larger 6 minute walk improvements. EBV RCTs provide the efficacy foundation once the physiologic precondition (no CV) is met; perfusion helps choose between equally suitable lobes. 
Why the others are wrong: A) Collapsing the RLL (35% perfusion) risks larger perfusion loss and V/Q imbalance. B–C) Left sided lobes are not the predominant disease/lowest perfusion targets here. D) RML is not the primary disease site and has higher perfusion than RUL.
Pearl: Among EBV eligible lobes, pick the most emphysematous lobe with the least perfusion to optimize exercise outcomes.
###References###
•	Argula RG, Strange C, Ramakrishnan V, Goldin J. Baseline regional perfusion impacts exercise response to EBV therapy. Chest. 2013;144:1578 1586. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23828481/
•	Criner GJ, Sue R, Wright S, et al. LIBERATE randomized trial. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151 1164. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/
•	Herth FJF, Slebos DJ, Criner GJ, et al. Endoscopic lung volume reduction—expert panel update 2019. Respiration. 2019;97:548 557. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30836374/

### Question  ###
A 62 year old man with homogeneous panlobular emphysema (FEV₁ 28% predicted, RV 175% predicted) completed pulmonary rehab. Thin section HRCT shows diffuse disease with intact fissures. Balloon occlusion testing confirms absent interlobar collateral ventilation in the left lower lobe. Which intervention has randomized trial evidence for clinically meaningful improvement in this phenotype?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Staged bilateral coil implantation
•	(B) Endobronchial valve placement to a single target lobe
•	(C) Thermal vapor ablation of the most diseased segments
•	(D) Upper lobe lung volume reduction surgery only
•	(E) Continue maximal medical therapy without intervention
Correct answer: B
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice B is correct. The IMPACT randomized trial enrolled patients with homogeneous emphysema and no CV and showed that Zephyr EBV treatment produced clinically meaningful improvements in FEV₁, hyperinflation, dyspnea, and exercise capacity compared with standard care. STELVIO and subsequent trials reinforce that no CV status is the key determinant of response, independent of heterogeneity. LIBERATE/TRANSFORM provide additional RCT confirmation of benefits when CV is absent. 
Why the others are wrong: A) Coils showed modest average gains; benefits were greater in selected subgroups, but RENEW did not show broad, robust superiority over usual care on its primary endpoint. C) Thermal vapor ablation RCT data (STEP UP) involve upper lobe–predominant heterogeneous disease, not homogeneous phenotypes. D) Surgical LVRS confers benefit in specific heterogeneous, upper lobe–predominant NETT like profiles, not diffuse homogeneous disease. E) Medical therapy alone lacks the additional improvements demonstrated with EBVs in the CV negative population.
Pearl: Do not exclude EBVs solely because emphysema is homogeneous; absence of collateral ventilation is the pivotal selection criterion.
###References###
•	Valipour A, Slebos DJ, Herth F, et al. IMPACT: EBVs in homogeneous emphysema. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2016;194:1073 1082. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27580428/

### Question  ###
A 69 year old woman with heterogeneous emphysema is evaluated for BLVR. HRCT (1.0 mm) shows complete fissures. Chartis occlusion of the left upper lobe demonstrates persistent, non declining expiratory flow; occlusion of the right lower lobe shows flow that decays to zero. She asks how to proceed to maximize benefit while minimizing risk. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Place endobronchial valves in the left upper lobe
•	(B) Switch to endobronchial coils in the left upper lobe
•	(C) Treat the right lower lobe with endobronchial valves
•	(D) Proceed to surgical LVRS for the left upper lobe
•	(E) Defer intervention and continue medical therapy
Correct answer: C
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice C is correct. Chartis planning identifies CV negative targets; EBV therapy should be directed to lobes with absent CV, where valve induced lobar collapse and clinical benefit are most likely. Treating a CV positive lobe (here, LUL) yields poor outcomes. Chartis guided selection and quantitative CT have been shown to improve response prediction and enable intra procedural target switching. RCTs (TRANSFORM/LIBERATE) confirm robust improvements when CV is absent. 
Why the others are wrong: A) Proceeding in a CV positive lobe risks non collapse and poor response. B) Coils are an alternative approach, but given an EBV eligible CV negative lobe, valves have strong randomized trial support. D) LVRS has a different risk/benefit profile and is not first line when a CV negative EBV target exists. E) Deferring intervention overlooks an available, evidence based option with expected symptomatic gains.
Pearl: If the primary target proves CV positive, re test another lobe—treat the first CV negative, well diseased lobe rather than abandoning valves.
###References###
•	Herth FJF, Eberhardt R, Gompelmann D, et al. Chartis guided planning improves clinical/radiologic outcomes. Eur Respir J. 2013;41:302 308. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/22556025/
•	Kemp SV, Slebos DJ, Kirk A, et al. TRANSFORM RCT. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2017;196:1535 1543. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28885054/
•	Criner GJ, Sue R, Wright S, et al. LIBERATE RCT. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2018;198:1151 1164. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29787288/

### Question  ###
A 65 year old man with severe emphysema has an incomplete left major fissure (≈70% on quantitative CT). He seeks a bronchoscopic option but is told he is unlikely to respond to valves because of collateral ventilation. Which bronchoscopic technique has efficacy that does not depend on absent interlobar collateral ventilation?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Zephyr endobronchial valves
•	(B) Spiration Valve System
•	(C) Endobronchial coils
•	(D) Endobronchial valves placed in two lobes of the same lung
•	(E) Endobronchial valves despite Chartis confirmed collateral ventilation
Correct answer: C
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice C is correct. Endobronchial coils compress emphysematous parenchyma and improve elastic recoil; their mechanism does not require fissure integrity or absence of CV, distinguishing them from one way valves. Best practice recommendations and randomized trials (RESET, REVOLENS, RENEW) support symptomatic and physiologic benefits, though average gains are modest and pneumonia/pneumothorax risks exist. 
Why the others are wrong: A–B) Valve efficacy depends on achieving lobar isolation; incomplete fissures/CV predict non response. D) Treating two lobes with valves does not bypass the need for lobar isolation across major fissures. E) Proceeding with valves despite Chartis confirmed CV is associated with poor outcomes.
Pearl: Consider coils when fissures are incomplete or Chartis indicates collateral ventilation—valves work best only when lobar isolation is achievable.
###References###
•	Slebos DJ, Ten Hacken NH, Hetzel M, Herth FJF, Shah PL. Endobronchial coils: best practice recommendations. Respiration. 2018;96:1 11. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29991060/
•	Shah PL, Zoumot Z, Singh S, et al. RESET randomized trial of coils. Lancet Respir Med. 2013;1:233 240. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24429129/
•	Deslée G, Mal H, Dutau H, et al. REVOLENS randomized trial. JAMA. 2016;315:175 184. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26757466/
•	Sciurba FC, Criner GJ, Strange C, et al. RENEW randomized trial. JAMA. 2016;315:2178 2189. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27179849/

### Question  ###
A 66 year old woman with severe heterogeneous emphysema is being considered for valve therapy. HRCT shows an intact left major fissure (≈92% complete by quantitative analysis). Chartis is not available at your center. Based on the evidence for the Spiration Valve System (SVS), which selection strategy aligns with its randomized trials?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Proceed with SVS using CT fissure completeness ≥90% without Chartis testing
•	(B) Require a negative Chartis study before any SVS placement
•	(C) Offer SVS only to homogeneous emphysema
•	(D) Limit SVS to lower lobe targets per trial protocols
•	(E) Select the highest perfusion lobe to avoid V/Q mismatch
Correct answer: A
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice A is correct. The REACH and EMPROVE randomized trials of the SVS relied on CT based fissure completeness thresholds (≈≥90%) to select candidates and demonstrated clinically meaningful improvements in FEV₁, symptoms, and quality of life versus standard care. Routine Chartis testing was not mandated in these SVS trials. 
Why the others are wrong: B) Chartis can be useful but was not required in REACH/EMPROVE. C) The evidence base focused on heterogeneous emphysema. D) Targets included upper and lower lobes; no lower lobe restriction exists. E) Intentionally selecting a high perfusion lobe risks V/Q mismatch; lower perfusion targets are generally favored when  ### Options###  exist. 
Pearl: For SVS, CT based fissure analysis (≥~90% complete) is sufficient for selection in many programs, mirroring the pivotal trials.
###References###

### Question  ###
A 58 year old man underwent segmental thermal vapor ablation of the right upper lobe apical and posterior segments for heterogeneous emphysema (general anesthesia, FiO₂ 0.40). On post procedure day 2, he develops fever, pleuritic chest discomfort, increased cough, and oxygen requirement (SpO₂ 90% on 2 L). CT shows new consolidation limited to the treated segments without effusion. Cultures are pending. What is the most appropriate immediate management?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Schedule lobectomy to remove necrotic treated segments
•	(B) Begin long term azithromycin prophylaxis only
•	(C) Perform urgent bronchoscopy to debride sloughed mucosa
•	(D) Discharge with observation only and no medications
•	(E) Initiate systemic corticosteroids and supportive care while evaluating for infection
Correct answer: E
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice E is correct. After thermal vapor ablation, a predictable post treatment inflammatory response (fever, dyspnea, chest pain, segmental opacities) often occurs in the treated territory. Trials describe managing this syndrome with supportive care and short course systemic corticosteroids, while excluding superimposed infection. A similar inflammatory reaction was reported after biological lung sealant, reinforcing the approach of anti inflammatory therapy plus careful monitoring for complications. 
Why the others are wrong: A) Surgery is not indicated for this expected reaction. B) Chronic macrolide prophylaxis is not evidence based for this acute post procedure response. C) Routine bronchoscopic debridement is unnecessary without obstruction or massive mucus plugging. D) Pure observation risks clinical deterioration without addressing the inflammatory component.
Pearl: Recognize the post BLVR inflammatory syndrome after vapor ablation/sealant—treat early with steroids and supportive care while ruling out infection.
###References###

•	Come CE, Kramer MR, Dransfield MT, et al. Randomized trial of lung sealant vs medical therapy. Eur Respir J. 2015;46:651 662. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25837041/

### Question  ###
A 71 year old man with severe hyperinflation and heterogeneous emphysema is referred for EBV therapy. Pre procedure echocardiogram shows right ventricular systolic pressure estimated at 55 mm Hg with right atrial enlargement. ABG on room air shows PaO₂ 64 mm Hg, PaCO₂ 42 mm Hg. What is the most appropriate next step in evaluation?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Proceed with EBV under careful hemodynamic monitoring
•	(B) Schedule upper lobe LVRS instead of BLVR
•	(C) Cancel EBV and refer directly for lung transplantation
•	(D) Obtain right heart catheterization to confirm and characterize pulmonary hypertension
•	(E) Increase diuretics and proceed to EBV once euvolemic
Correct answer: D
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice D is correct. Expert recommendations for endoscopic lung volume reduction advise formal hemodynamic assessment when echocardiography suggests pulmonary hypertension (e.g., elevated RVSP ~≥50 mm Hg), because significant PH may influence candidacy, periprocedural risk, and post BLVR outcomes. Right heart catheterization is the gold standard to confirm severity and mechanism before proceeding. 
Why the others are wrong: A) Proceeding without invasive confirmation risks undertreating unrecognized severe PH. B) LVRS does not obviate the need to evaluate PH and has its own PH related risks. C) Transplant consideration may be appropriate long term, but definitive PH characterization comes first. E) Empiric diuresis without hemodynamic clarity is insufficient and may delay appropriate decision making.
Pearl: When RVSP is high on echo, confirm with right heart catheterization before BLVR planning—PH severity can be a key determinant of candidacy and risk.
###References###

### Question  ###
Two patients undergo Zephyr EBV placement. Patient A (he) develops complete left lower lobe atelectasis on imaging within 2 weeks; Patient B (she) shows no significant volume loss in the treated lobe. Over long term follow up (years), which outcome difference is most likely based on available evidence?
 ### Options### 
•	(A) Equivalent survival; only dyspnea differs
•	(B) Higher mortality in the atelectasis group due to pneumothorax risk
•	(C) Improved long term survival associated with development of lobar atelectasis
•	(D) No survival difference, but higher pneumonia risk in the atelectasis group
•	(E) Increased need for re intervention in the atelectasis group due to hypoxemia
Correct answer: C
Explanation
Bottom line: Choice C is correct. Observational cohorts show that radiographic lobar atelectasis after EBV—a marker of effective lobar isolation and deflation—is associated with better long term survival. Hopkinson et al. first reported a survival advantage in patients with post BLVR atelectasis; subsequent series, including a large single center analysis, found improved 5 year survival when lobar collapse occurred, without evidence that pneumothorax worsened survival. 
While randomized trials were not designed for survival endpoints, the atelectasis survival association is consistent across cohorts.
Why the others are wrong: A) Survival differences have been observed, favoring those with atelectasis. B) Although pneumothorax is common early, it has not been linked to worse long term survival in these cohorts. D) No robust signal shows higher pneumonia driven mortality in the atelectasis group. E) Effective lobar collapse typically correlates with fewer symptoms, not increased re intervention.
Pearl: Post EBV lobar atelectasis is a good sign—its presence correlates with durable physiologic benefit and improved survival in observational studies.
###References###
•	Hopkinson NS, Kemp SV, Toma TP, et al. Atelectasis and survival after BLVR. Eur Respir J. 2011;37:1346 1351. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20947683/
•	Gompelmann D, Benjamin N, Bischoff E, et al. Survival after endoscopic valve therapy in severe emphysema. Respiration. 2019;97:145 152. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30227420/
•	Venuta F, Anile M, Diso D, et al. Long term follow up after BLVR. Eur Respir J. 2012;39:1084 1089. https://.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/22005999/
Pleural Disease
### Question  ###
A 62 year old man with type 2 diabetes, poor dentition, and alcohol use presents with 3 days of fever and pleuritic chest pain. He is tachycardic (HR 112), febrile (38.7 °C), and mildly hypoxemic on 2 L/min nasal cannula (SpO₂ 93%). Thoracic ultrasound shows a right, complex, septated pleural effusion. Diagnostic thoracentesis returns cloudy fluid with pH 7.16, glucose 58 mg/dL, and LDH 1,200 IU/L. Platelets are 220 × 10⁹/L and INR 1.0. What is the most appropriate next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Begin broad spectrum IV antibiotics alone and repeat thoracentesis tomorrow
(b) Obtain contrast enhanced “pleural phase” CT before intervening
(c) Insert an ultrasound guided ≤14F chest tube for drainage now
(d) Instill intrapleural tPA/DNase immediately before tube placement
(e) Schedule next day elective VATS decortication without tube drainage
###Discussion###
Bottom line: With pleural fluid pH ≤7.20 and a complex effusion, early tube drainage is indicated—making choice (c) correct. Evidence cited below is drawn from articles listed in the user supplied chapter reference lists. 

The 2023 BTS guideline stratifies pleural infection/complicated parapneumonic effusion (CPPE) by pleural pH; pH ≤7.20 confers high risk and warrants prompt intercostal chest drain if sonographically safe (often with a small bore tube), alongside antibiotics [BTS 2023]. This pH based approach is supported by prior work on factors that influence measurement and interpretation of pleural pH/glucose [Rahman 2008]. Regarding tube size, analysis from large pleural infection trials found no outcome advantage to larger drains, while ≤14F tubes are associated with less pain; regular saline flushes help maintain patency [BTS 2023; MIST 1/2 era data].
Why not the others? (a) Antibiotics alone are inadequate with pH ≤7.20 and a complex effusion. (b) Pleural phase CT helps define anatomy but should not delay indicated drainage; ultrasound already shows a drainable collection. (d) tPA/DNase is considered after initial tube drainage if medical treatment fails or is insufficient; it’s not given prior to establishing drainage. (e) Immediate VATS without an initial drainage attempt is not first line in a stable patient, and many improve with tube ± IET; randomized data are ongoing regarding early VATS vs IET.
Pearl: Pleural fluid pH ≤7.20 is a decision threshold for immediate drainage when safe access exists.
###References###
1.	Roberts ME, et al. British Thoracic Society Guideline for pleural disease. Thorax. 2023;78(Suppl 3):s1–42. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1 
2.	Rahman NM, et al. Clinically important factors influencing diagnostic measurement of pleural fluid pH and glucose. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2008;178:483–90. https://www.atsjournals.org/doi/10.1164/rccm.200801-062OC 
3.	Maskell NA, et al. U.K. trial of intrapleural streptokinase (MIST 1). N Engl J Med. 2005;352:865–74. https://www.nejm.org/doi/10.1056/NEJMoa042473

### Question  ###
A 58 year old woman with community acquired pneumonia develops a moderate left pleural effusion. Thoracic ultrasound shows loculations. At bedside thoracentesis, pleural fluid is turbid. You plan microbiologic testing. Which step best increases organism yield without adding risk?
 ### Options### 
(a) Inoculate pleural fluid directly into aerobic and anaerobic blood culture bottles at the bedside
(b) Send only standard culture bottles; blood culture bottles add little value
(c) Delay cultures until intrapleural tPA/DNase has been given
(d) Perform CT guided pleural biopsy before placing any drain
(e) Rely on serum procalcitonin to infer causative organisms
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (a) is correct—bedside inoculation into blood culture bottles increases diagnostic yield compared with standard culture alone.
A Thorax study (Menzies et al.) demonstrated that placing pleural fluid into blood culture bottles increased culture positivity to ~60%, compared with ~30–40% using standard microbiology alone [Menzies 2011]. Additionally, in MIST 1, peripheral blood cultures were the only positive source in ~12% of patients, further underscoring the value of comprehensive microbiologic sampling early in care [Maskell et al., 2005]. Broad contemporary data emphasize pleural infection’s often polymicrobial nature, supporting robust sampling (and empiric anaerobic coverage) [Kanellakis et al., 2022].
Why not the others? (b) Incorrect; blood culture bottles do add yield. (c) Delaying cultures risks sterilizing the space and reducing yield; sampling should occur before IET when feasible. (d) Ultrasound guided pleural biopsy can increase yield in select settings, but it is not required before drain placement and adds procedural steps; evidence is preliminary (AUDIO study). (e) Serum biomarkers cannot substitute for microbiologic sampling in empyema.
Pearl: Inoculate pleural fluid into blood culture bottles at the bedside—it’s a simple, high value step to improve organism recovery.
###References###
1.	Menzies SM, et al. Blood culture bottle culture of pleural fluid in pleural infection. Thorax. 2011;66:658–62. https://thorax.bmj.com/lookup/doi/10.1136/thx.2010.157842 
2.	Maskell NA, et al. MIST 1: intrapleural streptokinase (negative) trial; microbiology insights. N Engl J Med. 2005;352:865–74. https://www.nejm.org/doi/10.1056/NEJMoa042473 
3.	Kanellakis NI, et al. The bacteriology of pleural infection (TORPIDS). Lancet Microbe. 2022;3:e294–302. https://www.thelancet.com/journals/lanmic/article/PIIS2666-5247(21)00327-X/fulltext

### Question  ###
A 71 year old man on 2 L/min O₂ (SpO₂ 92%) is on day 2 of antibiotics and a 12F ultrasound guided chest tube for a large, septated right empyema. He remains febrile with tachycardia; CRP has fallen only 10% from peak. Chest tube output is minimal. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Continue current care and re assess in 72 hours
(b) Instill tPA/DNase without further imaging
(c) Replace the tube with a 28F drain for better flow
(d) Order a noncontrast CT immediately
(e) Obtain contrast pleural phase CT now and urgently involve thoracic surgery
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Persistent SIRS with residual collection by day 2 meets medical treatment failure criteria; choice (e)—contrast pleural phase CT plus early surgical input—is appropriate.
Delays in optimal drainage beyond ~2 days are associated with worse outcomes, supporting the concept of early escalation when there is systemic inflammation and persistent collection despite antibiotics and a drain [Meyer 2018]. Pleural phase contrast CT clarifies residual locules, drain position, and adjacent pathology, informing either additional catheter placement, IET, or early operative management [Heffner 2010; BTS 2023]. RCT feasibility work (MIST 3) shows teams can randomize to early VATS or IET; while definitive superiority data are pending, timely escalation rather than “watchful waiting” is emphasized [MIST 3, 2023].
Why not the others? (a) Waiting risks harm. (b) IET is reasonable after imaging confirms a drainable target/loculation pattern; imaging first refines strategy. (c) Upsizing alone doesn’t address malpositioned tubes or multiple locules; larger tubes don’t improve outcomes and cause more pain. (d) Noncontrast imaging is suboptimal; venous “pleural phase” contrast is preferred for pleural enhancement and loculation delineation.
Pitfall: Continuing the same care beyond 48 h despite persistent SIRS and residual collection risks avoidable morbidity.
###References###
1.	Meyer CN, et al. Pleural infection: outcomes and impact of delays. BMC Pulm Med. 2018;18:160. https://bmcpulmmed.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12890-018-0723-5 
2.	Heffner JE, et al. Imaging for pleural space infections. Chest. 2010;137:467–79. https://journal.chestnet.org/article/S0012-3692(15)32011-9/fulltext 
3.	Bedawi EO, et al. MIST 3 feasibility RCT (early VATS vs IET). Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2023;208:1305–15. https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC10765402/

### Question  ###
A 55 year old woman with a loculated empyema has persistent fevers despite antibiotics and a well positioned 12F drain. You plan intrapleural fibrinolytic/enzyme therapy. Which regimen reflects the studied protocol from the pivotal RCT?
 ### Options### 
(a) Urokinase 100,000 IU once daily for 3 days without DNase
(b) tPA 10 mg then DNase 5 mg, each followed by a 10 mL saline flush, twice daily for up to 6 doses with 1 hour clamp
(c) Streptokinase 250,000 IU twice daily for 3 days with DNase
(d) tPA 1 mg alone every 6 hours until output clears
(e) DNase 5 mg twice daily for 3 days without tPA
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (b) is correct—the MIST 2 regimen used tPA 10 mg plus DNase 5 mg twice daily up to six doses, improving radiographic clearance and reducing surgery and LOS compared with placebo [Rahman 2011].
Subsequent series explored dose de escalation (e.g., 2.5 mg tPA starts) or once daily/concurrent dosing, but these were non comparative and sometimes required escalation; they do not supersede the original randomized protocol [Popowicz 2017; 2022; Gupta 2017; Jiang 2020]. Modern statements from ERS/ESTS and BTS recommend combination IET (not fibrinolytic monotherapy) for appropriate non responders to tube + antibiotics, acknowledging ongoing trials against early VATS [ERS/ESTS 2023; BTS 2023].
Why others are wrong: (a) Urokinase monotherapy lacks robust RCT support and is inferior to tPA/DNase in evidence base; it may be considered where alteplase is unavailable but ideally with DNase. (c) Intrapleural streptokinase failed in MIST 1; adding DNase wasn’t studied there. (d) Very low dose tPA monotherapy lacks evidence. (e) DNase alone is inadequate; synergy with tPA is key.
Pearl: Use tPA + DNase together—synergy between fibrinolysis and DNA degradation drives efficacy.
###References###
1.	Rahman NM, et al. Intrapleural tPA/DNase in pleural infection (MIST 2). N Engl J Med. 2011;365:518–26. https://www.nejm.org/doi/10.1056/NEJMoa1012740 
2.	Popowicz N, et al. Alteplase dose de escalation projects. Ann Am Thorac Soc. 2017;14:929–36; Respirology. 2022;27:510. 
3.	Bedawi EO, et al. ERS/ESTS statement on adult pleural infection. Eur Respir J. 2023;61:2201062. http://erj.ersjournals.com/lookup/doi/10.1183/13993003.01062-2022

### Question  ###
A 74 year old man with atrial fibrillation on apixaban presents with a loculated empyema. He has a 12F drain in place and ongoing sepsis. His platelets are 110 × 10⁹/L, creatinine clearance is 22 mL/min, and RAPID score is high. You consider intrapleural tPA/DNase. What is the safest evidence guided approach?
 ### Options### 
(a) Full dose tPA/DNase now; bleeding is rare and unrelated to anticoagulation
(b) Proceed with DNase alone to avoid bleeding risk
(c) Stop the drain and schedule VATS decortication in a week
(d) Temporarily hold systemic anticoagulation, consider dose reduced tPA with DNase, and involve surgery/hematology early
(e) Proceed with tPA/DNase only if platelet transfusion increases platelets above 300 × 10⁹/L
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d) is correct—bleeding risk with IET is higher in patients on systemic anticoagulation, with renal insufficiency, low platelets, and higher RAPID scores; mitigation includes holding anticoagulation when feasible, considering dose reduction, and team ###Discussion### [Akulian 2022].
In the largest series of IET treated patients (n≈1850), pleural bleeding occurred in ~3–5%; most were managed conservatively (transfusion), but risk was significantly associated with concomitant anticoagulation, renal failure, and platelet <100 × 10⁹/L [Akulian 2022]. BTS and ERS/ESTS statements recommend weighing bleeding risk, pausing anticoagulation when safe, and—if IET is pursued—dose reduced tPA in selected high risk cases, with early surgical input [BTS 2023; ERS/ESTS 2023].
Why the others? (a) Incorrect; risk is not negligible and is linked to anticoagulation. (b) DNase alone lacks supportive RCT evidence. (c) Delaying a week risks harm; if IET is unsafe or fails, early surgery is favored. (e) No evidence supports a target platelet of 300 × 10⁹/L.
Pitfall: Mistaking the IET related red tinge in drainage for hemorrhage; confirm with pleural fluid hematocrit before declaring a bleed [Akulian 2022].
###References###
1.	Akulian J, et al. Bleeding risk with combination IET. Chest. 2022;162:1384–92. https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC9773231/ 
2.	Roberts ME, et al. BTS Pleural Guideline 2023. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1 
3.	Bedawi EO, et al. ERS/ESTS statement 2023. http://erj.ersjournals.com/lookup/doi/10.1183/13993003.01062-2022

### Question  ###
Your hospital has no alteplase for 48 hours. A 63 year old man with a 12F drain continues to have a large multiloculated empyema on ultrasound with persistent sepsis despite antibiotics. What is the best interim intrapleural strategy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Streptokinase monotherapy until alteplase arrives
(b) DNase alone twice daily
(c) Urokinase based fibrinolysis (preferably with DNase), recognizing the evidence is weaker than for tPA/DNase
(d) Wait for alteplase; do nothing more now
(e) Immediately proceed to thoracotomy without further drainage attempts
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (c) is reasonable—urokinase can be used where alteplase is unavailable, ideally combined with DNase, although supportive data are weaker than for tPA/DNase and not tested head to head in RCTs [BTS 2023; ERS/ESTS 2023].
MIST 2 established tPA/DNase as the combination with randomized evidence. Subsequent observational series support reduced dose and alternate scheduling strategies for alteplase but do not change the hierarchy of evidence [Rahman 2011; Popowicz 2017, 2022]. Where alteplase access is constrained, guidelines acknowledge urokinase as a lesser alternative, often paired with DNase; streptokinase is largely abandoned after MIST 1’s negative results and immunogenicity concerns [BTS 2023]. Surgical referral should proceed in parallel if clinical deterioration continues or IET is not feasible.
Why not others? (a) Streptokinase monotherapy lacks benefit in RCTs. (b) DNase alone is inadequate. (d) Waiting risks treatment delay; escalation should not pause entirely for drug availability. (e) Immediate thoracotomy without considering other minimally invasive options may be premature in a hemodynamically stable patient.
Pearl: When tPA is unavailable or contraindicated, urokinase ± DNase is a pragmatic stopgap, but continue surgical planning.
###References###
1.	Roberts ME, et al. BTS Pleural Guideline 2023. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1 
2.	Rahman NM, et al. MIST 2 tPA/DNase. N Engl J Med. 2011;365:518–26. https://www.nejm.org/doi/10.1056/NEJMoa1012740 
3.	Maskell NA, et al. MIST 1 streptokinase. N Engl J Med. 2005;352:865–74. https://www.nejm.org/doi/10.1056/NEJMoa042473

### Question  ###
A 67 year old woman with a loculated empyema has ongoing SIRS and a well positioned 12F drain. She has severe thrombocytopenia (platelets 38 × 10⁹/L) from chemotherapy, so IET is contraindicated. What adjunct can facilitate clearance?
 ### Options### 
(a) Exchange to a 32F chest tube
(b) Instill DNase alone
(c) Add systemic aminoglycoside for better pleural penetration
(d) Daily therapeutic thoracenteses instead of the drain
(e) Large volume intrapleural normal saline irrigation via the existing drain (e.g., 250 mL, TID for 3 days)
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (e)—pleural saline irrigation—improves radiographic clearance and reduces need for surgery versus standard care in a pilot RCT, and is a reasonable option when IET or surgery are unsuitable [Hooper 2015].
In the Pleural Irrigation Trial, adding 250 mL saline by gravity three times daily for 3 days led to greater reduction in effusion volume and fewer surgical referrals compared with standard drain care; while small, it supports irrigation as a rescue strategy [Hooper 2015]. Case series echo potential benefit, including combinations with urokinase where appropriate [Porcel 2017; Guinde 2021].
Why not others? (a) Larger tubes don’t improve core outcomes and increase pain. (b) DNase alone lacks evidence. (c) Aminoglycosides have poor pleural penetration and aren’t recommended for this purpose [BTS 2023]. (d) Iterative thoracentesis is feasible in selected low risk cases but not ideal here given SIRS and thrombocytopenia.
Pearl: Normal saline irrigation is a low tech, evidence supported adjunct when fibrinolytics are contraindicated.
###References###
1.	Hooper CE, et al. Pleural irrigation trial (PIT). Eur Respir J. 2015;46:456–63. https://erj.ersjournals.com/content/46/2/456 
2.	Porcel JM, Valencia H, Bielsa S. Saline flushing ± urokinase case series. Lung. 2017;195:135–8. https://doi.org/10.1007/s00408-016-9964-2 
3.	Roberts ME, et al. BTS 2023. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1
