### Question  ###
A 45 year old man with a loculated right empyema needs a chest tube. He is in severe pain from a prior large bore tube on the left after trauma years ago. Coagulation is normal. What is the preferred drain strategy today?
 ### Options### 
(a) 28–32F chest tube to maximize drainage and speed recovery
(b) ≤14F ultrasound guided catheter with routine saline flushes to maintain patency
(c) 20F tube placed blindly at the safe triangle
(d) Pigtail catheter without imaging to avoid delays
(e) Insert an IPC (indwelling pleural catheter) for temporary drainage
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (b) is correct—small bore (≤14F) drains are associated with less pain and similar outcomes to larger tubes in pleural infection trials; ultrasound guidance and regular saline flushes mitigate occlusion risk [BTS 2023; MIST 1 analysis].
Prospective trial data found no differences in mortality, need for surgery, or length of stay with larger tubes, while small bore drains cause less insertion and in situ pain [BTS 2023]. Ultrasound guidance reduces complications and ensures the largest pocket is targeted [BTS 2023].
Why not others? (a) Larger tubes increase pain without improving key outcomes. (c) Blind placement is avoidable; image guidance is standard. (d) Even pigtails should be ultrasound guided. (e) IPCs are not first line for acute empyema; they may be considered later for chronic infected spaces when surgery/IET are unsuitable.
Pearl: Pain is a meaningful outcome—small bore, ultrasound guided drains improve tolerance without compromising efficacy.
###References###
1.	Roberts ME, et al. BTS 2023. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1 
2.	Chen CH, et al. Ultrasound predictors of drainage success. Ultrasound Med Biol. 2009;35:1468–74. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ultrasmedbio.2009.04.020

### Question  ###
A 52 year old woman with a community acquired, culture pending empyema (RAPID moderate risk) has poor dentition and aspiration risk. What initial empiric antibiotic approach is most appropriate?
 ### Options### 
(a) β lactam/β lactamase inhibitor with anaerobic coverage (e.g., ampicillin sulbactam ± metronidazole), then tailor to cultures
(b) Ciprofloxacin monotherapy
(c) Aminoglycoside monotherapy
(d) Narrow spectrum penicillin alone
(e) Linezolid alone
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (a)—for community acquired pleural infection, guidelines recommend empiric coverage for Gram positive aerobes and anaerobes, typically with a β lactam/β lactamase inhibitor and anaerobic coverage, then narrow when culture data allow [BTS 2023; ERS/ESTS 2023].
Polymicrobial disease and anaerobes are common yet under recovered on routine cultures; maintaining anaerobic cover initially is advised [Hassan 2019; TORPIDS 2022]. Pleural penetration of commonly used β lactams and clindamycin is acceptable based on pharmacokinetic data, whereas aminoglycosides penetrate poorly [BTS 2023].
Why not others? (b) Fluoroquinolone monotherapy lacks adequate anaerobic and streptococcal coverage. (c) Aminoglycosides have poor pleural penetration and limited anaerobic activity. (d) Narrow penicillins miss β lactamase producers and anaerobes. (e) Linezolid targets MRSA/Gram positives only and lacks anaerobic/Gram negative coverage.
Pearl: Continue anaerobic coverage initially even if cultures don’t isolate anaerobes—under detection is common.
###References###
1.	Roberts ME, et al. BTS 2023. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1 
2.	Hassan M, et al. Microbiology of pleural infection in adults: systematic review. Eur Respir J. 2019;54:1900542. http://erj.ersjournals.com/lookup/doi/10.1183/13993003.00542-2019 
3.	Kanellakis NI, et al. TORPIDS metagenomics. Lancet Microbe. 2022;3:e294–302.

### Question  ###
A 69 year old man develops a right empyema 5 days into a complicated ICU admission (ventilated, FiO₂ 0.40). He has recent broad spectrum exposure and a tunneled central line. What empiric hospital acquired regimen is most appropriate while cultures are pending?
 ### Options### 
(a) Amoxicillin clavulanate alone
(b) Ceftriaxone monotherapy
(c) Doxycycline plus metronidazole
(d) Piperacillin–tazobactam plus vancomycin (pseudomonal, anaerobic, and MRSA coverage)
(e) Ertapenem monotherapy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d) is correct—hospital acquired empyema warrants prompt, broad coverage, including Gram negative (often pseudomonal), anaerobic, and MRSA risk coverage, based on local ecology and prior exposures [BTS 2023; Hassan 2019; Kollef 2005].
Nosocomial infections carry worse outcomes, drug resistance, and different microbiology than community acquired cases [BTS 2023; Hassan 2019]. Empiric regimens commonly combine an antipseudomonal β lactam (e.g., piperacillin–tazobactam or cefepime) plus MRSA coverage (vancomycin/linezolid), with anaerobic coverage included or added as needed [BTS 2023].
Why not others? (a) Insufficient Gram negative/MRSA coverage. (b) Ceftriaxone lacks antipseudomonal activity. (c) Doxycycline lacks necessary Gram negative potency. (e) Ertapenem lacks antipseudomonal activity.
Pearl: In hospital acquired empyema, cover Pseudomonas and MRSA up front, then de escalate when susceptibilities return.
###References###
1.	Roberts ME, et al. BTS 2023. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1 
2.	Hassan M, et al. Systematic review of microbiology. Eur Respir J. 2019;54:1900542. 
3.	Kollef MH, et al. Epidemiology/outcomes of healthcare associated pneumonia. Chest. 2005;128:3854–62. https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0012369215496278

### Question  ###
A 57 year old woman with a drained empyema is clinically improved after 6 inpatient days of IV β lactam/β lactamase inhibitor therapy; CRP has fallen by 60% and she’s afebrile. Cultures grew Streptococcus pneumoniae (susceptible). What is the most appropriate antibiotic course plan?
 ### Options### 
(a) Continue IV therapy for 4 full weeks regardless of response
(b) Stop antibiotics now; clinical improvement is sufficient
(c) Switch to oral therapy now and complete a total of ~2–3 weeks, tailoring to response
(d) Continue IV therapy for 14 days, then stop
(e) Fixed 6 week oral course to prevent relapse
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (c)—step down to oral after ~5–7 days IV when clinically improved with >50% CRP fall and source control, aiming for a total of ~2–3 weeks, tailored to the organism and response [BTS 2023].
The optimal duration is not definitively established. Two small randomized trials (ODAPE; SLIM) suggest shorter courses may be non inferior, but both were underpowered; thus, many experts individualize total duration around 2–3 (sometimes up to 4) weeks after adequate drainage, with earlier step down when criteria are met [Porcel 2020; Hassan 2023]. Longer courses may be needed in resistant organisms or inadequate source control [Towe 2021].
Why not others? (a) A blanket 4 week IV course is unnecessary in uncomplicated responders. (b) Stopping now is premature. (d) A rigid IV only 14 days is not required if oral step down criteria are met. (e) Six weeks is rarely required outside special situations (e.g., osteomyelitis, uncontrolled source).
Pearl: Clinical response + CRP trajectory + source control should guide early oral step down and total duration.
###References###
1.	Roberts ME, et al. BTS 2023. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1 
2.	Porcel JM, et al. ODAPE: 2 vs 3 weeks amoxicillin clavulanate. Pleura Peritoneum. 2020;5(1). https://doi.org/10.1515/pp-2019-0027 
3.	Hassan M, et al. SLIM trial. ERJ Open Res. 2023;9:00635 2022. https://doi.org/10.1183/23120541.00635-2022 
________________________________________
### Question  ###
A 63 year old man with dyspnea and fever has a moderate right effusion. Thoracic ultrasound shows multiple thin septations and pleural thickening; bedside pH testing is pending. What single imaging related step best guides immediate management?
 ### Options### 
(a) Use ultrasound to guide placement of a small bore drain into the largest locule
(b) Obtain noncontrast CT first to confirm loculations
(c) PET CT to differentiate malignancy from infection
(d) Contrast CT with 0.5 mm slices to assess the bronchus sign
(e) MRI thorax to quantify fluid viscosity
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (a)—Thoracic ultrasound is the gold standard point of care tool in pleural disease to identify septations/locules and guide safe, targeted drain placement, and is superior to CT for detecting septations [Svigals 2017; BTS 2023].
Ultrasound can detect small volumes (as low as ~5 mL), map locules, and assist safe real time catheter insertion, improving procedural success and reducing complications [Svigals 2017; Heffner 2010]. CT (ideally pleural phase contrast) is helpful later to delineate anatomy or assess tube position/adjacent lung disease, but it should not delay urgent drainage when US already demonstrates a drainable, complex collection. PET CT and MRI have no role in initial management of suspected empyema.
Pearl: When ultrasound shows a drainable, complex collection, proceed to image guided drainage rather than waiting for CT.
###References###
1.	Svigals PZ, et al. Accuracy of pleural US for complicated effusions. Thorax. 2017;72:94–5. https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5563972/ 
2.	Heffner JE, et al. Imaging in pleural infection. Chest. 2010;137:467–79. 
3.	Roberts ME, et al. BTS 2023. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/Suppl_3/s1

### Question  ###
A 59 year old woman with RAPID moderate risk empyema remains febrile after 48 hours of antibiotics and drainage, and after two doses of tPA/DNase her CT (venous pleural phase, 1.25 mm) shows persistent multiloculations and trapped lung. She is hemodynamically stable. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Switch to DNase monotherapy
(b) Place a second 32F chest tube
(c) Continue tPA/DNase for four more days regardless of response
(d) Proceed to early VATS debridement/decortication with surgical team involvement now
(e) Stop all interventions and re evaluate in 72 hours
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d)—early VATS is appropriate after failed medical therapy with persistent loculated collection and trapped lung.
Meta analysis suggests VATS decortication achieves high success with low failure rates in empyema compared to open thoracotomy [Pan 2017]. The MIST 3 feasibility RCT demonstrated practical randomization to early VATS vs IET; while it did not show clear outcome differences due to compliance/size, it supports early escalation when medical therapy is failing [Bedawi 2023]. Expert guidance (AATS; BTS/ERS ESTS) favors surgical referral when adequate drainage/IET do not control infection or when lung is trapped [Shen 2017; BTS 2023].
Why not others? (a) DNase alone lacks evidence. (b) Upsizing/adding large tubes doesn’t address multi loculation or fibrous peel. (c) Persisting with IET despite objective failure risks delay. (e) Watchful waiting is contraindicated with ongoing sepsis and residual collections.
Pearl: Escalate promptly to surgery when objective failure criteria are met—don’t repeat ineffective medical steps.
###References###
1.	Pan H, et al. VATS vs open decortication meta analysis. J Thorac Dis. 2017;9:2006–14. https://jtd.amegroups.org/article/view/12936/html 
2.	Bedawi EO, et al. MIST 3 feasibility RCT. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2023;208:1305–15. 
3.	Shen KR, et al. AATS empyema guidelines. J Thorac Cardiovasc Surg. 2017;153:e129–46. https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0022522317301526

### Question  ###
A 68 year old man with a 3 week history of pneumonia presents with empyema; pleural culture shows a Gram negative rod. He is scheduled for VATS decortication. Which preoperative factor is most strongly associated with conversion to thoracotomy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Stage II empyema
(b) Gram negative organism isolated from pleural infection
(c) Onset of symptoms <7 days
(d) Younger age (<50 years)
(e) Left sided effusion
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (b)—Gram negative infection is a predictor of conversion from VATS to thoracotomy; delayed referral/longer symptom duration also increases conversion risk [Lardinois 2005].
In a series examining predictors of conversion during VATS for empyema, Gram negative infection and delayed presentation significantly increased the likelihood of conversion [Lardinois 2005]. Other studies confirm the influence of disease stage and timing on conversion and outcomes, with more advanced disease prompting higher conversion rates [Jagelavicius 2017; Stefani 2013].
Why not others? (a) Stage II disease is generally favorable for successful VATS. (c) Short symptom duration is associated with lower, not higher, conversion risk. (d) Younger age is not a recognized risk factor. (e) Laterality is not a key determinant.
Pearl: Identify Gram negative infection and prolonged symptom duration preoperatively—they signal a higher conversion risk and inform consent and planning.
###References###
1.	Lardinois D, et al. Predictors of conversion in VATS for empyema. Ann Thorac Surg. 2005;79:1851–6. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.athoracsur.2004.12.032 
2.	Jagelavicius Z, et al. Preoperative predictors of conversion. Eur J Cardiothorac Surg. 2017;52:70–5. 
3.	Stefani A, et al. Predictors of successful surgical treatment. Ann Thorac Surg. 2013;96:1812–9.

### Question  ###
A 64 year old woman presents 5 weeks after pneumonia with dyspnea and a chronic, organized empyema (thick peel on CT; venous pleural phase, 1 mm slices). She is scheduled for VATS decortication. Which postoperative outcome is more likely given the prolonged disease duration?
 ### Options### 
(a) Lower transfusion requirements
(b) Shorter length of stay
(c) Shorter chest tube duration
(d) Lower risk of air leak
(e) Longer chest tube duration and hospital stay
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (e) is correct—longer symptom duration (>4 weeks) predicts longer chest tube duration (often due to air leak) and longer length of stay, and is associated with increased conversion risk [Chung 2014; Jagelavicius 2017].
Observational data indicate that delayed intervention is linked to more organized pleural rind and fissural adhesions, complicating decortication and increasing postoperative morbidity markers such as air leak, chest tube days, and LOS [Chung 2014]. These patients may also have higher conversion rates to thoracotomy [Jagelavicius 2017].
Why not others? Prolonged disease does not reduce bleeding or air leak risk and does not shorten LOS or tube days.
Pearl: Earlier surgical referral in non responders can reduce postoperative air leak related morbidity and LOS.
###References###
1.	Chung JH, et al. Optimal timing of thoracoscopic drainage/decortication. Ann Thorac Surg. 2014;97:224–9. 
2.	Jagelavicius Z, et al. Predictors of conversion. Eur J Cardiothorac Surg. 2017;52:70–5.

### Question  ###
A 39 year old man from a TB endemic region presents with chronic cough, weight loss, and right sided stage III tuberculous empyema. Ultrasound shows a thickened pleural rind; CT (1.0 mm) confirms trapped lung. He’s optimized on anti TB therapy. What is the most appropriate intervention?
 ### Options### 
(a) Open thoracotomy and planned thoracoplasty
(b) Streptokinase monotherapy via chest tube
(c) VATS decortication by an experienced team, with readiness to convert if needed
(d) No surgery; rely on prolonged antibiotics alone
(e) IPC placement and watchful waiting
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (c)—VATS decortication for selected stage III TB empyema is feasible and safe, with shorter blood loss and recovery versus open surgery, though conversion may be required; surgery complements anti TB therapy in organized disease [Kumar 2017; Yao 2023; Sun 2022].
Multiple series show thoracoscopic decortication (including uniportal) can achieve adequate re expansion with acceptable complication and re intervention rates in TB empyema, acknowledging that operative time and blood loss may be higher than in pyogenic disease and conversion may be required [Kumar 2017; Sun 2022]. Drug resistant TB empyema often entails longer operating times and higher complications than drug sensitive cases [Yao 2023].
Why not others? (a) Routine thoracoplasty is increasingly avoided due to morbidity, reserved for salvage. (b) Streptokinase monotherapy lacks evidence and is immunogenic. (d) In organized TB empyema, antibiotics alone are usually insufficient for re expansion. (e) IPC alone is not standard care for active TB empyema with trapped lung.
Pearl: In TB empyema with a thick pleural rind, surgery plus anti TB therapy is often necessary for durable recovery.
###References###
1.	Kumar A, et al. Thoracoscopic decortication of stage III TB empyema. Ann Thorac Surg. 2017;104:1688–94. 
2.	Yao L, et al. Decortication in drug resistant TB empyema. Interdiscip Cardiovasc Thorac Surg. 2023;37:ivad166. 
3.	Sun W, et al. Uniportal VATS for stage II–III TB empyema. BMC Pulm Med. 2022;22:398.

### Question  ###
A 72 year old woman with COPD, cachexia, and a chronic post lobectomy empyema with bronchopleural fistula (BPF) has recurrent sepsis despite prolonged antibiotics and drainage. She is a poor candidate for decortication or flap closure. What is the most appropriate next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Create an open thoracic window (Eloesser/Clagett) with ongoing cavity care, with VAC as feasible
(b) Prolonged DNase monotherapy via existing drain
(c) Attempt intrapleural streptokinase to close the BPF
(d) Proceed to full thoracoplasty as first step
(e) Remove the drain and monitor
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (a)—for medically unfit patients with chronic empyema ± BPF, open thoracic window (OTW) provides durable source control and allows cavity care; VAC systems can reduce dressing burden in modern practice [Regnard 2000; Reyes 2010; Hofmann 2012].
Series describe OTW (Eloesser/Clagett variants) for refractory empyema/BPF after resection, often followed by delayed closure or flap transposition when feasible; survival is acceptable but morbidity is high, so patient selection is key [Regnard 2000; Reyes 2010]. VAC therapy can be applied through the window or even without formal rib resection in selected cases, facilitating granulation and infection control while reducing nursing demands [Hofmann 2012].
Why not others? (b) DNase alone lacks evidence in chronic spaces. (c) Streptokinase is not indicated here. (d) Thoracoplasty is a salvage option for large residual spaces when other methods fail; not first line. (e) Removing drainage risks uncontrolled sepsis.
Pearl: OTW remains relevant for non operative candidates with chronic infected spaces and/or BPF.
###References###
1.	Regnard JF, et al. OTW followed by intrathoracic flap transposition. J Thorac Cardiovasc Surg. 2000;120:270–5. 
2.	Reyes KG, et al. Open window thoracostomy: modern update. Thorac Cardiovasc Surg. 2010;58:220–4. 
3.	Hofmann HS, et al. VAC of pleural empyema without classic OTW. Ann Thorac Surg. 2012;93:1741–2.

### Question  ###
During VATS decortication for a chronic empyema in a 61 year old man, the visceral pleura is denuded over broad areas and the lung is air leaky after freeing fissures. Which intraoperative adjunct best reduces postoperative air leak duration, chest tube days, and LOS?
 ### Options### 
(a) Broad pleural abrasion
(b) Routine pleurodesis with talc
(c) Routine application of fibrin glue over all surfaces
(d) Autologous blood patch insufflation
(e) Apply polyglycolic acid patches to the lung surface after decortication
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (e)—polyglycolic acid (PGA) patches applied to decorticated lung surfaces can significantly shorten air leak cessation time, chest tube duration, and LOS compared with no patches after decortication [Haberal 2022].
In a comparative series of decortication patients, PGA patches reduced median time to air leak cessation by nearly half and shortened drainage duration and hospital stay [Haberal 2022]. While data are observational, technique is low risk and biologically plausible in preventing alveolar air egress through friable, decorticated surfaces.
Why not others? (a) Abrasion is not targeted to air leak control and may add bleeding. (b) Routine talc pleurodesis is not standard in infected pleural spaces. (c) Fibrin sealants may help focal leaks but lack comparative decortication specific data. (d) Autologous blood patch is used for postoperative air leaks but not as a primary intraoperative strategy across broad surfaces.
Pearl: After decortication, PGA patches are a pragmatic adjunct when air leak risk is high.
###References###
1.	Haberal MA, et al. Polyglycolic acid patch after decortication. ANZ J Surg. 2022;92:1845–9.

### Question  ###
A 66 year old man with diabetes and recent chemotherapy develops Candida empyema. He is febrile, hypotensive (MAP 62 mm Hg on norepinephrine), and mechanically ventilated (FiO₂ 0.5). A drain is in place. What is the best immediate management strategy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Narrow Gram positive antibiotics; most fungal isolates are contaminants
(b) Start an antifungal with good pleural penetration (e.g., liposomal amphotericin B) and arrange early surgical source control
(c) Observe on current therapy; fungal culture likely colonization
(d) Stop drainage to prevent fungal dissemination
(e) Switch to aminoglycosides for synergy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (b)—fungal empyema (often Candida, Aspergillus) is associated with high mortality, prolonged ICU stays/ventilation, and warrants prompt antifungal therapy with agents that achieve therapeutic pleural levels (e.g., liposomal amphotericin B) and aggressive source control, frequently surgical [Cheng 2023; Iqbal 2024; Moriyama 2010].
Series comparing fungal vs bacterial empyema show markedly worse outcomes for fungal cases, including longer ventilation, LOS, and lower 1 year survival [Cheng 2023]. Contemporary reports emphasize initiating appropriate antifungals promptly in high risk hosts with positive pleural cultures [Iqbal 2024]. Pharmacokinetic data demonstrate liposomal amphotericin B attains bactericidal/ fungicidal concentrations in pleural fluid [Moriyama 2010].
Why not others? (a), (c) Fungal isolates from the pleural space in this context are pathogenic, not contaminants. (d) Stopping drainage is dangerous; source control is essential. (e) Aminoglycosides are irrelevant to fungal disease and poorly penetrate pleura.
Pearl: In suspected/confirmed fungal empyema, act fast—antifungals plus source control.
###References###
1.	Cheng YF, et al. VATS decortication outcomes: fungal vs bacterial empyema. BMC Infect Dis. 2023;23:8. https://bmcinfectdis.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12879-022-07969-0 
2.	Iqbal N, et al. Fungal empyema thoracis: case series. Ther Adv Infect Dis. 2024;11:20499361231223887. 
3.	Moriyama B, et al. Pharmacokinetics of liposomal amphotericin B in pleural fluid. Antimicrob Agents Chemother. 2010;54:1633–5. https://doi.org/10.1128/AAC.01438-09

### Question  ###
A 78 year old man with severe heart failure and advanced frailty has a chronic, infected pleural space with non expandable lung after multiple admissions. He is not a candidate for decortication/IET. He wishes to avoid prolonged hospitalization. What is the most appropriate management?
 ### Options### 
(a) Place a 28F tube and keep hospitalized for 4–6 weeks
(b) Empiric streptokinase to dissolve adhesions
(c) Serial outpatient thoracenteses weekly
(d) Indwelling tunneled pleural catheter for ambulatory drainage with prolonged oral antibiotics and close follow up
(e) Stop all interventions and observe
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d)—in select patients with chronic pleural infection and non expandable lung who are not surgical/IET candidates, an indwelling pleural catheter (IPC) with prolonged antibiotics can palliate, facilitate ambulatory care, and provide source control [Davies 2008; Saqib 2017].
While IPCs are standard for malignant effusions, limited evidence suggests a role in chronic infected spaces when conventional options are exhausted or unsafe. Case series describe symptom control and outpatient management using IPCs with prolonged antibiotics; careful monitoring for blockage and infection is essential [Davies 2008; Saqib 2017]. This approach aligns with patient centered goals in frail individuals.
Why not others? (a) Large bore inpatient drainage for weeks is burdensome and misaligned with goals. (b) Streptokinase is obsolete. (c) Repeated thoracenteses pose procedural risk and do not offer continuous source control. (e) Observation alone risks recurrent sepsis.
Pearl: In non expandable lung with chronic infection and limited options, IPC + prolonged antibiotics can provide pragmatic ambulatory source control.
###References###
1.	Davies HE, et al. Use of IPCs for chronic pleural infection. Chest. 2008;133:546–9. https://journal.chestnet.org/article/S0012-3692(15)49106-8/abstract 
2.	Saqib I UD, et al. Ambulatory management using small bore IPCs. Cureus. 2017;9(9):e1636. https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5665692/ 
### Question  ###
A 72 year old man with ischemic cardiomyopathy (LVEF 25%), long standing hypertension, and CKD 3 presents with progressive dyspnea and orthopnea despite intensified diuresis (furosemide 80 mg twice daily plus spironolactone). CXR shows a large right pleural effusion with mild left sided blunting. He denies fever or chest pain. Diagnostic thoracentesis removes 1.2 L of clear yellow fluid: protein 3.2 g/dL (serum 6.5), LDH 180 U/L (serum 260; lab ULN 200), pH 7.47, glucose 95 mg/dL, and predominantly lymphocytic cells. By Light’s criteria the effusion classifies as exudative. ECG and serial troponins are unchanged from baseline; CT chest with contrast shows no pleural nodularity or mass. He remains symptomatic but hemodynamically stable. To adjudicate etiology and guide management (diuretic escalation vs oncologic/infectious work up), which additional test or interpretive approach best supports a cardiac transudative mechanism in this diuretic treated patient?
 ### Options### 
(a) Pleural fluid cholesterol >55 mg/dL
(b) Pleural fluid NT proBNP >1,500 pg/mL
(c) Serum–pleural fluid albumin gradient >1.2 g/dL
(d) Repeating cytology on two additional specimens
(e) Pleural fluid pH >7.40
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice C correct. In diuretic treated heart failure, Light’s criteria frequently misclassify “pseudoexudates.” The serum–effusion albumin gradient >1.2 g/dL (Roth) reclassifies many such effusions as transudative and better aligns with clinical context, preventing unnecessary malignant/infectious work ups and focusing therapy on heart failure optimization. This gradient is particularly useful when pleural fluid pH, glucose, and imaging are noninflammatory and when diuresis has been intensified. Pearl: When Light’s criteria and bedside probability conflict in a diuretic treated patient, check the albumin gradient before labeling the effusion an exudate.
Why others are wrong: (a) High pleural cholesterol thresholds help identify exudates, not transudates; using >55 mg/dL would misdirect management. (b) NT proBNP can support a cardiac cause, but pleural measurement adds no advantage over serum and is not the preferred adjudicator when Light’s criteria are discordant. (d) Additional cytology increases yield for malignancy only when suspicion is high; it does not settle transudate vs exudate. (e) A higher pH is compatible with transudates but is insufficient alone to reclassify a pseudoexudate. Wilcox’s Rational Clinical Examination review and a recent practical review emphasize pseudoexudates and the primacy of the albumin (or, secondarily, protein) gradients in this scenario.
###References###
1.	Roth BJ, O’Meara TF, Cragun WH. The serum effusion albumin gradient in the evaluation of pleural effusions. Chest. 1990;98:546–9.
2.	Wilcox ME, et al. Does this patient have an exudative pleural effusion? JAMA. 2014;311:2422–31.
3.	Mohan G, et al. A practical approach to pseudoexudative pleural effusions. Respir Med. 2023;214:107279.
4.	Light RW, et al. Pleural effusions: the diagnostic separation of transudates and exudates. Ann Intern Med. 1972;77:507–13. https://doi.org/10.7326/0003-4819-77-4-507

### Question  ###
A 68 year old retired shipyard mechanic (40 pack years; remote asbestos exposure) has weight loss, pleuritic right sided pain, and a moderate right effusion that rapidly reaccumulates. Thoracentesis shows an exudate with lymphocyte predominance; cytology is negative. Contrast chest CT reconstructed at 1.25 mm slices reveals circumferential, nodular pleural thickening with mediastinal pleural involvement and a “rind” encasing the lung; no resectable parenchymal mass is seen. He is on rivaroxaban for atrial fibrillation (held 48 h). Oxygen saturation is 94% on room air. He seeks both a diagnosis and symptom control and asks about “a scan to tell if it’s cancer.” What is the most appropriate next diagnostic step to secure tissue and plan management?
 ### Options### 
(a) Repeat thoracentesis submitting a larger cytology volume
(b) PET CT–targeted pleural biopsy instead of any other modality
(c) Blind closed pleural biopsy (Abrams needle)
(d) CT guided biopsy of a 4 mm subpleural lung micronodule
(e) Medical thoracoscopy with targeted pleural biopsies under direct vision
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice E correct. With negative cytology but high probability imaging for pleural malignancy (including mesothelioma), medical thoracoscopy offers direct visualization with high diagnostic yield for malignancy and allows same session therapeutic decisions (e.g., pleurodesis or tunneled catheter). Large series and guidelines consistently support thoracoscopy when imaging is compelling yet cytology is nondiagnostic. Pearl: Negative pleural cytology—especially with mesothelioma pattern CT—should trigger thoracoscopic biopsy rather than repeated taps.
Why others are wrong: (a) Increasing cytology volume (≥50 mL is usually adequate) has limited incremental yield in this setting and is notably poor for mesothelioma. (b) PET CT may aid staging, but randomized data do not show superiority of PET targeted over CT guided biopsy after a nondiagnostic attempt; PET can be confounded by prior talc pleurodesis or inflammation. (c) Blind closed biopsy has substantially lower yield for malignancy compared with image guided or thoracoscopic techniques. (d) Sampling a tiny parenchymal micronodule risks missing pleural disease and will not address effusion control.
###References###
1.	Rahman NM, et al. Local anaesthetic thoracoscopy: BTS Pleural Disease Guideline 2010. Thorax. 2010;65(Suppl 2):ii54–60.
2.	Harris RJ, et al. The impact of thoracoscopy on the management of pleural disease. Chest. 1995;107:845–52.
3.	Arnold DT, et al. Investigating unilateral pleural effusions: the role of cytology. Eur Respir J. 2018;52:1801254. https://erj.ersjournals.com/content/52/5/1801254
4.	de Fonseka D, et al. PET CT guided vs CT guided biopsy in suspected malignant pleural thickening: randomized trial. Eur Respir J. 2024;63:2301295. https://erj.ersjournals.com/content/63/2/2301295

### Question  ###
A 55 year old woman with metastatic risk breast cancer history presents with a new moderate left pleural effusion and exertional dyspnea. She takes an aromatase inhibitor and apixaban (held for procedure). Ultrasound shows a free flowing anechoic effusion without septations. You plan a first diagnostic thoracentesis and want to maximize pleural fluid cytology yield and create a cell block suitable for immunohistochemistry and reflex molecular testing if positive. The lab can process only one cytology container today. Aside from careful handling and prompt fixation, what minimum volume should you ensure is submitted from this first thoracentesis to optimize diagnostic adequacy?
 ### Options### 
(a) 50 mL
(b) 10 mL
(c) 20 mL
(d) 100 mL
(e) 200 mL
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice A correct. Prospective data show that submitting ≥50 mL pleural fluid improves cytology adequacy and reliably enables a cell block; higher volumes do not consistently increase sensitivity and may delay processing. In practice, the first, well processed specimen provides the largest step up in diagnostic yield; if cytology is negative but suspicion persists, proceed to image guided or thoracoscopic biopsy rather than serially chasing larger volumes. Pearl: Send at least 50 mL from the first thoracentesis to secure a cell block; volume beyond this threshold has diminishing returns.
Why others are wrong: (b) 10 mL risks a false negative from scant cellularity. (c) 20 mL is better than 10 mL but still suboptimal for consistent cell block creation. (d) 100 mL is often unnecessary and can slow fixation without clear incremental benefit. (e) 200 mL likewise adds logistic burden without proven gain in first sample sensitivity. Evidence from modern series (and tumor type–specific data) supports moving to tissue biopsy when cytology is negative but clinical probability remains high.
###References###
1.	Abouzgheib W, et al. Volume of pleural fluid required for accurate diagnosis of malignant pleural effusion. Chest. 2009;135:999–1001.
2.	Arnold DT, et al. Investigating unilateral pleural effusions: the role of cytology. Eur Respir J. 2018;52:1801254. https://erj.ersjournals.com/content/52/5/1801254
3.	Garcia LW, et al. The value of multiple fluid specimens in the cytological diagnosis of malignancy. Mod Pathol. 1994;7:665–8.

### Question  ###
A 64 year old woman (20 pack years, quit 15 years ago) has a unilateral right pleural effusion and 10 lb weight loss. She is afebrile with mild pleuritic pain. Ultrasound at the bedside shows a moderate effusion with areas of pleural thickening; on sweeping the diaphragm, you identify several 6–8 mm hypoechoic nodules over the dome. Pleural fluid later returns as an exudate with lymphocyte predominance; pH and glucose are normal. Contrast CT (1.5 mm reconstructions) confirms subtle pleural thickening but no lung mass. Before scheduling tissue sampling, you want to risk stratify ultrasound findings. Which sonographic feature most strongly suggests malignant pleural involvement and should direct targeted biopsy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Anechoic, free flowing effusion without septations
(b) Pleural or diaphragmatic nodularity
(c) Fine internal septations alone
(d) Dynamic swirling of echoes (“plankton sign”)
(e) Bilateral symmetric effusions
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice B correct. Pleural or diaphragmatic nodularity on thoracic ultrasound is a specific predictor of malignant pleural disease and should prompt targeted sampling of the abnormal pleura. Qureshi and colleagues demonstrated that pleural/diaphragmatic nodularity and pleural thickening have high specificity for malignancy; guidelines endorse using these findings to guide biopsy rather than relying on cytology alone. Pearl: Ultrasound visible pleural or diaphragmatic nodules are a “biopsy me here” sign.
Why others are wrong: (a) A simple, anechoic effusion is nonspecific and can be transudative or exudative. (c) Fine septations occur in both benign and malignant exudates and do not, by themselves, point to cancer. (d) Dynamic swirling reflects suspended cells/debris but lacks specificity for malignancy. (e) Bilateral symmetry favors systemic causes (e.g., heart failure), not focal pleural malignancy, and is a distribution pattern rather than a sonographic sign of malignant pleural involvement. When US suggests malignancy, pair it with image guided pleural biopsy or medical thoracoscopy for highest yield.
###References###
1.	Qureshi NR, Rahman NM, Gleeson FV. Thoracic ultrasound in the diagnosis of malignant pleural effusion. Thorax. 2009;64:139–43. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/64/2/139
2.	Roberts ME, et al. British Thoracic Society Guideline for pleural disease. Thorax. 2023;78:1143–56. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/11/1143
3.	Hallifax RJ, et al. Role of CT in assessing pleural malignancy prior to thoracoscopy. Thorax. 2015;70:192–3.

### Question  ###
A 70 year old man with a recurrent unilateral left pleural effusion underwent a nondiagnostic CT guided pleural biopsy 4 months ago despite targeting thickened parietal pleura. He now has progressive dyspnea and persistent circumferential pleural thickening on thin slice CT. He had talc pleurodesis 2 years ago after spontaneous pneumothorax. Oncology asks whether PET CT guided biopsy would improve the diagnostic yield for suspected pleural malignancy on the second attempt. He can tolerate local anesthesia and outpatient procedures. Which statement is most accurate to guide your plan?
 ### Options### 
(a) PET CT–targeted pleural biopsy improves yield over CT guided biopsy in this setting
(b) Whole body PET CT has near perfect diagnostic accuracy for malignant effusion
(c) A negative PET CT reliably excludes pleural malignancy
(d) In a randomized trial, PET CT–guided pleural biopsy did not improve diagnostic yield over CT guidance
(e) PET CT should be used routinely to select indwelling pleural catheter vs pleurodesis
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice D correct. The TARGET randomized trial showed no improvement in diagnostic yield when PET CT guided the second pleural biopsy after a nondiagnostic prior biopsy, compared with standard CT guided targeting. PET CT remains valuable for staging or problem solving but is not a routine biopsy guidance tool. Pitfall: Prior talc pleurodesis and pleural inflammation can cause intense FDG uptake, leading to false positive PET signals and suboptimal targets.
Why others are wrong: (a) Directly contradicted by randomized data. (b) PET CT’s sensitivity/specificity are imperfect; it cannot “rule in” or “rule out” pleural malignancy with certainty and should not replace histology. (c) A negative PET CT does not reliably exclude pleural malignancy; CT and clinical context still drive biopsy. (e) PET CT is not used to choose drainage strategy (IPC vs pleurodesis); that decision rests on lung expandability, expected survival, patient preference, and oncologic plan. Contemporary guidelines recommend PET CT selectively—e.g., when histology is negative yet suspicion persists or when biopsy is not feasible.
###References###
1.	de Fonseka D, et al. PET CT guided vs CT guided biopsy in suspected malignant pleural thickening: randomized trial. Eur Respir J. 2024;63:2301295. https://erj.ersjournals.com/content/63/2/2301295
2.	Roberts ME, et al. British Thoracic Society Guideline for pleural disease. Thorax. 2023;78:1143–56. https://thorax.bmj.com/content/78/11/1143
3.	Reuter S, et al. CT in discriminating malignant from non malignant unresolved unilateral pleural effusions: systematic review. Eur Clin Respir J. 2019;6:1565803.

### Question  ###
A 65 year old woman with a symptomatic right pleural effusion has ultrasound evidence of a 1 cm hypoechoic pleural rind over the posterolateral chest wall, with a safe intercostal window. She is a never smoker, ECOG 1, and wants a tissue diagnosis and drainage in one visit if possible. CT shows no discrete lung mass; PET CT has not been done. You are deciding how to obtain tissue. Which approach best balances high diagnostic yield, low complication rate, no ionizing radiation, and the ability to place a drain in the same session if warranted?
 ### Options### 
(a) Ultrasound guided pleural biopsy
(b) CT guided pleural biopsy
(c) Blind closed (Abrams) pleural biopsy
(d) Repeat large volume thoracentesis for cytology alone
(e) Routine first line medical thoracoscopy for all such cases
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice A correct. When a pleural target is clearly sonographically visible, ultrasound guided pleural biopsy offers excellent yield with low complications, is efficient at the bedside, and allows same session drain or IPC placement. A large meta analysis (Mei) and contemporary series show high diagnostic adequacy for malignancy with fewer complications than CT guidance. Pearl: If you can see it well on ultrasound, biopsy it under ultrasound—faster, safer, and no radiation.
Why others are wrong: (b) CT guided biopsy has slightly higher pooled yield in some analyses but higher complication rates and lacks bedside flexibility; use when the target is not well seen on ultrasound. (c) Blind closed biopsy is inferior for malignancy and largely supplanted by image guided or thoracoscopic techniques. (d) Cytology alone misses many malignant effusions—especially mesothelioma—and delays definitive management. (e) Thoracoscopy has high sensitivity and therapeutic advantages but is not required first line when a high probability, ultrasound visible target is present and a minimally invasive outpatient biopsy is feasible.
###References###
1.	Mei F, et al. Diagnostic yield and safety of image guided pleural biopsy: systematic review and meta analysis. Respiration. 2021;100:77–87.
2.	Mychajlowycz M, et al. Ultrasound  vs CT guided subpleural lung and pleural biopsy. Can Assoc Radiol J. 2021;72:883–9.
3.	Sconfienza LM, et al. Pleural and peripheral lung lesions: comparison of US  and CT guided biopsy. Radiology. 2013;266:930–5.

### Question  ###
In a resource limited hospital, a 58 year old man with HIV and new unilateral effusion undergoes thoracentesis in the ED overnight; bloodwork will not return until the next day. Pleural studies show protein 3.8 g/dL, LDH 420 U/L (lab ULN 200), and lymphocyte predominance; pH and glucose are normal. He is afebrile and stable; CXR suggests a moderate free flowing effusion. You must make provisional decisions (admit vs outpatient, antibiotic coverage) before serum results arrive. Which pleural fluid–only classification rule is a validated alternative to Light’s criteria for identifying an exudate when serum tests are unavailable?
 ### Options### 
(a) Glucose <60 mg/dL plus pleural RBC >100,000/mm³
(b) Triglycerides elevated with low cholesterol
(c) Serum–pleural albumin gradient >1.2 g/dL
(d) pH <7.20 plus WBC >10,000/mm³
(e) Pleural cholesterol >40 mg/dL and pleural LDH >0.6× the laboratory upper limit of normal
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice E correct. When serum values are not available, a two analyte pleural fluid rule—cholesterol >40 mg/dL plus LDH >0.6× ULN—performs comparably to Light’s criteria for identifying exudates (Lépine), building on prior work showing cholesterol’s utility (Valdés). This bedside alternative enables timely disposition and empiric decisions while awaiting serum. Pearl: Use the lab’s own LDH ULN for the 0.6× cutoff; this keeps the rule portable across institutions.
Why others are wrong: (a) Low glucose and high RBC suggest specific processes (complicated infection/empyema or hemothorax/malignancy) but are not validated classification rules. (b) Features of chylous effusions do not classify exudate vs transudate per se. (c) The albumin gradient requires a serum value. (d) Low pH and high WBC point to complicated parapneumonic infection but are not a validated exudate rule. Systematic review data (Wilcox) confirm that Light’s criteria are standard, but cholesterol LDH rules are reasonable substitutes when serum is unavailable.
###References###
1.	Lépine PA, et al. Simplified criteria using pleural cholesterol and LDH to distinguish exudates from transudates. Respiration. 2019;98:48–54.
2.	Valdés L, et al. Cholesterol: a useful parameter for distinguishing exudates from transudates. Chest. 1991;99:1097–102.
3.	Wilcox ME, et al. Does this patient have an exudative pleural effusion? JAMA. 2014;311:2422–31.

### Question  ###
A 66 year old man with chronic kidney disease and alcoholic cardiomyopathy is admitted for volume overload and receives aggressive IV diuresis. He has a moderate right pleural effusion; bedside ultrasound shows anechoic, free flowing fluid. Initial pleural studies (obtained during diuresis) meet Light’s criteria for an exudate, but he has no fever, pleuritic pain, or leukocytosis. You are asked to interpret Light’s criteria for the team and advise whether further invasive work up is indicated. Which statement best characterizes the diagnostic performance of Light’s criteria and informs next steps?
 ### Options### 
(a) Specificity exceeds 95%, so exudate calls are almost never wrong
(b) The negative likelihood ratio is high, so a negative result is uninformative
(c) Light’s criteria are less sensitive than the albumin gradient
(d) Light’s criteria are highly sensitive with a low negative likelihood ratio, but only moderate specificity—so diuretic treated heart failure effusions are often misclassified as exudates
(e) Light’s criteria have been superseded by ultrasound scores and are no longer recommended
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice D correct. Light’s criteria remain the most sensitive biochemical method to detect exudates and have a low negative likelihood ratio, so failure to meet the criteria argues strongly against an exudate. However, specificity is only moderate; diuretic treated heart failure effusions are frequently misclassified as exudates (“pseudoexudates”). In that scenario, albumin (or protein) gradients help reclassify and align management with the clinical picture. Pitfall: Calling an exudate purely on Light’s criteria in a recently diuresed patient can lead to unnecessary invasive testing.
Why others are wrong: (a) Specificity is not >95%; transudates are misclassified at meaningful rates. (b) The negative LR is low, so a negative result is informative. (c) Light’s criteria are more sensitive than albumin gradient approaches for exudates. (e) Ultrasound scores (e.g., DUETS) are promising but remain adjuncts; Light’s criteria are still the standard biochemical approach in guidelines.
###References###
1.	Light RW, et al. Pleural effusions: the diagnostic separation of transudates and exudates. Ann Intern Med. 1972;77:507–13. https://doi.org/10.7326/0003-4819-77-4-507
2.	Porcel JM. Pearls and myths in pleural fluid analysis. Respirology. 2011;16:44–52.
3.	Romero S, et al. Evaluation of different criteria for separation of pleural transudates from exudates. Chest. 1993;104:399–404.
4.	Mohan G, et al. A practical approach to pseudoexudative pleural effusions. Respir Med. 2023;214:107279.

### Question  ###
A 61 year old woman with an undiagnosed unilateral exudative effusion undergoes medical thoracoscopy after two nondiagnostic taps. Multiple biopsies of inflamed parietal pleura show nonspecific pleuritis (NSP) without granulomas or malignancy. Cultures are negative. She feels better after drainage; lung fully re expands; CT shows no mass or suspicious nodularity. She is active (ECOG 0–1) and prefers to avoid more invasive procedures unless necessary. What is the most appropriate management strategy now?
 ### Options### 
(a) Empiric corticosteroids for presumed inflammatory pleuritis
(b) Close clinical and radiographic follow up, with re evaluation if features evolve or the effusion recurs
(c) Immediate repeat thoracoscopy within 4 weeks
(d) Start empiric antituberculous therapy
(e) Routine talc pleurodesis to prevent recurrence
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice B correct. After thoracoscopic biopsies yielding nonspecific pleuritis, most patients follow a benign course with spontaneous resolution; a minority (≈8–11% across cohorts) later manifest malignancy—often mesothelioma—over months. Therefore, the appropriate course is structured follow up (clinical review and imaging), reserving repeat invasive evaluation for recurrence, new suspicious radiology, or symptom re emergence. This strategy avoids overtreatment while remaining vigilant for delayed diagnoses. Pearl: NSP ≠ diagnosis of exclusion forever—watchful follow up is part of the diagnostic plan.
Why others are wrong: (a) Empiric steroids lack evidence and risk masking evolving disease. (c) Routine early re thoracoscopy is unnecessary in stable patients without new red flags. (d) Empiric antituberculous therapy is not indicated without supportive clinical, biochemical, or histologic evidence. (e) Talc pleurodesis should be individualized to symptom burden and recurrence, not performed routinely when the diagnosis is uncertain and the lung currently expands.
###References###
1.	Venekamp LN, et al. Natural history of nonspecific pleuritis diagnosed after thoracoscopy. Respiration. 2005;72:74–8.
2.	Davies HE, et al. Outcome of patients with nonspecific pleuritis/fibrosis on thoracoscopic pleural biopsies. Eur J Cardiothorac Surg. 2010;38:472–7.
3.	Ferrer JS, et al. Evolution of idiopathic pleural effusion: long term follow up. Chest. 1996;109:1508–13.

### Question  ###
A 63 year old woman with a history of ovarian carcinoma presents with a new unilateral right pleural effusion causing dyspnea on exertion. She completed platinum based chemotherapy 18 months ago. CXR and ultrasound confirm a free flowing moderate effusion; CT shows no obvious pleural nodularity. You plan an initial diagnostic thoracentesis and want to counsel her on the likelihood that pleural fluid cytology will establish the diagnosis at the first attempt versus the need for pleural biopsy or thoracoscopy. Based on tumor type–specific data, which primary cancer is most likely to be detected on initial pleural cytology?
 ### Options### 
(a) Lung adenocarcinoma
(b) Lymphoma
(c) Ovarian carcinoma
(d) Pleural mesothelioma
(e) Small cell lung carcinoma
###Discussion###
Bottom line — choice C correct. The sensitivity of initial pleural cytology varies by tumor type. Modern series show ovarian carcinoma among the highest first sample cytology yields, often exceeding that of lung adenocarcinoma, whereas mesothelioma and hematologic malignancies have much lower yields. Counseling this patient that cytology is likely to be diagnostic on the first thoracentesis helps plan a stepwise approach; when cytology is negative in low yield primaries, escalate earlier to image guided biopsy or thoracoscopy. Pearl: Think “high yield” (ovarian, many adenocarcinomas) vs “low yield” (mesothelioma, lymphoma) when setting expectations and planning next steps.
Why others are wrong: (a) Lung adenocarcinoma has a good cytology yield but generally lower than ovarian carcinoma in comparative series. (b) Lymphoma frequently requires flow cytometry and/or tissue biopsy; cytology alone is less sensitive. (d) Mesothelioma is notoriously poorly detected by cytology; tissue is usually required. (e) Small cell lung carcinoma can shed cells but is not the top yield group on initial pleural cytology in contemporary cohorts.
###References###
1.	Arnold DT, et al. Investigating unilateral pleural effusions: the role of cytology. Eur Respir J. 2018;52:1801254. https://erj.ersjournals.com/content/52/5/1801254
2.	Grosu HB, et al. Sensitivity of initial thoracentesis for malignant pleural effusion stratified by tumor type. Respiration. 2018;96:363–9.
3.	Prakash UB, Reiman HM. Comparison of needle biopsy with cytologic analysis for pleural effusion. Mayo Clin Proc. 1985;60:158–64.
Complications in Bronchoscopy 
### Question  ###
A 62 year old man with GOLD III COPD (45 pack year smoker) undergoes flexible bronchoscopy with BAL for a new infiltrate. He is breathing spontaneously on 4 L/min nasal cannula; sedation is midazolam 2 mg IV and fentanyl 50 µg IV. After the first 100 mL lavage aliquot, SpO₂ falls to 86% and remains <90% for >1 minute despite suctioning and brief pause. Heart rhythm is sinus tachycardia. Which intervention is the best next step to reduce further hypoxemia episodes during the ongoing procedure?
 ### Options### 
(a) Switch to noninvasive ventilation via oronasal mask (IPAP 12/EPAP 5 cm H₂O)
(b) Increase nasal cannula to 6 L/min and proceed without other changes
(c) Abort the bronchoscopy and give empiric antibiotics now
(d) Initiate high flow nasal cannula at 40–60 L/min and continue with close monitoring
(e) Electively intubate and complete bronchoscopy via a single lumen endotracheal tube
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Initiating high flow nasal cannula (HFNC) (choice d) reduces desaturation events during bronchoscopy under sedation compared with conventional oxygen devices, and meets guideline triggers to escalate oxygen when SpO₂ falls ≥4% or <90%. A meta analysis of bronchoscopy under sedation found HFNC decreased hypoxemia episodes versus other delivery systems across studies. 
HFNC preserves apneic oxygenation, provides modest PEEP, and is compatible with ongoing flexible bronchoscopy. In contrast, simple nasal cannula flow increase (choice b) often fails once shunt from BAL and airway occupation are present; guidelines recommend supplemental oxygen escalation when desaturation persists. 
Non invasive ventilation (choice a) can support gas exchange but is cumbersome during oral bronchoscopy and typically inferior to HFNC for preventing desaturation events during sedated bronchoscopy; it also impedes instrument maneuverability. 
Elective intubation (choice e) is reserved for refractory hypoxemia or anticipated airway control problems and is unnecessary here. Aborting the procedure and starting antibiotics (choice c) addresses neither the immediate hypoxemia nor an established infection—post BAL fever is often noninfectious. 
Pitfall: Assuming a small increase in low flow oxygen will reverse BAL related shunt; escalation to HFNC is often required during sedated bronchoscopy. 
###References###
1.	Roy A, et al. The Impact of High flow Nasal Cannula vs Other Oxygen Delivery Devices during Bronchoscopy under Sedation: A Systematic Review and Meta analyses. Indian J Crit Care Med. 2022;26:1131 1140. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/36876212/
2.	Du Rand IA, et al. British Thoracic Society guideline for diagnostic flexible bronchoscopy in adults. Thorax. 2013;68(Suppl 1):i1–i44. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23860341/
3.	Kvale PA. Prevention and management of hypoxemia during fiberoptic bronchoscopy. Chest. 2002;121:1021 1022. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/11948026/

### Question  ###
A 72 year old man with atrial fibrillation (no mechanical valve; CHADS VASc = 3), prior drug eluting stent 4 months ago, and a 3.2 cm right hilar node is scheduled for linear EBUS TBNA. Medications: warfarin (INR 2.3), aspirin 81 mg, clopidogrel 75 mg. He is otherwise stable. What is the best periprocedural antithrombotic plan?
 ### Options### 
(a) Continue warfarin and clopidogrel without changes
(b) Stop warfarin 5 days before, no LMWH bridge, confirm day of INR < 1.5, continue aspirin and clopidogrel, and restart warfarin the evening after EBUS
(c) Stop clopidogrel 7–10 days before and bridge with LMWH; continue warfarin
(d) Stop all antithrombotics 48 hours before the procedure only
(e) Switch from warfarin to a DOAC 3 days before the case
###Discussion###
Bottom line: For EBUS TBNA, choice b balances bleeding and thrombosis: stop warfarin 5 days (aiming for INR < 1.5) without bridging in moderate risk AF, continue antiplatelets (including clopidogrel), and restart warfarin the evening after the procedure. EBUS TBNA has a very low bleeding risk, and observational data show no significant increase in bleeding when clopidogrel is continued. 
Guidelines and reviews recommend stopping warfarin 5 days prior (INR target <1.5) and avoiding bridging unless thrombotic risk is high (e.g., mechanical mitral valve). 
Continuing clopidogrel is reasonable for EBUS TBNA based on cohort data. 
Continuing warfarin (choice a) exposes to unnecessary anticoagulant effect; stopping clopidogrel and bridging (choice c) increases bleeding and thrombosis complexity without evidence of benefit for EBUS. Abruptly stopping all agents 48 hours pre procedure (choice d) is insufficient for warfarin clearance. Switching to a DOAC shortly before (choice e) provides no benefit and complicates timing. Pearl: EBUS TBNA can often be done safely without interrupting clopidogrel; tailor warfarin strategy to individual thrombotic risk and confirm INR on the day. 
###References###
1.	Gil HI, et al. Effects of Antithrombotic Treatment on Bleeding Complications of EBUS TBNA. Medicina (Kaunas). 2021;57:142. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33562541/
2.	Meena N, et al. EBUS TBNA and EUS FNA: Risk Assessment for Patients Receiving Clopidogrel. J Bronchology Interv Pulmonol. 2016;23:303 307. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27479011/
3.	Du Rand IA, et al. Thorax. 2013;68(Suppl 1):i1–i44. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23860341/
4.	Bernasconi M, et al. Iatrogenic bleeding during flexible bronchoscopy. ERJ Open Res. 2017;3:00084 2016. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28656131/

### Question  ###
A 65 year old man on rivaroxaban (held 48 h) undergoes flexible bronchoscopy with transbronchial cryobiopsy (volume controlled ventilation; FiO₂ 0.5). Brisk segmental bleeding follows a third biopsy, obscuring the view despite wedging, 4 °C saline, and aliquots of diluted epinephrine. SpO₂ is 92% on FiO₂ 0.6; hemodynamics are stable. What is the most appropriate immediate next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Continue repeated cold saline instillation until bleeding clears
(b) Withdraw the bronchoscope to reoxygenate and reinsert
(c) Administer prophylactic antibiotics to prevent fever
(d) Proceed directly to bronchial artery embolization
(e) Deploy an endobronchial balloon/bronchial blocker to tamponade and isolate the bleeding segment
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice e—balloon tamponade/isolation of the bleeding segment—is recommended for persistent procedural bleeding after local vasoconstrictors and wedging fail. Reviews and guidelines emphasize early mechanical isolation for transbronchial bleeding, especially after cryobiopsy where severity can be higher. 
Continuing lavage alone (choice a) risks dilutional loss of visibility and ongoing shunt. Withdrawing the scope (choice b) can lose airway control and worsen contamination of non bleeding lung. 
Antibiotics (choice c) do not control acute hemorrhage and do not prevent typical post bronchoscopy fever. 
Bronchial artery embolization (choice d) is a rescue option after endoscopic measures or if bleeding recurs; it is not the first escalation when balloon occlusion is available. 
Pitfall: Do not remove the scope during active hemorrhage; maintain wedge position and proceed to isolation (balloon) and hemostasis. 
###References###
1.	Bernasconi M, et al. Iatrogenic bleeding during flexible bronchoscopy. ERJ Open Res. 2017;3:00084 2016. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28656131/
2.	Radchenko C, et al. A systematic approach to the management of massive hemoptysis. J Thorac Dis. 2017;9:S1069 S1086. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29214066/
3.	Tüller C, et al. Hemodynamic effects of endobronchial ornipressin vs terlipressin. Respiration. 2004;71:397 401. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15316215/

### Question  ###
A 54 year old woman undergoes flexible bronchoscopy with four transbronchial biopsies of the right upper lobe (thin flexible scope; alligator forceps; no fluoroscopy). She is asymptomatic in recovery with SpO₂ 95% on room air and normal lung exam 2 hours post procedure. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Routine CT scan to exclude delayed pneumothorax
(b) Routine upright and expiratory chest radiographs before discharge
(c) Discharge without routine imaging, with instructions to return if chest pain or dyspnea develops
(d) Prophylactic small bore chest tube placement before discharge
(e) Observe an additional 24 hours in hospital for delayed pneumothorax
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In asymptomatic patients after transbronchial biopsy, choice c—no routine imaging and symptom based follow up—is supported by a prospective study of 350 cases showing that routine chest radiography did not alter management; pneumothoraces were rare and usually small in asymptomatic patients. 
Guidelines advise at least brief monitoring for symptoms rather than routine post biopsy imaging. 
Routine CT (choice a) is unnecessary radiation and cost. Routine radiographs (choice b) are not indicated without symptoms. Prophylactic tube thoracostomy (choice d) is inappropriate when exam and oximetry are normal. Extended 24 hour observation (choice e) offers no benefit in an otherwise stable patient. Pearl: After TBLB, routine chest X ray is not required in asymptomatic patients; counsel about delayed chest pain or dyspnea. 
###References###
1.	Izbicki G, et al. Is routine chest radiography after transbronchial biopsy necessary? Chest. 2006;129:1561 1564. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16778275/
2.	Du Rand IA, et al. Thorax. 2013;68(Suppl 1):i1–i44. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23860341/

### Question  ###
A 64 year old woman develops fever to 38.3 °C about 12 hours after diagnostic bronchoscopy with BAL (sedation: midazolam 2 mg; fentanyl 25 µg). She feels well, has no cough or chest pain, and is hemodynamically stable. WBC is 8.9 K/µL; CRP is elevated; procalcitonin is 0.08 ng/mL. What is the most appropriate management now?
 ### Options### 
(a) Treat supportively with antipyretics and observe without antibiotics
(b) Start broad spectrum IV antibiotics for presumed post procedure pneumonia
(c) Obtain emergent chest CT angiography
(d) Admit for 24 hours of telemetry monitoring
(e) Repeat bronchoscopy to aspirate retained secretions
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice a is correct. Post bronchoscopy fever (especially after BAL) is common, often sterile and self limited; procalcitonin helps distinguish self limited fever from bacterial infection. A randomized trial showed prophylactic antibiotics did not reduce post bronchoscopy fever or pyrogenic cytokines, supporting observation rather than empiric antibiotics in low risk presentations. 
Procalcitonin outperforms CRP for identifying bacterial infection when fever occurs post bronchoscopy. 
Broad antibiotics (choice b) are unnecessary without clinical or laboratory evidence of infection. CT angiography (choice c) is not indicated absent cardiorespiratory symptoms. Routine admission (choice d) is unwarranted. Repeat bronchoscopy (choice e) is not needed for isolated fever. Pearl: Low procalcitonin with isolated fever after BAL supports observation without antibiotics. 
###References###
1.	Park JS, et al. Impact of antibiotic prophylaxis on postbronchoscopy fever: RCT. Int J Tuberc Lung Dis. 2011;15:528 535. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/21396214/
2.	Um SW, et al. Prospective analysis of post bronchoscopy fever. Chest. 2004;125:945 952. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15006953/
3.	Hackner K, et al. Procalcitonin for early diagnosis of post interventional infection. BMC Pulm Med. 2017;17:156. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29179755/

### Question  ###
During outpatient flexible bronchoscopy in a 69 year old man (40 pack year smoker) performed seated with transoral insertion and no baseline oxygen, SpO₂ drifts from 95% to 88% for >1 minute while passing the cords; ECG shows frequent PVCs but stable blood pressure. What is the most appropriate immediate step to lower arrhythmia risk and improve safety?
 ### Options### 
(a) Escalate supplemental oxygen to maintain SpO₂ ≥ 90% and continue continuous ECG monitoring
(b) Administer IV amiodarone 150 mg now
(c) Give adenosine 6 mg IV for presumed SVT
(d) Abort the case and schedule under general anesthesia
(e) Administer flumazenil 0.2 mg IV immediately
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice a is correct. Arrhythmias during bronchoscopy are typically triggered or worsened by hypoxemia and airway stimulation; the first intervention is to correct desaturation (oxygen) and continue monitoring. Studies link arrhythmia frequency to periods of maximal oxygen desaturation, and guidelines recommend prompt oxygen supplementation when SpO₂ falls >4% or below 90%. 
Antiarrhythmics ( ### Options###  b and c) are unnecessary for isolated, hemodynamically stable ectopy driven by hypoxemia and may complicate the procedure. Aborting the case (choice d) is not required once oxygenation is corrected. Flumazenil (choice e) treats benzodiazepine related oversedation, not arrhythmia or hypoxemia. Pitfall: Treating bronchoscopy related ectopy with antiarrhythmics before correcting hypoxemia; prioritize oxygenation. 
###References###
1.	Hamdy G, et al. Cardiac arrhythmias during fiberoptic bronchoscopy and relation with oxygen saturation. Lung India. 2005;22:54 59. https://www.researchgate.net/publication/45263786
2.	Davies L, et al. Cardiovascular consequences of fibreoptic bronchoscopy. Eur Respir J. 1997;10:695 698. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/9073008/
3.	Du Rand IA, et al. Thorax. 2013;68(Suppl 1):i1–i44. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23860341/

### Question  ###
A 45 year old man undergoing EBUS under moderate sedation receives fentanyl 150 µg IV rapidly plus midazolam 1 mg. Within 1 minute he becomes difficult to ventilate by mask despite patent airway maneuvers; chest wall is rigid to manual ventilation; SpO₂ 82%. Blood pressure is stable. Which immediate management is most appropriate?
 ### Options### 
(a) Administer rocuronium 1 mg/kg and intubate immediately
(b) Give epinephrine 10 µg IV boluses for presumed anaphylaxis
(c) Reverse benzodiazepines with flumazenil 0.5 mg IV
(d) Provide positive pressure ventilation and give naloxone 0.04 mg IV, repeating every 2–5 min until ventilation improves
(e) Stop the case and place the patient in the lateral decubitus position
###Discussion###
Bottom line: This presentation is classic for fentanyl induced chest wall rigidity (“wooden chest syndrome”). The best immediate step is choice d—assist ventilation and administer low dose naloxone (0.04 mg IV, repeat every 2–5 min) until rigidity resolves and ventilation improves. 
Case reports describe this complication even with non anesthetic bolus doses; rapid injection increases risk. Naloxone reverses opioid effect and resolves rigidity; temporary positive pressure ventilation is often required. 
Paralysis and intubation (choice a) is a last resort if naloxone and ventilation fail. Epinephrine (choice b) is not indicated without signs of anaphylaxis. Flumazenil (choice c) addresses benzodiazepines, not opioid induced rigidity. Positioning (choice e) does not treat the pathophysiology. Pearl: Start with small, titrated naloxone doses (0.04 mg) to reverse fentanyl induced rigidity while preserving analgesia and avoiding acute withdrawal. 
###References###
1.	Çoruh B, et al. Fentanyl induced chest wall rigidity. Chest. 2013;143:1145 1146. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23546488/
2.	Rosal NR, et al. Wooden Chest Syndrome: case report. J Investig Med High Impact Case Rep. 2021;9:23247096211034036. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/34301155/
3.	Du Rand IA, et al. Thorax. 2013;68(Suppl 1):i1–i44. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23860341/

### Question  ###
An 80 year old woman (BMI 32) received midazolam 4 mg IV and fentanyl 25 µg for outpatient bronchoscopy. Thirty minutes post procedure she is somnolent with shallow respirations (RR 8/min), SpO₂ 90% on 2 L/min nasal cannula, BP 128/72 mm Hg, and a patent airway. What is the most appropriate immediate management?
 ### Options### 
(a) Place on HFNC and observe for spontaneous arousal
(b) Administer flumazenil 0.2 mg IV, then 0.1 mg every 1 min (max 1 mg), and monitor closely for resedation
(c) Give naloxone 0.4 mg IV bolus
(d) Proceed to endotracheal intubation
(e) Administer reversal for both agents simultaneously (flumazenil 0.5 mg and naloxone 0.2 mg)
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice b is correct. This pattern suggests benzodiazepine related oversedation. Flumazenil reverses midazolam and improves respiratory drive; dosing is 0.2 mg initially, then 0.1 mg every minute to a maximum of 1 mg. Because flumazenil’s action may be shorter than midazolam’s, careful observation for resedation is essential. 
HFNC (choice a) does not treat drug effect. Naloxone (choice c) targets opioids and risks withdrawal without benefit if opioid effect is minimal. Intubation (choice d) is unnecessary if reversal is available and the airway is patent. Routine dual reversal (choice e) is not recommended; antagonize the suspected agent first. A randomized, placebo controlled trial supports flumazenil’s safety and efficacy in benzodiazepine facilitated bronchoscopy. 
Pitfall: Failing to monitor after flumazenil; recurrence of sedation can occur due to its shorter half life than midazolam. 
###References###
1.	Williamson BH, et al. Placebo controlled study of flumazenil in bronchoscopic procedures. Br J Clin Pharmacol. 1997;43:77 83. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/9056056/
2.	Du Rand IA, et al. Thorax. 2013;68(Suppl 1):i1–i44. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23860341/
3.	Dreher M, et al. Midazolam vs midazolam+alfentanil in patients with respiratory failure. Respiration. 2010;79:307 314. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20016132/

### Question  ###
A 58 year old woman with suspected sarcoidosis is scheduled for transbronchial lung biopsy (thin bronchoscope; conscious sedation). She had a prior small spontaneous pneumothorax and is anxious about recurrence. Which technique choice best minimizes pneumothorax risk during TBLB?
 ### Options### 
(a) Prefer middle lobe or lingula sampling because those segments are small
(b) Perform bilateral lung biopsies at the same sitting to maximize yield
(c) Rely on fluoroscopy for all biopsies to prevent pneumothorax
(d) Use larger cup forceps to obtain deeper bites
(e) Use alligator forceps rather than cup forceps for sampling
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice e is correct. A randomized trial in sarcoidosis found cup forceps caused more pneumothoraces than alligator forceps during TBLB; selecting alligator forceps modestly reduces pneumothorax risk. 
Sampling the middle lobe or lingula (choice a) can increase risk due to proximity to fissures and should be avoided when diffuse disease allows alternative sites. 
Bilateral same session biopsies (choice b) risk bilateral pneumothoraces and are discouraged. 
Routine fluoroscopy (choice c) may improve diagnostic yield in localized lesions but does not reliably reduce pneumothorax incidence. 
Larger/deeper bites (choice d) plausibly raise risk without proven benefit for safety. Pearl: Technique tweaks—forceps choice and avoiding fissure adjacent segments—matter more for pneumothorax prevention than routine fluoroscopy. 
###References###
1.	Sehgal IS, et al. Cup vs alligator forceps in sarcoidosis TBLB: RCT. Chest. 2016;149:1584 1586. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27287583/
2.	Du Rand IA, et al. Thorax. 2013;68(Suppl 1):i1–i44. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23860341/

### Question  ###
Ninety minutes after discharge from outpatient bronchoscopy with three transbronchial biopsies, a 67 year old man calls with sudden right sided pleuritic chest pain and mild dyspnea. In the ED he is stable, SpO₂ 93% on room air, with diminished right apical breath sounds. Which diagnostic test is the best initial choice to quickly detect a clinically significant pneumothorax?
 ### Options### 
(a) Upright PA/lateral chest radiographs
(b) Expiratory chest radiograph
(c) Point of care thoracic ultrasound at bedside
(d) CT chest without contrast
(e) ABG followed by observation
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice c is correct. For symptomatic patients after TBLB, point of care thoracic ultrasound is a rapid, accurate bedside test for clinically significant pneumothorax; recent data report high specificity (≈100%) and good sensitivity (~87%) for clinically meaningful pleural separation. 
While plain radiography ( ### Options###  a and b) may miss anterior/apical pneumothoraces in supine or semi upright patients, ultrasound allows immediate decision making and triage. CT (choice d) is most sensitive but is not the fastest initial study and entails radiation and transport. ABG and observation (choice e) delay diagnosis. Pearl: POCUS is the fastest accurate test for clinically significant post biopsy pneumothorax at the bedside. 
###References###
1.	DeMasi S, et al. Thoracic point of care ultrasound for clinically significant traumatic pneumothorax. Acad Emerg Med. 2023;30:653 661. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/36658000/
2.	Du Rand IA, et al. Thorax. 2013;68(Suppl 1):i1–i44. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23860341/
Peripheral Bronchoscopy
### Question  ###
A 58-year-old man with hypertension and COPD (FEV₁ 55% predicted) is a current smoker with a 22–pack year history. Vitals are normal. He asks about lung cancer screening. What is the most appropriate screening strategy to offer now?
 ### Options### 
(a) Initiate annual low dose chest CT (LDCT) with shared decision making
(b) Obtain an annual screening chest radiograph instead of CT
(c) Defer screening until he reaches age 60 years
(d) Advise smoking cessation first and delay screening for 1 year
(e) Enroll him in breath volatile organic compound (VOC) testing in place of imaging
###Discussion###
Bottom line: For this patient, choice (a) is correct—offer annual LDCT now. The 2021 USPSTF recommends annual LDCT for adults aged 50–80 with ≥20 pack years who currently smoke or quit within 15 years; the mortality benefit of LDCT was demonstrated in NLST. 
Lung Cancer Screening

NLST showed a 20% relative reduction in lung cancer mortality with LDCT versus chest radiography among high risk smokers, establishing LDCT (not chest x ray) as the validated screening modality. USPSTF lowered the age to 50 and pack years to 20 in 2021 to extend benefit to more high risk people. Screening should be coupled with smoking cessation counseling.
(b) Chest radiography lacks evidence for mortality reduction and was inferior to LDCT in NLST; it should not be used for screening. (NLST.) 
Lung Cancer Screening

(c) There is no need to wait until age 60; eligibility begins at 50 under USPSTF. 
Lung Cancer Screening

(d) Screening is not delayed for a quit attempt; current smokers are eligible based on age/pack years. 
Lung Cancer Screening

(e) Exhaled VOC approaches remain investigational for population screening; they do not replace LDCT. 
Lung Cancer Screening
Pearl: LDCT is the only screening test proven to reduce lung cancer mortality in high risk smokers. 
Lung Cancer Screening
###References###
1.	Aberle DR, et al. Reduced lung-cancer mortality with low-dose computed tomographic screening. N Engl J Med. 2011;365:395–409. PubMed
2.	US Preventive Services Task Force. Screening for Lung Cancer. JAMA. 2021;325:962–970. PubMed
3.	de Koning HJ, et al. NELSON trial. N Engl J Med. 2020;382:503–513. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 58-year-old woman with idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis (stable on nintedanib) quit smoking 18 years ago after 22 pack years. She is active, with an estimated life expectancy >5 years. She asks if she still qualifies for screening given she quit >15 years ago. What should you recommend?
 ### Options### 
(a) Do not screen because she quit >15 years ago under USPSTF criteria
(b) Offer annual LDCT now, as contemporary guidelines support screening despite time since cessation
(c) Screen only if PLCOm2012 6 year risk is ≥1.5%
(d) Obtain biennial chest radiographs rather than LDCT
(e) Order a cell free DNA (cfDNA) assay instead of imaging
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (b) is correct—offer annual LDCT now using up to date guidance. The 2023/2024 American Cancer Society update recommends annual LDCT for adults aged ≥50 with ≥20 pack years regardless of years since quitting, with continuation individualized for those with life expectancy ≥5 years. The 2025 NCCN Insights similarly emphasizes risk based selection and additional risk factors (e.g., ILD) when deciding on LDCT. 
Lung Cancer Screening

While the 2021 USPSTF retains a 15 year quit limit, ACS (and NCCN’s risk based approach) broaden eligibility to capture high risk former smokers who remain vulnerable many years after quitting—particularly those with added risks such as fibrotic ILD. Shared decision making and smoking cessation reinforcement remain essential.
(a) This reflects USPSTF, but contemporary ACS/NCCN recommendations support screening here; strictly following USPSTF would miss high risk former smokers. 
Lung Cancer Screening

(c) Risk models like PLCOm2012 can aid selection, but ACS does not require a model threshold when age and pack years are met. 
Lung Cancer Screening

(d) Chest radiography has no proven mortality benefit for screening. 
Lung Cancer Screening

(e) cfDNA is promising but not a replacement for LDCT screening at this time. 
Lung Cancer Screening
Pitfall: Applying only USPSTF’s ≤15 year quit rule can exclude high risk former smokers whom ACS/NCCN would screen. 
Lung Cancer Screening
###References###
1.	Wolf A, et al. ACS Lung Cancer Screening Guideline Update. CA Cancer J Clin. 2024;74:50–81. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 52-year-old man with mild COPD (FEV₁ 70%), BMI 20 kg/m², and a first degree relative with lung cancer has an 18–pack year history and quit 2 years ago. He works in a region with elevated residential radon. He asks whether he should be screened even though he has <20 pack years. Which selection strategy is most likely to identify more lung cancers in patients like him?
 ### Options### 
(a) Use CHEST 2021 criteria (≥30 pack years, age 55–77) only
(b) Apply ACCP pretest probability categories without a model
(c) Use a validated multivariable risk model (e.g., PLCOm2012) to determine screening eligibility
(d) Require a screening PET/CT before deciding on LDCT
(e) Offer a one time diagnostic LDCT outside a program
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (c) is correct—risk prediction models such as PLCOm2012 identify more high risk individuals (and cancers) than pack year–only criteria. In comparative studies, PLCOm2012 captured additional cancers missed by USPSTF style rules, supporting model based selection where feasible. 
Lung Cancer Screening

Tammemägi et al. prospectively showed higher detection with PLCOm2012 versus USPSTF 2013; a UK comparison likewise favored PLCOm2012 over several alternatives. Models incorporate age, sex, COPD, family history, education, race, and detailed smoking variables, better reflecting individualized risk. Programs using model based thresholds can improve efficiency and equity, especially for individuals with substantial non pack year risks (e.g., COPD, family history, radon).
(a) CHEST 2021 retains older (≥30 pack years, 55–77) limits and will miss many at risk persons <55 or with 20–29 pack years. 


(b) ACCP categories guide management of nodules, not screening eligibility. 

(d) PET/CT is not a screening tool; LDCT is. 


(e) Ad hoc one time LDCT without longitudinal follow up is inferior to programmatic screening. 

Pearl: When available, PLCOm2012 based selection improves case finding compared with pack year cutoffs alone. 

###References###
1.	Tammemägi MC, et al. USPSTF2013 vs PLCOm2012 eligibility. Lancet Oncol. 2022;23:138–148. PubMed
2.	Robbins HA, et al. Comparative performance of lung cancer risk models. Br J Cancer. 2021;124:2026–2034. PubMed
3.	Wood DE, et al. NCCN Insights 2025. JNCCN. 2025;23(1). Publisher
