### Question  ###
A 60-year-old man eligible for LDCT screening worries about “radiation from yearly scans.” Which counseling statement is most appropriate?
 ### Options### 
(a) “LDCT dose exceeds that of a standard diagnostic chest CT but is still low.”
(b) “Radiation from LDCT is negligible and carries no risk.”
(c) “MRI is preferred to avoid any radiation for lung screening.”
(d) “Annual LDCT is ~1–1.5 mSv—near background exposure—and in high risk adults the mortality benefit outweighs this small risk.”
(e) “PET/CT uses less radiation than LDCT and is safer for screening.”
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d) is correct. LDCT screening typically delivers about 1–1.5 mSv—close to annual background radiation—and confers a proven mortality benefit in high risk populations (NLST), which outweighs the small modeled radiation risk. 


Seminal work synthesizing LDCT dose and risk indicates very low incremental lifetime cancer risk compared with the mortality benefit observed in randomized trials. Avoiding screening on the basis of radiation concerns would forfeit a demonstrated reduction in lung cancer deaths.
(a) LDCT dose is lower than a standard diagnostic chest CT; implying the opposite is inaccurate. 


(b) Risk is small but not zero; transparent risk communication is recommended. 


(c) MRI is not validated for lung cancer screening. 


(e) PET/CT has substantially higher radiation than LDCT and is not a screening test. 

Pearl: Frame LDCT risks with context (≈1–1.5 mSv) and emphasize the randomized trial mortality benefit. 

###References###
1.	Frank L, et al. Radiation risk of lung cancer screening. Semin Respir Crit Care Med. 2013;34:738–747. PubMed
2.	Aberle DR, et al. NLST. N Engl J Med. 2011;365:395–409. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 69-year-old man (current smoker, 48–pack years) with severe emphysema (FEV₁ 45%) on 2 L/min oxygen has a 12 mm spiculated left upper lobe nodule on thin section CT (1.0 mm). No clear bronchus sign is seen. Platelets 180 K/µL; INR 1.0. He is anxious to avoid a chest tube. Which diagnostic approach is most appropriate initially?
 ### Options### 
(a) CT guided transthoracic needle biopsy under deep sedation
(b) Proceed directly to VATS wedge resection without tissue diagnosis
(c) PET/CT surveillance without biopsy
(d) Standard bronchoscopy with forceps biopsy only
(e) Electromagnetic navigation bronchoscopy with radial EBUS and fluoroscopic guidance
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (e) is correct—navigational bronchoscopy with adjuncts is a reasonable first approach in a patient with severe emphysema who strongly wishes to avoid pneumothorax. Large multicenter data (NAVIGATE) show acceptable yields and low pneumothorax rates for electromagnetic navigation bronchoscopy in peripheral lesions, supporting a safety focused strategy here. Guideline frameworks (ACCP) highlight balancing diagnostic yield with complication risk; severe emphysema increases pneumothorax risk with transthoracic needle biopsy. 
(a) CT guided biopsy has high yield but higher pneumothorax risk, particularly in emphysema; patient values and risk profile make this less favorable initially. 

(b) Up front surgery without tissue diagnosis is generally reserved for very high pretest probability in good surgical candidates; his emphysema increases perioperative risk and alternative less invasive diagnosis exists. 

(c) PET/CT may aid staging but “watchful PET/CT surveillance” is inappropriate for a suspicious 12 mm spiculated nodule in a high risk smoker. 

(d) Non navigated bronchoscopy alone has low yield for small peripheral lesions. Enhanced guidance (navigation ± radial EBUS) is preferred. 
Pitfall: Defaulting to CT guided biopsy in severe emphysema can overlook a safer bronchoscopic pathway that aligns with patient p###References###. 
###References###
1.	Folch EE, et al. NAVIGATE study. J Thorac Oncol. 2019;14:445–458. PubMed
2.	Gould MK, et al. ACCP guideline on pulmonary nodules. Chest. 2013;143(5 Suppl):e93S–e120S. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 45-year-old woman with no smoking history undergoes CT for trauma. Thin section CT (1.0 mm) shows a solitary solid 7 mm right lower lobe nodule. She is asymptomatic; vitals are normal. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Repeat thin section chest CT in 6–12 months, then at 18–24 months if stable
(b) PET/CT now to rule out malignancy
(c) CT guided transthoracic needle biopsy now
(d) Begin annual LDCT “screening”
(e) Bronchoscopic biopsy with transbronchial forceps
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (a) is correct. For an incidentally detected solid 6–8 mm nodule in a low risk adult, the 2017 Fleischner Society guideline recommends CT follow up at 6–12 months and, if stable, a second scan at 18–24 months; immediate PET or biopsy is not indicated. 

PET has limited sensitivity for nodules under ~8–10 mm, and tissue sampling carries unnecessary risk at this size and risk profile. LDCT “screening” refers to programmatic annual CTs in high risk smokers, not management of an incidental nodule in a never smoker.
(b) PET/CT has limited accuracy for small nodules and is not first line here. 

(c) Invasive biopsy is not indicated for a 7 mm solid nodule in a low risk patient without worrisome features. 

(d) Screening protocols do not apply; this is incidental nodule management. 

(e) Bronchoscopic biopsy would be low yield and unnecessary at this stage. 
Pearl: Use nodule size and risk to guide care: 6–8 mm solid nodules generally warrant CT surveillance, not immediate biopsy. 
###References###
1.	MacMahon H, et al. Fleischner Society 2017 guidelines. Radiology. 2017;284:228–243. PubMed
2.	Gould MK, et al. ACCP guideline. Chest. 2013;143(5 Suppl):e93S–e120S. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 62-year-old man (former smoker, 30 pack years, quit 5 years ago) is found to have a 12 mm part solid left upper lobe nodule on thin section CT (1.0 mm) with a 4 mm solid component. He takes apixaban for atrial fibrillation (INR not applicable; platelets 210 K/µL). He is asymptomatic. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Immediate CT guided needle biopsy after holding anticoagulation
(b) Repeat thin section CT in 3–6 months to confirm persistence and reassess the solid component
(c) PET/CT now to exclude malignancy
(d) Empiric antibiotics followed by a 1 month CT
(e) Permanent cessation of anticoagulation and proceed directly to lobectomy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (b) is correct. The Fleischner 2017 guideline recommends that part solid nodules ≥6 mm undergo repeat thin section CT at 3–6 months to confirm persistence; continued surveillance is advised if stable, with intervention considered if the solid component is ≥6 mm or grows. PET is often less informative for small part solid lesions, and immediate biopsy may be premature before demonstrating persistence. 

This approach avoids procedural risk (including bleeding on anticoagulants) while adhering to evidence based timelines that reflect the biology of subsolid lesions. If the solid focus enlarges to ≥6 mm or overall risk becomes high, tissue diagnosis or resection is pursued.
(a) Biopsy can be considered later if growth occurs, but guideline directed early follow up is first. 

(c) PET has limited sensitivity for small or part solid nodules and cannot reliably exclude malignancy at this size. 

(d) Antibiotics lack evidence for subsolid nodules unless clinical infection is suspected. 

(e) Surgery without confirming persistence/growth is not appropriate here. 
Pearl: For part solid nodules, the size of the solid component (≥6 mm) is the trigger for aggressive management. 
###References###
1.	MacMahon H, et al. Fleischner 2017. Radiology. 2017;284:228–243. PubMed
2.	Gould MK, et al. ACCP guideline. Chest. 2013;143(5 Suppl):e93S–e120S. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 59-year-old man in an LDCT program has a new 7 mm solid right upper lobe nodule on 1.0 mm images. PET/CT performed for anxiety is visually negative. He feels reassured and asks to skip follow up. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Reassure and discontinue imaging
(b) Refer directly for surgical resection
(c) Continue guideline directed CT surveillance; do not be falsely reassured by a negative PET at this size
(d) Start empiric antibiotics and re scan in 2 weeks
(e) Bronchoscopic biopsy now
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (c) is correct. For nodules in this size range, PET has limited sensitivity; a negative PET cannot exclude malignancy. Guideline directed CT surveillance remains appropriate. ACCP guidance emphasizes that small nodules (<8–10 mm) should not be “ruled out” by PET alone, and Fleischner algorithms guide timing based on size and risk. 

For a 7 mm nodule, short interval CT follow up is favored over invasive sampling unless additional high risk features exist. Using PET results to stop surveillance risks delayed diagnosis.
(a) Stopping follow up contradicts guideline recommendations for small new nodules. 

(b) Surgery is excessive for a 7 mm lesion without high risk features or growth. 

(d) Empiric antibiotics are not supported absent infection. 

(e) Biopsy yields are limited and risk/benefit is unfavorable at 7 mm in most settings. 
Pitfall: Over relying on “negative” PET in small nodules can inappropriately halt necessary surveillance. 
###References###
1.	Gould MK, et al. ACCP guideline. Chest. 2013;143(5 Suppl):e93S–e120S. PubMed
2.	MacMahon H, et al. Fleischner 2017. Radiology. 2017;284:228–243. PubMed

### Question  ###
An LDCT screening program deploys an FDA cleared AI tool to flag nodules and estimate malignancy risk. In the first quarter, detection sensitivity seems higher, but call backs for additional imaging have increased. How should the program integrate AI most appropriately?
 ### Options### 
(a) Replace radiologist review with AI to standardize interpretations
(b) Turn off AI because of reduced specificity
(c) Mandate PET/CT for all AI flagged nodules
(d) Use AI as an adjunctive concurrent reader while standardizing follow up protocols to limit unnecessary work ups
(e) Switch from LDCT to chest radiography to reduce AI related callbacks
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d) is correct. Systematic reviews show AI assisted software can increase sensitivity in screening but may reduce specificity, risking more follow ups. Best practice is to use AI as an adjunct to radiologist interpretation, with protocolized thresholds and follow up pathways to maintain value and minimize over testing. 

AI can assist measurement and detection, but current evidence does not support replacing radiologists. Programs should prospectively define action thresholds (e.g., volume, growth) aligned with guideline algorithms, audit downstream testing, and monitor false positive rates.
(a) Full replacement is unsupported; AI augments but does not supplant expert review. 

(b) Abandoning AI forfeits potential sensitivity gains; optimization is preferable. 

(c) Routine PET/CT for all AI flags would inflate radiation, cost, and false positives. 

(e) Chest radiography is not an evidence based substitute for CT screening. 
Pearl: AI can help find more nodules, but programs must anticipate lower specificity and build guardrails to avoid unnecessary downstream testing. 
###References###
1.	Geppert J, et al. AI for nodule/cancer detection in CT screening: Systematic review. Thorax. 2024;79:1040–1049. PubMed
2.	Ardila D, et al. End to end deep learning for LDCT screening. Nat Med. 2019;25:954–961. PubMed

### Question  ###
A 60-year-old former smoker (28–pack years; quit 6 years ago) declines CT because he “just wants a blood test.” He asks if a blood based test can replace LDCT. What should you recommend?
 ### Options### 
(a) Breath VOC testing alone provides screening accuracy comparable to LDCT
(b) A plasma protein panel alone can replace LDCT and safely avoid imaging
(c) Enroll him in PET/CT based screening to avoid CT
(d) Continue to recommend annual LDCT as the only strategy with proven mortality reduction; consider blood based assays only as adjuncts/in trials
(e) Semiannual chest radiography as a compromise strategy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d) is correct. LDCT is the only modality proven to reduce lung cancer mortality in randomized trials (NLST; supported by NELSON). Blood based tests—such as cfDNA fragmentome assays or protein panels—are promising but currently adjunctive; none has demonstrated population level mortality reduction to replace LDCT screening. 

A recent clinical validation of a cfDNA fragmentome assay reported encouraging diagnostic performance, particularly when combined with imaging, but evidence has not supplanted LDCT as the backbone of screening. Patients should be counseled that declining CT forfeits the proven mortality benefit; participation in trials or adjunct testing may be discussed but not as substitutes.
(a) VOC approaches remain investigational and heterogeneous across studies. 

(b) Protein panels may aid risk stratification but have not replaced LDCT. 

(c) PET/CT is not validated for screening and adds radiation/cost without mortality evidence. 

(e) Chest radiography does not reduce lung cancer mortality in screening. 
Pearl: Counsel that LDCT remains the standard for mortality reduction; liquid biopsy is an adjunct, not a replacement. 
###References###
1.	Aberle DR, et al. NLST. N Engl J Med. 2011;365:395–409. PubMed
2.	de Koning HJ, et al. NELSON. N Engl J Med. 2020;382:503–513. PubMed
3.	Mazzone PJ, et al. cfDNA fragmentome assay validation. Cancer Discov. 2024;14:2224–2242. PubMed
### Question  ###
A 67 year old man (30 pack year former smoker) is referred for a new 1.7 cm peripheral right lower lobe solid nodule seen on 1 mm thin section CT. The nodule abuts the chest wall, is pleural based, and lacks a bronchus sign. There is mild centrilobular emphysema. He takes apixaban for atrial fibrillation (last dose 48 hours ago), has normal PFTs, and prefers the quickest route to a diagnosis if a biopsy is needed. The Brock estimate is 22% (intermediate risk). PET/CT would not change his desire for tissue confirmation either way. Which initial diagnostic strategy is most appropriate?
 ### Options### 
(a) Electromagnetic navigational bronchoscopy with fluoroscopy
(b) Proceed directly to surgical wedge resection
(c) FDG PET/CT first and defer tissue sampling unless avid
(d) CT guided transthoracic needle biopsy
(e) Short interval CT surveillance at 3 and 12 months
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice D correct. For a peripheral, pleural based lesion without a bronchus sign, CT guided transthoracic needle biopsy (TTNB) offers a high likelihood of diagnosis as a first test and aligns with risk adapted modality selection based on lesion to airway anatomy. Contemporary analyses emphasize choosing the approach by size, location, and airway proximity; pleural based “chest wall” targets without a bronchus sign favor TTNB over guided bronchoscopy, which performs best when a bronchus sign is present. The chapter explicitly notes that a small tumor near the chest wall may be better approached with TTNB rather than bronchoscopic techniques. 
Guided bronchoscopy yields about 69% across modalities in pooled data—useful, but generally less than TTNB in this anatomic scenario—so option A is reasonable in other contexts but not optimal here. 
Surgery (B) is disproportionate at 22% pretest probability. PET/CT first (C) will not obviate the need for tissue and may delay care. Surveillance (E) is inappropriate for an intermediate risk, 17 mm solid nodule. Pearl: Match the biopsy route to anatomy—pleural based targets without a bronchus sign usually favor TTNB over guided bronchoscopy.
###References###
1.	Silvestri GA, et al. An evaluation of diagnostic yield from bronchoscopy: impact of clinical/radiographic factors and procedure type. Chest. 2020;157(6):1656 1664. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/31978428/
2.	Nadig TR, et al. Guided bronchoscopy for pulmonary lesions: updated meta analysis. Chest. 2023;163(6):1589 1598. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/36640994/

### Question  ###
A 52 year old woman (never smoker) has an incidentally detected 8 mm smooth solid nodule in the left lower lobe on 1 mm CT. There is no bronchus sign, and the nodule is >10 mm from pleura. She is asymptomatic, not on antithrombotics, and has normal PFTs. Using the Mayo model and clinical factors, her pretest probability of malignancy is estimated at <5%. She asks what to do next. What is the most appropriate management?
 ### Options### 
(a) Navigational bronchoscopy now
(b) CT surveillance at 3–6, 9–12, and 18–24 months
(c) PET/CT to “rule in” malignancy before deciding
(d) CT guided transthoracic needle biopsy now
(e) Surgical wedge resection
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice B correct. For very low risk solid nodules (>8 mm but pCA <5%), the ACCP recommends serial CT surveillance (typically at 3–6, 9–12, and 18–24 months) rather than immediate PET or biopsy. This risk stratified approach minimizes harm from unnecessary invasive procedures while maintaining safety through structured follow up. The algorithm reproduced in the chapter (see Fig. 1, page 7) places very low risk nodules on a surveillance pathway. 
PET/CT (C) is not needed to characterize a very low risk 8 mm nodule and will not eliminate the need for interval imaging. Bronchoscopic (A) or transthoracic (D) biopsies are disproportionate given the low pCA and small size; procedure related pneumothorax/bleeding risks exceed anticipated benefit. Resection (E) is reserved for high probability/operable scenarios. Pearl: Estimate pCA first; in very low risk nodules, surveillance is the test—it safely avoids unnecessary procedures while detecting clinically meaningful growth.
###References###
1.	Gould MK, et al. ACCP evidence based guidelines for pulmonary nodules. Chest. 2013;143(5 Suppl):e93S e120S. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23649456/
2.	MacMahon H, et al. Fleischner Society 2017 recommendations. Radiology. 2017;284(1):228 243. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28240562/

### Question  ###
A 70 year old man (40 pack year former smoker) has a part solid 18 mm right upper lobe nodule with a 9 mm solid component on 1 mm CT. No prior scans exist. He is off anticoagulants, with FEV₁ 2.4 L (86%) and DLCO 82%. The Mayo estimate is intermediate–high; he is an excellent surgical candidate and asks whether PET could “decide” if this is cancer before any intervention. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Proceed directly to surgical diagnosis/resection; use PET/CT for staging rather than characterization
(b) PET/CT only and observe if mildly avid
(c) Repeat CT in 3 months to confirm persistence
(d) CT guided transthoracic biopsy only if PET is intensely avid
(e) Navigational bronchoscopy to sample the ground glass portion
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice A correct. For a part solid nodule with a solid component ≥8 mm (and overall size >15 mm), guideline based management supports definitive evaluation—i.e., PET/CT for staging and tissue diagnosis/resection in appropriate surgical candidates—rather than short interval observation or reliance on PET to “rule in” malignancy. Subsolid lesions often have limited FDG avidity, and PET negativity does not exclude malignancy; moreover, here the solid core is ≥8 mm, a threshold at which escalation to biopsy/resection is recommended. The ACCP algorithm (Fig. 1, p. 7) and subsolid nodule guidance in the chapter support this approach. 
PET only (B) risks false reassurance, and waiting 3 months (C) is for part solid nodules whose solid portion is <8 mm. Triggering biopsy solely by intensity of PET uptake (D) misuses PET as a diagnostic arbiter in subsolid disease. Sampling the ground glass component (E) is less informative than targeting the solid core. Pitfall: Do not rely on PET to exclude malignancy in subsolid nodules—early adenocarcinomas may be PET negative.
###References###
1.	Gould MK, et al. ACCP evidence based guidelines for pulmonary nodules. Chest. 2013;143(5 Suppl):e93S e120S. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23649456/
2.	Nomori H, et al. FDG PET in nodules <3 cm with ground glass features. Lung Cancer. 2004;45(1):19 27. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15196730/

### Question  ###
A 61 year old woman (never smoker) has a solitary 9 mm right middle lobe solid nodule. Baseline volumetry (thin section CT) measured 260 mm³; repeat CT at 6 months shows 340 mm³. She feels well and takes no antithrombotics. Using Brock, pCA is ~12% (intermediate). Which next step best aligns with volumetric evidence based management?
 ### Options### 
(a) Continue annual CT only (change is within error)
(b) Repeat CT again in 6 months; still early
(c) PET/CT to characterize; if negative, discharge
(d) Proceed directly to surgical resection
(e) Escalate evaluation now—PET/CT and plan tissue diagnosis
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice E correct. Volumetric growth ≥25% between scans exceeds measurement variability and indicates true growth; here, 260→340 mm³ is ~31%. In BTS informed pathways, growth or VDT <400 days in otherwise indeterminate nodules should trigger further evaluation rather than continued surveillance. Escalation typically involves PET/CT (if lesion ≥8 mm) to inform staging/localization, followed by tissue diagnosis (percutaneous vs bronchoscopic based on anatomy). The chapter summarizes this volume based approach, including use of volumetry and VDT thresholds, and emphasizes that volumetrics add predictive value beyond diameter alone. 
Annual CT only (A) or repeating CT yet again at 6 months (B) ignores established growth. PET negativity does not “rule out” cancer (C), particularly given documented growth. Immediate surgery (D) may be appropriate at high pCA, but with intermediate risk, biopsy to confirm and guide therapy is reasonable. Pearl: A ≥25% volume increase defines growth—act on it; do not dismiss it as noise.
###References###
1.	van Klaveren RJ, et al. Management of lung nodules detected by volume CT scanning. N Engl J Med. 2009;361(23):2221 2229. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19955524/
2.	Revel MP, et al. Volumetric doubling times for differentiating benign vs malignant nodules. AJR Am J Roentgenol. 2006;187(1):135 142. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16794167/
3.	Callister MEJ, et al. BTS pulmonary nodule guideline. Thorax. 2015;70(Suppl 2):ii1 ii54. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26082159/

### Question  ###
Your 250 bed hospital performs ~120 bronchoscopies/year (half after hours), has two aging reusable flexible bronchoscopes (RFBs) with escalating repair costs, and lacks on site weekend reprocessing. ICU clinicians want single use flexible bronchoscopes (SUFBs) for urgent cases, but the CFO asks how to decide between all SUFB, all RFB, or a mixed fleet. What should you do to choose the most cost efficient strategy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Buy only SUFBs—unit price is falling and they eliminate reprocessing
(b) Replace both RFBs—the capital cost is amortized and always cheaper
(c) Use a mathematical capacity/cost model with local annual volume, repair, and reprocessing costs to optimize a mixed device fleet
(d) Decide case by case based on location (ICU vs OR)
(e) Choose the brand with the lowest listed SUFB price
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice C correct. Cost effectiveness hinges on annual throughput and the local burden of repair and reprocessing; a model based analysis using institutional inputs best determines the optimal mixed device strategy. Edenharter and colleagues developed a mathematical framework to optimize the cost efficient mix of reusable and single use bronchoscopes, explicitly incorporating demand, repairs, and processing resources. In practice, low volume centers (or high repair/after hours load) often reach parity or advantage with SUFBs; high volume, low repair programs may favor RFBs. All SUFB (A) or all RFB (B) one size fits all decisions ignore local drivers. Location alone (D) is insufficient without costs. Sticker price (E) misses life cycle costs—capital, repairs, consumables, reprocessing labor, and infection related costs. The chapter’s cost effectiveness section underscores that each hospital has its own “break even” and that modeling supports rational procurement. 
Single Use Bronchoscopy
Pearl: Volume drives value—use your numbers with a model to choose a mixed SUFB/RFB fleet.
###References###
1.	Edenharter GM, et al. Decision support to optimize the bronchoscope mix. Anesth Analg. 2017;124:1963 1969. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28221228/
2.	McCahon RA, Whynes DK. Cost comparison of reusable vs single use fibrescopes. Anaesthesia. 2015;70:699 706. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25858454/
3.	Perbet S, et al. Cost analysis of single use vs reusable bronchoscopes in the ICU. Ann Intensive Care. 2017;7:3. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28078466/

### Question  ###
A 58 year old woman with symptomatic endobronchial tumor (obstructing the right main bronchus) is scheduled for argon plasma coagulation (APC) with mechanical debulking and balloon dilation under general anesthesia. After hours, no sterile processing staff are available; SUFBs and RFBs are both on hand. The team asks whether to perform this advanced “hot” therapeutic bronchoscopy with a SUFB. What is the best plan?
 ### Options### 
(a) Use a reusable flexible bronchoscope for this advanced thermal case; reserve SUFBs for routine inspection, BAL, and standard endobronchial biopsy
(b) Use a SUFB because it is sterile and avoids reprocessing
(c) Delay the procedure until morning to ensure reprocessing availability
(d) Proceed with SUFB only if a trainee operates to minimize RFB damage
(e) Use SUFB now and re use it later on the same patient after saline flushing
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice A correct. While the latest generation SUFBs perform non inferiorly to RFBs for routine bronchoscopy, BAL, and standard endobronchial biopsy, robust comparative clinical data for advanced therapeutic (“hot”) interventions remain limited to bench/cadaver evaluations. In this urgent APC case, an RFB is the prudent choice for instrument performance, durability, and established experience. He et al. showed SUFB noninferiority for routine diagnostic tasks, supporting their use for BAL/biopsy—but not as definitive evidence for complex energy based therapy. Bench work (e.g., tool interaction testing) suggests feasibility, yet clinical evidence is still maturing. 
Single Use Bronchoscopy
Sterility (B) alone does not substitute for proven performance in thermal therapy. Delaying care (C) risks clinical deterioration. Training plans (D) are unrelated to instrument selection. Re using a SUFB (E) is unsafe—significant microbial colonization occurs even after simple cleaning; SUFBs are single use only. 
Single Use Bronchoscopy
Pitfall: Do not extrapolate SUFB noninferiority in routine tasks to energy based therapeutic bronchoscopy without supporting clinical data.
###References###
1.	He S, et al. Single use vs reusable flexible bronchoscopes: prospective controlled study. BMC Pulm Med. 2023;23:202. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/37288664/
2.	Lamb CR, et al. Performance of a new single use bronchoscope vs comparator (bench study). BMC Pulm Med. 2022;22:189. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/35618127/
3.	McGrath BA, et al. Contamination of single use bronchoscopes after basic cleaning. Anaesthesia. 2017;72(Suppl 1):36 41. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28097767/

### Question  ###
Your EBUS program is launching a quality dashboard. To enable fair benchmarking of diagnostic performance and complication rates across operators and centers, you are building a standardized procedure report template. Which reporting element is most critical to allow meaningful comparison of diagnostic sensitivity across cases?
 ### Options### 
(a) Exact milligrams of sedatives and anesthetic agents used
(b) Number of passes per nodal station only
(c) Whether ROSE was used during the case
(d) The clinical indication and radiographic context (e.g., diagnostic sampling vs systematic staging; cN0/1 vs cN2/3)
(e) The brand and gauge of the aspiration needle
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice D correct. EBUS TBNA sensitivity varies by indication and disease prevalence—it is lower in cN0/1 staging populations than in cohorts with radiographically abnormal nodes targeted for diagnosis. Therefore, standardized reports must capture indication and clinical/radiographic context to permit apples to apples comparisons. The WABIP consensus on EBUS quality indicators and reporting explicitly prioritizes indication and staging context as core data elements, and meta analytic data demonstrate the prevalence effect on sensitivity. 
Sedation doses (A), ROSE use (C), and needle details (E) are relevant process variables but do not, by themselves, allow performance normalization across fundamentally different case mixes. Pass counts alone (B) omit the clinical denominator that most strongly shifts expected sensitivity. Pearl: Always stratify EBUS outcomes by indication—diagnostic vs staging and cN category drive achievable sensitivity.
###References###
1.	Steinfort DP, Evison M, et al. WABIP consensus on EBUS quality indicators & standard reporting items. Respirology. 2023;28(8):722 743. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/37169888/
2.	Leong TL, et al. Preoperative EBUS staging in cN0/N1 lung cancer: systematic review/meta analysis. J Bronchol Interv Pulmonol. 2019;26(3):155 165. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/31425260/
3.	Silvestri GA, et al. ACCP guideline—methods for staging NSCLC. Chest. 2013;143(5 Suppl):e211S e250S. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23649440/

### Question  ###
A busy IP unit audits complications after bronchoscopy and EBUS. The database reliably captures immediate events (e.g., hypoxemia, bleeding) documented in procedure notes, but a chart review reveals post discharge events (pneumonia, ED visits) are inconsistently recorded. The director asks how to improve adverse event (AE) capture for quality monitoring without overburdening staff. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Rely on voluntary emails from clinicians about complications
(b) Only count events during the procedure; delayed AEs are not attributable
(c) Increase sedation documentation detail instead
(d) Review 5 randomly selected cases monthly
(e) Implement standardized AE definitions and active postprocedure follow up (e.g., phone calls/EMR query at a fixed interval) to capture delayed AEs
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice E correct. High quality performance monitoring requires standardized AE definitions and active surveillance beyond the procedure day to identify delayed events that otherwise go unrecorded. GI endoscopy quality frameworks (adopted broadly for endoscopic quality) emphasize structured AE definitions, time windows, and proactive follow up mechanisms; similar principles apply to bronchoscopy/EBUS programs and are advocated in quality indicator statements. 
Relying on ad hoc clinician reports (A) misses many events; confining AE capture to intraprocedural events (B) ignores clinically meaningful harms. Better sedation documentation (C) improves process detail, not AE ascertainment. Small haphazard samples (D) are underpowered and biased. Implementing an AE follow up workflow (e.g., 7–14 day calls or EMR queries tied to diagnosis codes) enables reliable, comparable complication rates for audit and feedback. Pearl: Measure what matters—define AEs up front and look for them after discharge.
###References###
1.	Rizk MK, et al. Quality indicators common to endoscopic procedures. Gastrointest Endosc. 2015;81(1):3 16. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25480096/
2.	Steinfort DP, Evison M. Quality indicators & performance monitoring in IP (reviewed principles). Respirology. 2023;28(8):722 743. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/37169888/
________________________________________
### Question  ###
A 64 year old man (former 35 pack year smoker) has a 1.2 cm lobulated solid nodule in the right upper lobe (no bronchus sign) with Brock pCA 20%. He is hesitant about procedures but wants to avoid missing a cancer. FDG PET/CT is indeterminate (SUVmax 2.3). A nasal epithelial cell genomic classifier returns “high risk.” He is off anticoagulants; PFTs are normal. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Annual CT only; PET was indeterminate
(b) Short interval CT for 3–6 months to “watch it”
(c) Proceed to tissue diagnosis (percutaneous vs bronchoscopic based on anatomy)
(d) Repeat PET/CT in 6–8 weeks to confirm
(e) Serum proteomic (LG3BP/C163A) test to “double check”
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice C correct. A high risk nasal genomic classifier (validated in smokers/ex smokers presenting with pulmonary nodules) functions as a rule in signal (reported high specificity) and should escalate management toward tissue diagnosis when pretest risk is intermediate. In contrast, the low risk result performs as a rule out (high sensitivity) to support surveillance. Thus, with intermediate pCA and a positive “rule in” test, pursuing biopsy is appropriate; the route depends on lesion anatomy (pleural adjacency/bronchus sign). PET repetition (D) adds little after an indeterminate study. Annual (A) or short interval (B) CT ignores the available rule in signal and may delay diagnosis. The proteomic classifier (E) is designed for rule out in intermediate risk nodules when negative; a second biomarker is unlikely to clarify management after a positive rule in result. The chapter summarizes the performance characteristics and intended uses of these biomarkers. 
Pearl: Use biomarkers for what they’re built to do—rule in pushes you to biopsy; rule out supports surveillance.
###References###
1.	Lamb CR, et al. A nasal swab classifier to evaluate the probability of lung cancer in pulmonary nodules. Chest. 2024;165(4):1009 1019. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/38721977/
2.	Silvestri GA, et al. PANOPTIC proteomic classifier trial. Chest. 2018;154(3):491 500. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29496499/
________________________________________
### Question  ###
An 82 year old man (ex smoker) with severe COPD (FEV₁ 0.8 L, 32% predicted), CAD with recent drug eluting stent on dual antiplatelet therapy, and CKD stage 4 has a new 2.4 cm spiculated RUL nodule on 1 mm CT with an upper lobe bronchus sign. PET/CT shows no distant disease. He is inoperable and at prohibitive risk for invasive biopsy under sedation. He and his family are anxious to treat if likely malignant. What is the most appropriate management now?
 ### Options### 
(a) Navigational bronchoscopy despite risk—tissue is mandatory
(b) Multidisciplinary decision to proceed with stereotactic body radiotherapy (SBRT) without tissue confirmation, after staging
(c) CT guided biopsy followed by outpatient observation
(d) Annual CT only; the risks of action outweigh benefits
(e) Empiric antibiotics then repeat CT in 4 weeks
###Discussion###
Bottom line—choice B correct. When a patient has a high pretest probability malignant lung nodule but is not a candidate for surgery or invasive biopsy due to prohibitive risk, it is reasonable—after multidisciplinary review and appropriate staging—to proceed with SBRT without tissue confirmation. Population level data document increasing use of SBRT without prior biopsy in such contexts, and contemporary multidisciplinary frameworks endorse individualized decisions balancing procedural risk, comorbidity, and likelihood of malignancy. The chapter notes SBRT may be offered when surgery is not feasible and biopsy is not possible/nondiagnostic. 
Forcing bronchoscopy (A) or TTNB (implicit in C) disregards the patient’s risk profile. Annual CT alone (D) risks undertreating a likely stage I lung cancer with a curative option available. Empiric antibiotics (E) are not indicated for a chronic spiculated upper lobe nodule without infectious features. Pearl: In prohibitive risk patients with high pCA nodules, SBRT without tissue may be appropriate after MDT review and staging.
###References###
1.	Rutter CE, et al. Increase in use of lung SBRT without preceding biopsy in the U.S. Lung Cancer. 2014;85(3):390 394. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24995607/
2.	Mankidy BJ, et al. High risk lung nodule: multidisciplinary diagnosis and management. Respir Med. 2023;214:107277. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/37466863/
### Question  ###
A 78 year old man (60 pack year smoker, severe emphysema on 2 L/min home O₂) has a biopsy proven 2.1 cm right upper lobe peripheral adenocarcinoma planned for stereotactic body radiotherapy (SBRT). Thin section CT (1.0 mm) shows the lesion 18 mm from pleura without endobronchial connection. Platelets 230 × 10⁹/L, INR 1.0. The radiation oncologist requests fiducials for real time tracking. Which fiducial placement approach best minimizes pneumothorax risk while achieving high technical success in this patient?
 ### Options### 
(a) CT guided percutaneous placement of smooth cylindrical gold seeds directly into the tumor
(b) Percutaneous endovascular coil deployment via segmental pulmonary artery access
(c) Electromagnetic navigation bronchoscopy–guided placement of endobronchial coil fiducials into/adjacent to the lesion
(d) Surgical wedge resection to place sutured radiopaque clips on the pleura
(e) Endoscopic ultrasound (EUS)–guided placement through the esophageal wall
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In a patient with severe emphysema, choice (c)—bronchoscopic, navigation guided placement of endobronchial coil fiducials—offers high success with substantially lower pneumothorax risk than transthoracic routes. In the NAVIGATE prospective cohort, ENB guided fiducial placement achieved high technical success with low pneumothorax rates, supporting a bronchoscopic approach when percutaneous risk is high (Bowling et al.). Transthoracic fiducial implantation, while effective, carries appreciable pneumothorax and hemorrhage risks—particularly problematic in emphysema—across multiple series (Yousefi et al.; Kothary et al.). Endobronchial placement also enables favorable geometry (multiple non collinear markers) for tracking as emphasized in multimodality guidance reports (Steinfort et al.). Pitfall: assuming all fiducials behave similarly—coil type markers conform and anchor within small airways and show better stability than smooth seeds in several series.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Percutaneous smooth seeds are effective but have higher pneumothorax risk in emphysema and are more migration prone than coils. (b) Endovascular coils are an option in select centers but require interventional radiology expertise and are not first line for most lung SBRT programs. (d) Surgical clip placement changes the treatment plan (surgery rather than SBRT) and is unnecessary solely for fiducial needs. (e) EUS guided placement is described for mediastinal targets but not standard for peripheral lung fiducials and may not reach the RUL lesion.
###References###
1.	Bowling MR, Folch EE, Khandhar SJ, et al. Fiducial marker placement with electromagnetic navigation bronchoscopy: a NAVIGATE subgroup analysis. Ther Adv Respir Dis. 2019;13:1753466619841234. 
2.	Yousefi S, Collins BT, Reichner CA, et al. Complications of thoracic CT guided fiducial placement for cyberknife radiotherapy. Clin Lung Cancer. 2007;8(4):252 256. 
3.	Kothary N, Dieterich S, Louie JD, et al. Percutaneous implantation of fiducial markers for imaging guided radiation therapy. AJR Am J Roentgenol. 2009;192(4):1090 1096. 
4.	Steinfort DP, Siva S, Kron T, et al. Multimodality guidance for accurate bronchoscopic insertion of fiducial markers. J Thorac Oncol. 2015;10(2):324 330.

### Question  ###
A 62 year old woman (never smoker) is scheduled for a robotic left upper lobe segmentectomy tomorrow for an 8 mm part solid nodule 18 mm from the pleura on thin section CT (1.0 mm). The OR has near infrared fluorescence capability. She has normal platelets and INR. You are asked to localize the nodule bronchoscopically. Which strategy best facilitates precise sublobar resection with adequate margins?
 ### Options### 
(a) Single site indigo carmine injection via conventional bronchoscopy
(b) Multispot bronchoscopic mapping using indocyanine green plus indigo carmine (dual stain VAL MAP)
(c) Hookwire placed percutaneously on day of surgery
(d) Radiotracer injection for gamma probe localization only
(e) Endobronchial lipiodol injection without fluorescence capability
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (b) is best. Dual stain virtual assisted lung mapping (VAL MAP) using ICG plus indigo carmine enhances marking detectability versus indigo alone and supports adequate margins for sublobar resections (Yanagiya et al.). The multicenter experience with VAL MAP shows high rates of successful resection and identification for small/impalpable lesions, improving margin planning (Sato et al.). ENB guided dye marking series also report feasibility and safety for minimally invasive resection (Awais et al.).
Why others are incorrect: (a) Single indigo injection alone is more prone to fading and lower detectability than dual stain approaches. (c) Hookwire localization is effective but carries risks (pneumothorax, dislodgement) and doesn’t leverage the available NIR system. (d) Radiotracer only strategies help when fluoroscopy/gamma probe workflows are planned but do not provide surface mapping for planned segmentectomy planes. (e) Lipiodol is fluoroscopy dependent and offers no NIR fluorescence; without routine intraoperative fluoroscopy, it is less helpful.
###References###
1.	Yanagiya M, Sato M, Ijiri N, et al. Virtual assisted lung mapping using dual staining with ICG and indigo carmine enhanced marking detectability. J Thorac Dis. 2022;14(4):1061 1069. 
2.	Sato M, Kobayashi M, Kojima F, et al. Effect of VAL MAP on acquisition of surgical margins in sublobar resection. J Thorac Cardiovasc Surg. 2018;156(4):1691 1701.e5. 
3.	Awais O, Reidy MR, Mehta K, et al. Electromagnetic navigation bronchoscopy–guided dye marking for thoracoscopic resection. Ann Thorac Surg. 2016;102(1):223 229.

### Question  ###
A radiation physicist requests your plan for fiducial configuration to enable robust real time tracking during SBRT of a 2.3 cm RLL lesion. You can place 3–4 bronchoscopic fiducials around the lesion using ENB. Which layout is optimal for consistent tracking?
 ### Options### 
(a) Two markers placed collinearly along a single subsegmental bronchus
(b) Three markers all within 5 mm of each other at the tumor center
(c) Three markers placed in one straight line spanning the lesion
(d) Four markers confined to one airway branch proximal to the tumor
(e) Three markers placed non collinearly bracketing the lesion in different planes
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (e)—placing ≥3 fiducials non collinearly to bracket the target—is preferred to support stable triangulation and rotational tracking. Reviews and multimodality guidance emphasize marker geometry (not just number) for bronchoscopically placed fiducials; non collinear spacing around the target improves stability and tracking performance (Casutt et al.; Steinfort et al.). The NAVIGATE analysis also supports bronchoscopic approaches with high placement success when multiple fiducials are deployed (Bowling et al.).
Why others are incorrect: (a) Collinear markers limit rotational information. (b) Clustering centrally increases overlap and reduces tracking robustness. (c) A single straight line provides poor 3 D triangulation. (d) Confined proximal placement risks parallax/motion mismatch from the target.
###References###
1.	Casutt A, Kinj R, Ozsahin E M, et al. Fiducial markers for stereotactic lung RT: transthoracic, endovascular, and endobronchial approaches (review). Eur Respir Rev. 2022;31:210149. 
2.	Steinfort DP, Siva S, Kron T, et al. Multimodality guidance for accurate bronchoscopic insertion of fiducial markers. J Thorac Oncol. 2015;10(2):324 330. 
3.	Bowling MR, Folch EE, Khandhar SJ, et al. ENB guided fiducial placement (NAVIGATE subgroup). Ther Adv Respir Dis. 2019;13:1753466619841234.

### Question  ###
A 66 year old man with COPD has an 18 mm peripheral nodule in the posterior segment of the right upper lobe, 17 mm from pleura. Thin section CT (1.0 mm) shows no bronchus sign. Radial EBUS images are nondiagnostic. You plan a bronchoscopic procedure under general anesthesia with fluoroscopy available. Which approach is most likely to obtain tissue by circumventing the absent bronchus sign?
 ### Options### 
(a) Bronchoscopic transparenchymal nodule access (BTPNA) using virtual planning with vessel mapping and fused fluoroscopy
(b) Repeat radial EBUS with transbronchial brushings alone
(c) Traditional ENB guided forceps biopsy despite lack of airway leading to lesion
(d) Proceed directly to VATS wedge without diagnosis
(e) Percutaneous transthoracic needle aspiration via CT guidance from bronchoscopy suite
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (a) is best. First in human experience established the feasibility of BTPNA, which creates a planned transparenchymal tunnel to the lesion under image guidance (Herth et al.). Subsequent work integrating vessel mapping and fused fluoroscopy demonstrated safe, effective access via a planned point of entry to biopsy peripheral lesions independent of bronchus sign (Sun et al.). When no airway connects to the target, a planned transparenchymal route overcomes the key limitation of conventional guided bronchoscopy.
Why others are incorrect: (b) Brushings alone have low yield for small peripheral nodules lacking an airway route. (c) ENB guided forceps still depends on an airway leading to (or very near) the target; absent bronchus sign reduces success. (d) Proceeding to surgery without diagnosis may be unnecessary in a medically operable patient when bronchoscopic diagnosis is feasible. (e) TTNA may be diagnostic but carries higher pneumothorax risk in COPD; the ### Question  ### asks for a bronchoscopic approach overcoming airway constraints.
###References###
1.	Herth FJF, Eberhardt R, Sterman DH, et al. BTPNA: first in human trial. Thorax. 2015;70(4):326 332. 
2.	Sun J, Criner GJ, Shojaee S, et al. Virtual bronchoscopic navigation with fused fluoroscopy and vessel mapping for access to pulmonary lesions. Respirology. 2022;27(5):357 365.

### Question  ###
A 32 year old woman with treated pulmonary tuberculosis has dyspnea and left lung volume loss. Bronchoscopy shows complete occlusion of the left main bronchus; attempts at guidewire passage fail. CT angiography shows no adjacent large vessels along a short parenchymal route. Which intervention is most appropriate to re establish airway continuity?
 ### Options### 
(a) Rigid bronchoscopy with Nd:YAG laser coring through the occlusion
(b) Cryosurgical recanalization via the occluded segment alone
(c) Surgical left main bronchus reconstruction
(d) BTPNA guided recanalization creating an avascular tunnel to the distal airway, followed by stenting
(e) Endobronchial valve placement distal to the occlusion
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d) is supported by case experience using BTPNA to safely perforate an occluded main bronchus from a proximal airway into the distal lumen along an avascular path, restoring continuity and enabling stenting (Jing et al.). Virtual planning with vessel mapping helps select a safe point of entry and trajectory (Sun et al.). This approach is particularly useful when the occlusion is dense and cannot be traversed endoluminally.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Laser coring requires intraluminal access and risks perforation when the distal lumen cannot be identified. (b) Cryo alone is limited by lack of a luminal target. (c) Surgical reconstruction is more invasive and may be unnecessary if bronchoscopic recanalization is feasible. (e) Valves treat air leaks/emphysema but do not re establish a blocked airway.
###References###
1.	Jing Q, Xu D, Li S, et al. Application of BTPNA in tuberculous bronchial occlusion. Clin Respir J. 2022;16(12):842 848. 
2.	Sun J, Criner GJ, Shojaee S, et al. VBN with vessel mapping and fused fluoroscopy for lesion access. Respirology. 2022;27(5):357 365.

### Question  ###
A 69 year old man with severe coronary disease (LVEF 25%) has a 1.2 cm right lower lobe lesion 20 mm from the pleura. He is not a candidate for surgery or SBRT due to prior high dose overlap. You intend bronchoscopic ablation under general anesthesia. Which approach is supported by in vivo preclinical data for precise energy delivery through a bronchoscopic transparenchymal tunnel?
 ### Options### 
(a) Microwave ablation via standard transbronchial forceps channel without tunnel creation
(b) Radiofrequency ablation delivered through a BTPNA sheath (BTPNA guided RFA)
(c) Photodynamic therapy to the adjacent segmental bronchus only
(d) Ethanol injection via radial EBUS needle into the lesion
(e) Cryoablation through a standard transbronchial cryoprobe without navigation
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (b) is supported by a canine study demonstrating the feasibility and safety of RFA delivered via a BTPNA tunnel, achieving controlled ablation zones with defined energy settings and histologic healing over time (Zhong et al.). Human feasibility of BTPNA has been established (Herth et al.), providing the platform for such tunneled therapies.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Microwave via a standard forceps channel cannot reliably reach a parenchymal target off the airway without a tunnel. (c) Photodynamic therapy is luminal and not designed for deep parenchymal nodules. (d) Ethanol injection lacks robust supporting data and risks unpredictable spread. (e) Standard cryoprobes are straight and airway confined, limiting reach to off airway targets.
###References###
1.	Zhong CH, Wei YF, Zhu YZ, et al. RFA guided by BTPNA in canines: feasibility and safety. Respiration. 2021;100(11):1097 1104. 
2.	Herth FJF, Eberhardt R, Sterman DH, et al. BTPNA first in human trial. Thorax. 2015;70(4):326 332.

### Question  ###
A 74 year old man with a 4.5 cm left hilar squamous cell carcinoma is referred for SBRT because of prohibitive surgical risk. Thin section CT (0.75 mm) shows tumor abutting the left main bronchus and pulmonary artery. Which fiducial strategy is most appropriate to safely place markers for tracking?
 ### Options### 
(a) Convex probe EBUS–guided fiducial marker placement into the central tumor via a 19 gauge needle under real time ultrasound
(b) ENB guided fiducials into distal subsegmental bronchi of the left upper lobe
(c) CT guided percutaneous fiducials through the fissure into the hilum
(d) Endovascular coil deployment into a segmental pulmonary artery
(e) Hookwire placement for intraoperative fluoroscopic localization
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (a)—CP EBUS–guided fiducial placement—is supported for central thoracic lesions, enabling real time vascular avoidance and precise deployment within or adjacent to the mass (Seides et al.; Harley et al.). In several case series, CP EBUS safely placed fiducials to facilitate SBRT when percutaneous routes were hazardous.
Why others are incorrect: (b) Distal ENB placement may be too far from the central target, creating geometric mismatch. (c) Transthoracic access to the hilum has high bleeding and pneumothorax risk. (d) Endovascular approaches exist but are not standard and require interventional radiology; coils in arteries may not represent tumor motion. (e) Hookwires are for surgical resection, not SBRT tracking.
###References###
1.	Seides BJ, Egan JP III, French KD, et al. Fiducial placement via convex probe EBUS for SBRT: case series and review. J Thorac Dis. 2018;10(3):1972 1983. 
2.	Harley DP, Krimsky WS, Sarkar S, et al. Fiducials via EBUS/navigational bronchoscopy for stereotactic radiosurgery. Ann Thorac Surg. 2010;89(2):368 373.

### Question  ###
A 58 year old woman scheduled for a right upper lobe segmentectomy has a 9 mm pure ground glass nodule 22 mm deep on thin section CT (1.0 mm). Prior single dye markings have faded intraoperatively. The team has near infrared capability. Which bronchoscopic injection strategy best improves intraoperative marking visibility?
 ### Options### 
(a) Single 1.0 mL indocyanine green (ICG) injection at one site without mapping
(b) CT guided hookwire placed just before surgery
(c) Fluoroscopy assisted bronchoscopic microcoil alone
(d) Dual stain VAL MAP using ICG plus indigo carmine at multiple planned sites
(e) Endobronchial lipiodol injection without fluorescence imaging
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d) is favored. Dual stain VAL MAP (ICG + indigo carmine) increases detectable markings compared with indigo alone and supports accurate sublobar resections (Yanagiya et al.). Multicenter VAL MAP data show high lesion identification and margin adequacy, particularly for small or subsolid lesions (Sato et al.). ENB guided dye marking series further support feasibility and safety (Awais et al.).
Why others are incorrect: (a) Single site ICG without a mapping strategy risks inadequate guidance; multiple spots guide resection lines. (b) Hookwires work but risk dislodgement/pneumothorax and do not exploit NIR. (c) Microcoils require intraoperative fluoroscopy, not planned here. (e) Lipiodol is radiopaque (fluoroscopy dependent) and lacks fluorescence.
###References###
1.	Yanagiya M, Sato M, Ijiri N, et al. Dual stain VAL MAP with ICG and indigo carmine. J Thorac Dis. 2022;14(4):1061 1069. 
2.	Sato M, Kobayashi M, Kojima F, et al. VAL MAP for margin acquisition. J Thorac Cardiovasc Surg. 2018;156(4):1691 1701.e5. 
3.	Awais O, Reidy MR, Mehta K, et al. ENB guided dye marking for VATS. Ann Thorac Surg. 2016;102(1):223 229.

### Question  ###
A 70 year old man underwent ENB guided placement of four endobronchial coil fiducials around a 2 cm right lower lobe tumor for upcoming SBRT. He is stable post procedure. When should confirmatory imaging be obtained to verify fiducial stability before treatment planning?
 ### Options### 
(a) Same day radiograph only
(b) Next day cone beam CT in the OR
(c) At 6–8 weeks to ensure mature fibrosis
(d) Approximately 1–2 weeks after placement, prior to simulation
(e) No confirmatory imaging is necessary
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (d) is appropriate. Early experience with bronchoscopic fiducial placement incorporated a follow up CT about 2 weeks after deployment to verify stability before proceeding with radiosurgery (Harley et al.). Broader fiducial literature emphasizes that migration, when it occurs, is typically early, and early confirmatory imaging prior to simulation is standard practice (Kothary et al.; reviews). Pearl: verifying stability before simulation avoids replanning and treatment delays.
Why others are incorrect: (a) A same day radiograph may not detect migration over time. (b) Next day imaging can miss early migration that occurs over several days. (c) Waiting 6–8 weeks unnecessarily delays therapy. (e) Skipping confirmation risks planning on unstable markers.
###References###
1.	Harley DP, Krimsky WS, Sarkar S, et al. EBUS/navigational bronchoscopy fiducials with 2 week CT verification. Ann Thorac Surg. 2010;89(2):368 373. 
2.	Kothary N, Dieterich S, Louie JD, et al. Percutaneous fiducial implantation: safety and technical success. AJR Am J Roentgenol. 2009;192(4):1090 1096. 
3.	Casutt A, Kinj R, Ozsahin E M, et al. Fiducial markers for stereotactic lung RT (review). Eur Respir Rev. 2022;31:210149.
