### Question  ###
A 67 year old man with COPD is scheduled for VATS wedge resection of a 12 mm nodule 25 mm deep in the left lower lobe. The IR CT suite is unavailable on the day of surgery, and the OR does not have intraoperative fluoroscopy, but a handheld gamma probe is available. Which localization method is most appropriate?
 ### Options### 
(a) CT guided hookwire on the morning of surgery
(b) Bronchoscopic lipiodol injection the day before surgery
(c) Preoperative radiotracer injection (e.g., 99mTc) with intraoperative gamma probe detection
(d) Bronchoscopic metallic microcoil placement the day before surgery
(e) No localization needed for a 12 mm lesion
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choice (c) fits the constraints. Image guided radiotracer localization allows reliable intraoperative gamma probe detection without fluoroscopy, facilitating resection of small/deep lesions (Radiographics review, McDermott et al.). Lipiodol and microcoils are radiopaque and require fluoroscopy. Hookwires are effective but can dislodge and also typically rely on immediate surgical transfer; the absence of fluoroscopy does not help with deep lesions. For a 12 mm deep lesion, “no localization” risks conversion or excessive resection.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Hookwire dislodgement risk and lack of adjunctive imaging undermine reliability. (b) Lipiodol needs fluoroscopy; not useful here. (d) Microcoils require fluoroscopy to visualize. (e) Small, deep nodules are often impalpable; localization improves success and limits margins.
###References###
1.	McDermott S, Fintelmann FJ, Bierhals AJ, et al. Image guided preoperative localization of pulmonary nodules. Radiographics. 2019;39(5):1264 1279. 
2.	Suzuki K, Shimohira M, Hashizume T, et al. CT guided hookwire marking before VATS: usefulness and rare severe complications. J Med Imaging Radiat Oncol. 2014;58(6):657 662. 
3.	Rose M, Siva S, Ball D, et al. Bronchoscopic lipiodol as a fiducial marker (fluoroscopy dependent). J Thorac Oncol. 2014;9(10):1579 1583. 

Other
### Question  ###
A 67 year old man with GOLD D COPD (60 pack year former smoker) has 3 moderate exacerbations in the past year despite adherence to LAMA/LABA/ICS. Thin section CT (≤1.25 mm) shows no large bullae and anatomy suitable for catheter access. Under general anesthesia you plan a single session bronchoscopic procedure using a dual cooled radiofrequency catheter in both main bronchi. Which mechanism best explains the expected clinical benefit of this therapy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Ablation of sympathetic chain fibers to reduce catecholamine mediated inflammation
(b) Up regulation of bronchial β₂ adrenergic receptors to enhance bronchodilator response
(c) Ablation of parasympathetic (vagal) peribronchial fibers to reduce cholinergic tone and mucus hypersecretion
(d) Selective destruction of airway C fibers to blunt the cough reflex
(e) Depletion of alveolar macrophages to reduce neutrophilic elastase burden
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Targeted lung denervation (TLD) reduces cholinergic tone by ablating peribronchial vagal fibers—choice (c) correct. Cholinergic pathways drive bronchoconstriction, mucus production, and exacerbation propensity in COPD; blocking muscarinic signaling pharmacologically or interrupting vagal efferents mechanistically reduces these effects. Foundational work established the centrality of airway cholinergic pathways in COPD pathobiology, supporting why vagal denervation should lower exacerbations without necessarily yielding large spirometric gains. Prospective human studies—including randomized, sham controlled trials—used a dual cooled RF catheter to thermally disrupt peribronchial parasympathetic fibers and reported acceptable safety with signals for fewer exacerbations over time. The anti inflammatory signal (e.g., reduced airway cytokines in bronchial samples) further corroborates a vagal tone mechanism. Pitfall: assuming benefits arise from sympathetic ablation or β₂ receptor modulation—TLD acts on cholinergic pathways, not adrenergic ones.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Sympathetic ablation would worsen bronchomotor tone; studies target parasympathetic fibers. (b) β₂ receptor up regulation is not the described mechanism of RF denervation. (d) C fiber effects are not the target in TLD trials. (e) Macrophage depletion is not a mechanism or goal of the procedure. (See the device level schematic reproduced in the chapter for orientation; evidence below derives from the peer reviewed studies.) 
Emerging Bronchoscopic Therapies
###References###
1.	Belmonte KE. Cholinergic pathways in the lungs and anticholinergic therapy for COPD. Proc Am Thorac Soc. 2005;2:297–304. https://doi.org/10.1513/pats.200504-043SR
2.	Kistemaker LEM, Slebos D J, Meurs H, et al. Anti inflammatory effects of TLD in COPD. Eur Respir J. 2015;46:1489–92. https://doi.org/10.1183/13993003.00413-2015
3.	Slebos D J, Shah PL, Herth FJF, et al. Safety/adverse events after TLD (AIRFLOW). Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2019;200:1477–86. https://doi.org/10.1164/rccm.201903-0624OC
### Question  ###
A 61 year old man with moderate–severe COPD (two prednisone treated exacerbations in 10 months) undergoes targeted lung denervation using a dual cooled radiofrequency catheter after CT confirms suitable main bronchial diameters. Which outcome is most consistent with randomized, sham controlled and follow up data for this procedure?
 ### Options### 
(a) Sustained ≥15% increase in FEV₁ at 12 and 24 months
(b) Significant improvement in DLCO within 3 months
(c) Radiographic reversal of emphysema on CT quantification
(d) Reduction in moderate–severe exacerbations and prolongation of time to first exacerbation over up to 2 years
(e) Marked reduction in hospitalization length for unrelated pneumonias
###Discussion###
Bottom line: The strongest replicated clinical signal for TLD is fewer COPD exacerbations and a longer time to first exacerbation—choice (d) correct. AIRFLOW 2, a randomized, double blind sham controlled trial with 2 year outcomes, showed fewer moderate–severe exacerbations and prolonged time to first event in treated patients; crossover analyses confirmed similar trends. Lung function and health status tended to remain stable rather than dramatically improve, which is still meaningful in a progressive disease. Early RCT data established an acceptable safety profile while highlighting gastrointestinal events that subsequent procedural refinements sought to mitigate. Pearl: In TLD evidence, exacerbation reduction—not large spirometric gains—is the key effect size to remember.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Large, durable FEV₁ jumps were not the primary signal. (b) DLCO changes are not a consistent finding. (c) TLD does not reverse structural emphysema. (e) Hospitalization metrics for unrelated infections are not the principal outcome reported.
###References###
1.	Valipour A, Shah PL, Herth FJ, et al. Two year outcomes of AIRFLOW 2. Int J Chron Obstruct Pulmon Dis. 2020;15:2807–16. https://doi.org/10.2147/COPD.S267409
2.	Conway F, Tonkin J, Valipour A, et al. Crossover outcomes in AIRFLOW 2. Respiration. 2022;101:1069–74. https://doi.org/10.1159/000527455
3.	Slebos D J, Shah PL, Herth FJF, et al. Safety/adverse events after TLD (AIRFLOW). Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2019;200:1477–86. https://doi.org/10.1164/rccm.201903-0624OC
### Question  ###
A 66 year old woman with chronic bronchitic COPD (persistent cough/sputum despite optimized inhalers) elects bronchial rheoplasty. Under general anesthesia, a catheter delivers nonthermal, high frequency pulsed electrical energy segment by segment. Which complication is most commonly reported in prospective rheoplasty studies?
 ### Options### 
(a) Immediate tracheal perforation requiring stent placement
(b) COPD exacerbation within the peri procedural period
(c) Persistent left vocal cord paralysis
(d) Massive hemoptysis requiring embolization
(e) Air embolism with stroke
###Discussion###
Bottom line: The most common adverse event after bronchial rheoplasty is COPD exacerbation—choice (b) correct. In a multicenter clinical trial, rheoplasty—designed to reduce goblet cell hyperplasia—was feasible and generally safe, with improvements in symptom scores (SGRQ, CAT). However, COPD exacerbation emerged as the most frequent adverse event; pneumonia also occurred, particularly in longer follow up cohorts. Two year U.S. feasibility data similarly reported COPD exacerbations and pneumonia as the main serious adverse events, with some attenuation of benefit over time. Pitfall: confusing rheoplasty with thermal ablation or cryotherapy—its nonthermal, pulsed energy mechanism preserves extracellular matrix, enabling epithelial regeneration while targeting goblet cells.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Tracheal perforation is not a characteristic complication in published series. (c) Recurrent laryngeal nerve injury is not a described pattern. (d) Life threatening hemoptysis is not commonly reported. (e) Air embolism is not a recognized signal in these studies.
###References###
1.	Valipour A, Fernandez Bussy S, Ing AJ, et al. Bronchial rheoplasty for chronic bronchitis: 12 month multicenter trial. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2020;202:681–9. https://doi.org/10.1164/rccm.201908-1546OC
2.	Sciurba FC, Dransfield MT, Kim V, et al. Rheoplasty: 2 year U.S. feasibility results. BMJ Open Respir Res. 2023;10:e001710. https://doi.org/10.1136/bmjresp-2023-001710
### Question  ###
A 59 year old man with chronic bronchitic COPD enrolls in a prospective study of metered liquid nitrogen cryospray to reduce cough and sputum burden. Pre /post treatment biopsies will assess tissue effects. Which histologic pattern is most consistent with pilot and feasibility data for this therapy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Full thickness airway necrosis extending through cartilage
(b) Chondrolysis with loss of structural support by 2 weeks
(c) Bronchiolar epithelial slough with transmural perforation risk
(d) Predominantly adventitial damage sparing mucosa
(e) Mucosal/submucosal cryonecrosis with preservation of cartilage and extracellular matrix
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Metered cryospray produces superficial injury—mucosal and submucosal cryonecrosis with cartilage preservation (choice e)—as shown in human pilot histology and clinical studies. Early work (low pressure spray cryotherapy) demonstrated that injury was limited to mucosa/submucosa without cartilage damage. Subsequent metered cryospray studies confirmed the targeted depth and reported acceptable safety with symptomatic improvements. The mechanism—rapid freezing of goblet cells while sparing deeper structural layers—aims to reset epithelial composition toward healthier mucosa. Pearl: Cartilage sparing is a key safety rationale enabling repeated segmental applications.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Full thickness necrosis through cartilage has not been reported in these series. (b) Chondrolysis is inconsistent with published histology. (c) Transmural perforation is not a characteristic effect. (d) The primary target is mucosa/submucosa—not the adventitia.
###References###
1.	Krimsky WS, Broussard JN, Sarkar SA, Harley DP. Spray cryotherapy: safety and depth of injury. J Thorac Cardiovasc Surg. 2010;139:781–2. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jtcvs.2009.03.051
2.	Slebos D J, Breen D, Coad J, et al. Histologic effect of liquid nitrogen metered spray cryotherapy. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2017;196:1351–2. https://doi.org/10.1164/rccm.201611-2220LE
3.	Garner JL, Shaipanich T, Hartman JE, et al. Prospective safety/feasibility of metered cryospray in CB COPD. Eur Respir J. 2020;56:2000556. https://doi.org/10.1183/13993003.00556-2020
### Question  ###
A 55 year old woman with frequent COPD exacerbations (≥2 in the past year) despite optimized inhaled therapy asks about targeted lung denervation. She has a prior diagnosis of adult onset asthma with reversible obstruction on outside spirometry. Which patient factor would exclude her based on key TLD trials?
 ### Options### 
(a) A concomitant diagnosis of asthma
(b) Current use of inhaled corticosteroids
(c) Active cigarette smoking
(d) Age younger than 60 years
(e) BMI 32 kg/m²
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Major TLD trials excluded patients with asthma—choice (a) correct—to focus on COPD without variable airflow obstruction confounders. Trial designs targeted moderate–severe COPD with frequent exacerbations on optimized therapy; they sought to isolate the effect of cholinergic denervation on COPD outcomes. Pitfall: assuming that any patient with chronic airway disease qualifies; patient selection in the evidence to date is restrictive, and extrapolation to asthma or asthma COPD overlap is unsupported by trial data.
Why others are incorrect: (b) ICS use was common and not an exclusion. (c) Some studies included current/former smokers; active smoking per se was not the defining exclusion in the core trials. (d) Age thresholds were not set at 60 years. (e) Obesity alone was not an exclusion criterion in the pivotal trials.
###References###
1.	Slebos D J, Shah PL, Herth FJF, et al. Safety/adverse events after TLD (AIRFLOW). Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2019;200:1477–86. https://doi.org/10.1164/rccm.201903-0624OC
2.	Valipour A, Shah PL, Herth FJ, et al. Two year outcomes of AIRFLOW 2. Int J Chron Obstruct Pulmon Dis. 2020;15:2807–16. https://doi.org/10.2147/COPD.S267409
### Question  ###
A 52 year old woman with debilitating tracheobronchomalacia after prolonged intubation has complex, noncylindric malacia spanning the lower trachea and proximal bronchi. After mechanical debulking/dilatation, you obtain a post debulking thin section CT for segmentation. Which stent strategy is most appropriate based on peer reviewed clinical experience?
 ### Options### 
(a) Place a standard straight silicone stent and accept a suboptimal fit
(b) Deploy a covered SEMS with maximal radial force to prevent migration
(c) Use two overlapping off the shelf Y stents to approximate anatomy
(d) Order a patient specific, 3 D engineered silicone stent designed from CT/CAD for her unique geometry
(e) Avoid stenting; 3 D devices remain experimental in animals only
###Discussion###
Bottom line: For anatomically complex central airway disease, a patient specific, 3 D engineered silicone stent has demonstrated feasibility and symptom/physiology improvement in proof of concept human studies—choice (d) correct. Using CT based modeling with CAD, customized stents can match noncylindric, branched, or tapered segments, improving fit where conventional cylinders perform poorly. In a prospective series of 10 adults with complex stenoses, 3 D stents fit well in most patients and improved dyspnea, quality of life, and spirometry, though complications (e.g., mucus plugging, migration, cough) still occurred and randomized trials are lacking. Pearl: Customization addresses geometry, but stents still behave as foreign bodies—granulation and mucus issues do not vanish with a perfect fit.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Accepting a poor fit risks migration, mucus trapping, and granulation. (b) Excess radial force does not solve shape mismatch and worsens tissue injury. (c) Overlapping off the shelf devices increase step offs and secretion stasis. (e) Human clinical experiences with customized stents are published; they are not confined to animal models. (See the chapter’s CAD/stent images for workflow context; evidence below comes from the cited peer reviewed reports.) 
###References###
1.	Guibert N, Didier A, Moreno B, et al. Patient specific 3 D engineered stents: proof of concept. Thorax. 2019;74:810–3.
2.	Freitag L, Gördes M, Zarogoulidis P, et al. Toward individualized tracheobronchial stents: considerations. Respiration. 2017;94:442–56.
### Question  ###
A 36 year old man with benign post intubation tracheal stenosis needs temporary scaffolding after dilatation. He lives remotely, making stent removal logistically difficult, and he has had granulation with prior silicone stents. Which airway prosthesis best aligns with his goals?
 ### Options### 
(a) A self expandable biodegradable polydioxanone (PDS) stent that gradually resorbs after weeks to months
(b) A fully covered self expandable metallic stent intended for permanent placement
(c) A standard straight silicone stent to be removed electively in 6–12 months
(d) A customized 3 D printed silicone Y stent for long term use
(e) A permanent uncovered metallic stent to allow epithelial ingrowth
###Discussion###
Bottom line: For temporary support when removal is problematic, a biodegradable PDS stent is appropriate—choice (a). Reviews and adult series support the feasibility and safety of bioresorbable airway stents that maintain patency initially and then degrade, obviating the need for removal. Adult cohorts report clinical effectiveness with acceptable adverse events profiles. Pearl: Biodegradable stents are attractive for benign disease requiring time limited scaffolding, especially when retrieval risk or logistics are unfavorable.
Why others are incorrect: (b) Permanent covered metal stents are discouraged in benign disease due to complications and the need for removal. (c) Silicone stents require planned removal, counter to the patient’s constraint. (d) Customized silicone still requires surveillance and removal. (e) Uncovered metal stents risk epithelial ingrowth and difficult extraction, particularly unsuitable for benign lesions.
###References###
1.	Dutau H, Musani AI, Laroumagne S, et al. Biodegradable airway stents—bench to bedside. Respiration. 2015;90:512–21.
2.	Stehlik L, Hytych V, Letackova J, et al. PDS stents in adult tracheal narrowing. BMC Pulm Med. 2015;15:164.
3.	Stehlik L, Guha D, Anandakumar S, et al. Ten year adult experience with biodegradable tracheal stents. BMC Pulm Med. 2024;24:238.
### Question  ###
A 67 year old woman with persistent malignant central airway obstruction from recurrent squamous cell carcinoma has tumor ingrowth through a covered stent despite radiation. She asks about stents that “carry chemotherapy.” Which experimental approach is most directly supported by peer reviewed preclinical studies?
 ### Options### 
(a) Nondegradable silicone stent eluting methotrexate for 12 months
(b) Biodegradable cisplatin eluting airway stent demonstrating sustained local drug release and antitumor effects in vivo
(c) Bare metal stent soaked in paclitaxel immediately before deployment
(d) Drug free biodegradable stent shown to shrink tumors via hydrolysis byproducts alone
(e) Sirolimus eluting laryngeal stent proven in randomized lung cancer trials
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Preclinical work supports biodegradable cisplatin eluting stents that release drug locally for weeks with antitumor effects—choice (b). Animal and bench studies have shown feasibility of drug eluting bioabsorbable platforms that combine temporary scaffolding with chemotherapeutic delivery. Additional laboratory/early translational work explores other agents (e.g., paclitaxel) and immunomodulators, but robust human cancer RCTs are lacking. Pitfall: extrapolating preclinical success to standard of care; drug eluting airway stents remain investigational without definitive clinical outcomes in lung cancer.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Long term methotrexate eluting silicone devices are not established. (c) Simple soaking of bare stents is not an evidence based, controlled release method. (d) Hydrolysis byproducts alone are not antitumor therapy. (e) Sirolimus eluting devices show promise in airway stenosis models, not proven lung cancer RCTs.
###References###
1.	Chao YK, Liu KS, Wang YC, et al. Biodegradable cisplatin eluting tracheal stent: in vivo and in vitro. Chest. 2013;144:193–9.
2.	Zhu GH, Ng AH, Venkatraman SS, et al. A bioabsorbable drug eluting tracheal stent. Laryngoscope. 2011;121:2234–9.
3.	Xu J, Ong HX, Traini D, et al. Paclitaxel eluting silicone stent review. Expert Opin Drug Deliv. 2020;17:1631–45.
### Question  ###
A 58 year old man with chronic bronchitic COPD undergoes three staged sessions of metered cryospray (liquid nitrogen) for cough and sputum. What best reflects short term clinical outcomes and safety from prospective studies?
 ### Options### 
(a) No change in patient reported outcomes at 3 months; frequent pneumothorax
(b) Dramatic FEV₁ increase ≥20% sustained for 12 months; frequent airway cartilage injury
(c) Reduced sputum but worsening CAT and SGRQ; high barotrauma rate
(d) Improved CT emphysema index at 3 months; neutral symptoms
(e) Clinically meaningful improvements in SGRQ/CAT at ~3 months with acceptable safety (exacerbation/pneumonia most common AEs) and no signal for pneumothorax; benefit persisting to ~6–9 months in many patients
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Prospective metered cryospray studies report symptom score improvements at ~3 months and acceptable safety, without a pneumothorax signal—choice (e) correct. Patients typically experienced better SGRQ, CAT, and cough metrics; histology shows cartilage sparing injury depth. Adverse events included COPD exacerbations and pneumonia rather than barotrauma. Longer term follow up shows benefits can persist through 6–9 months in many, though durability beyond that remains under study. Pearl: Cryospray aims to improve symptoms/health status rather than produce large spirometric or structural (CT) changes.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Symptom improvements, not deterioration, are typical; pneumothorax was not a signal. (b) Large FEV₁ jumps and cartilage injury are not expected. (c) Scores improve, and barotrauma is uncommon with proper technique. (d) CT emphysema index is not the target outcome.
###References###
1.	Garner JL, Shaipanich T, Hartman JE, et al. Metered cryospray feasibility and outcomes. Eur Respir J. 2020;56:2000556. https://doi.org/10.1183/13993003.00556-2020
2.	Slebos D J, Breen D, Coad J, et al. Histologic effect of metered cryotherapy. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2017;196:1351–2. https://doi.org/10.1164/rccm.201611-2220LE
### Question  ###
A 70 year old man with moderate–severe COPD and 3 exacerbations in 12 months undergoes targeted lung denervation. At a 2 year follow up visit, he asks what to expect in terms of lung function and symptoms if all goes well. Which pattern best matches published outcomes?
 ### Options### 
(a) Large sustained FEV₁ improvement with worsened quality of life scores
(b) Progressive FEV₁ decline but marked improvement in diffusion capacity
(c) Generally stable lung function and health status over time, with fewer exacerbations and a longer time to first exacerbation
(d) Normalization of CT air trapping measures and emphysema index
(e) Substantial reduction in pneumonia admissions unrelated to COPD
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Longer term randomized trial follow up shows stable spirometry/health status alongside fewer exacerbations and delayed time to first event—choice (c) correct. In AIRFLOW 2, 2 year outcomes demonstrated treatment durability in exacerbation metrics without large, consistent spirometric gains; stability is encouraging given COPD’s progressive trajectory. Crossover data reinforce this signal. Pearl: When counseling patients on TLD, emphasize exacerbation risk reduction and stability—not dramatic FEV₁ gains or structural reversal.
Why others are incorrect: (a) Large FEV₁ increases with worse QoL are not observed. (d) CT metrics of emphysema/air trapping are not reversibly “normalized” by denervation. (b) and (e) describe effects not supported as primary findings of TLD trials.
###References###
1.	Valipour A, Shah PL, Herth FJ, et al. Two year AIRFLOW 2 outcomes. Int J Chron Obstruct Pulmon Dis. 2020;15:2807–16. https://doi.org/10.2147/COPD.S267409
2.	Conway F, Tonkin J, Valipour A, et al. AIRFLOW 2 crossover outcomes. Respiration. 2022;101:1069–74. https://doi.org/10.1159/000527455
### Question  ###
A 47 year old man with prior tuberculosis presents with progressive dyspnea and monophonic wheeze. CT with 1 mm reconstructions shows a 1.2 cm concentric stricture of the left main bronchus 2 cm distal to the carina, without extrinsic compression. He is a former 20 pack year smoker, on daily aspirin but no anticoagulants. Spirometry shows flattening of both inspiratory and expiratory limbs. Under general anesthesia with a rigid bronchoscope, you perform graded balloon bronchoplasty (non compliant balloon, serial inflations to 10–12 atm). During the second 60 second inflation, a short, superficial mucosal laceration appears along the posterior wall; oxygenation and hemodynamics remain stable, and bleeding is scant. Post deflation, the lumen is visibly wider and the patient remains asymptomatic. What is the most appropriate interpretation/next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Airway laceration can accompany effective dilation and often predicts longer patency; observe and manage conservatively unless instability develops
(b) Immediate thoracotomy is indicated because transmural perforation is likely
(c) Place a covered airway stent now to “seal” the tear
(d) Abort and schedule surgical segmental resection
(e) Start broad spectrum antibiotics prophylactically for mediastinitis
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Manage conservatively—choice (a) correct. In a Chest series of benign strictures, balloon related airway lacerations occurred in roughly half of procedures, were predominantly superficial, healed without intervention, and—critically—were associated with longer airway patency versus cases without laceration (median ~24 vs ~4 months), supporting observation when the patient is stable and the tear is non–full thickness. Through and through perforation was not observed, and transient symptoms (mild chest pain, scant hemoptysis, occasional pneumomediastinum) resolved spontaneously. Routine prophylactic antibiotics are not indicated for a clean, superficial mucosal tear (e incorrect). Emergent surgery (b) or planned resection (d) is reserved for full thickness injury, uncontrolled bleeding, or failure of endoscopic management. Stenting solely to cover a superficial tear (c) is unnecessary and should be reserved for separate indications (e.g., recoil, rapid restenosis, malacia). Pearl: A small mucosal tear during ballooning—when the patient is stable—often signals a “successful” disruption of fibrotic rings rather than a complication requiring escalation. Table 3 (p. 4) of the chapter lists anticipated complications such as laceration and pneumomediastinum, aligning with these observations. 
Balloon Dilitation
###References###
1.	Kim JH, et al. Tracheobronchial laceration after balloon dilation for benign strictures: incidence and clinical significance. Chest. 2007;131:1114–1117. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/17426217/
2.	Shitrit D, et al. Bronchoscopic balloon dilatation of tracheobronchial stenosis: long term follow up. Eur J Cardiothorac Surg. 2010;38:198–202. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20153661/

### Question  ###
A 62 year old man with subacute cough, dyspnea, and uveitis has CT showing 4R (18 mm) and subcarinal (28 mm) lymphadenopathy with perilymphatic nodules. He is a lifelong nonsmoker. EBUS TBNA (21 gauge needle, 4 passes per node) yields granulomatous inflammation but is nondiagnostic for sarcoidosis; flow cytometry is insufficient to exclude lymphoma. He takes apixaban, held 72 hours pre procedure per institutional protocol. You plan a repeat bronchoscopy to secure a diagnosis and, if cancer is found, enable molecular testing. Which strategy best increases yield while maintaining safety?
 ### Options### 
(a) Repeat EBUS TBNA alone with a larger (19 gauge) needle
(b) Combine EBUS TBNA with endobronchial ultrasound guided transbronchial mediastinal cryobiopsy in the same session
(c) Start a prednisone trial now and re image in 6 weeks
(d) Proceed directly to cervical mediastinoscopy
(e) Perform EUS FNA alone of the subcarinal station
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Add cryobiopsy to TBNA—choice (b) correct. Randomized data show that combining EBUS TBNA with mediastinal cryobiopsy maintains staging accuracy while significantly increasing overall diagnostic yield, especially for benign diseases (e.g., sarcoidosis) and improving tissue adequacy for downstream molecular analyses, without an increase in serious complications. This combined approach addresses both the need for architecture preserving tissue and molecular readiness should malignancy be found. Simply upsizing the needle (a) may not resolve architectural and cellularity limitations. Empiric steroids (c) risk obscuring pathology and are inappropriate without a definitive diagnosis. Mediastinoscopy (d) remains effective but is more invasive and is not the next best step when less invasive, higher yield endosonographic options exist. EUS FNA alone (e) samples a single route and may still be limited by cytology predominant specimens. Pearl: When TBNA is nondiagnostic, pairing it with cryobiopsy can convert many “cytology only” nodes into architecture rich cores suitable for immunophenotyping and molecular testing. 
###References###
1.	Fan Y, et al. TBNA combined with cryobiopsy for mediastinal diseases: multicentre randomized trial. Lancet Respir Med. 2023;11:256–264. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/36279880/
2.	Zhang J, et al. Transbronchial mediastinal cryobiopsy vs TBNA: randomized trial. Eur Respir J. 2021;58:2100055. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33958432/
3.	Vilmann P, et al. Combined EBUS/EUS for diagnosis and staging of lung cancer (ESGE/ERS/ESTS Guideline). Eur Respir J. 2015;46:40–60. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26034128/

### Question  ###
A 55 year old woman with idiopathic tracheal stenosis (1 cm web just below the cricoid; thin slice CT confirms intraluminal disease without cartilage involvement) undergoes planned balloon bronchoplasty via a laryngeal mask airway under general anesthesia. She has no bleeding diathesis and is not taking antiplatelets or anticoagulants. The stenosis is easily visualized with a flexible bronchoscope; a non compliant balloon catheter fits through the working channel, and the lesion can be centered under direct vision. The interventional radiology suite is unavailable today. What is the most appropriate approach to intraprocedural imaging?
 ### Options### 
(a) Fluoroscopy is mandatory to position the balloon for all dilations
(b) Recommend fluoroscopy for all benign lesions but omit it in malignant disease
(c) Proceed without fluoroscopy because direct endoscopic visualization is adequate in many cases
(d) Use fluoroscopy only if inflation pressures exceed 10 atm
(e) Avoid fluoroscopy categorically because radiation exposure outweighs benefit
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Direct visualization suffices—choice (c) correct. A prospective series showed safe, effective bronchoscopic balloon dilation performed without fluoroscopy when the lesion and balloon can be aligned under endoscopic vision; fluoroscopy may be reserved for difficult positioning or when over the wire techniques are required. There is no requirement to use fluoroscopy routinely (a, b, d incorrect), and it is not categorically contraindicated (e). Practical technique details—including serial 60 second inflations with step up diameters—derive from clinical series that did not mandate fluoroscopy, reinforcing selective use. Pearl: If you can see the stenosis and the balloon clearly, fluoroscopy usually adds little to safety or efficacy. 
Balloon Dilitation

### Question  ###
A 55 year old woman with a 2.6 cm RUL mass and PET avid 4R and 7 nodes is scheduled for endosonographic staging. She has a 10 pack year smoking history, ECOG 1, and good pulmonary reserve on PFTs. The oncology plan hinges on accurate N staging and obtaining tissue adequate for EGFR/ALK/ROS1 and PD L1 testing. Prior outside TBNA of a hilar node was positive for adenocarcinoma but insufficient for full molecular profiling. You will sample multiple stations (≥3 passes/node) and want to maximize both staging accuracy and molecular readiness without moving straight to surgery. Which bronchoscopic plan best meets these goals?
 ### Options### 
(a) EBUS TBNA alone with a 21 gauge needle
(b) EBUS TBNA plus EBUS guided miniforceps biopsies
(c) Miniforceps biopsies only
(d) EBUS TBNA combined with mediastinal cryobiopsy during the same session
(e) Proceed directly to cervical mediastinoscopy for nodal sampling
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Combine TBNA with cryobiopsy—choice (d) correct. In a multicenter randomized trial, adding mediastinal cryobiopsy to TBNA delivered similar accuracy for detecting nodal metastases compared with TBNA alone but significantly improved tissue adequacy for molecular testing and immunoprofiling, reducing indeterminate biomarker results without increasing serious complications. A randomized head to head study also showed cryobiopsy cores are larger than several standard forceps bites, which translates into better overall yield when combined with TBNA. Miniforceps (b) can augment yield—particularly in benign disease and lymphoma—but the strongest randomized evidence for molecular adequacy in lung cancer supports adding cryobiopsy. TBNA alone (a) is guideline supported for staging but risks insufficient tissue for comprehensive biomarkers. Miniforceps alone (c) or routine mediastinoscopy (e) are unnecessary first line steps when a less invasive combined endosonographic strategy can satisfy both staging and molecular needs. Pearl: In lung cancer staging, the TBNA + cryo combination reduces “quantity not sufficient” reports for genomics without sacrificing oncologic accuracy. 
###References###
1.	Fan Y, et al. TBNA combined with cryobiopsy for mediastinal diseases: multicentre randomized trial. Lancet Respir Med. 2023;11:256–264. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/36279880/
2.	Cheng TL, et al. Cryobiopsy vs forceps for mediastinal lesions: randomized trial. Pulmonology. 2024;30:466–474. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/38182469/
3.	Vilmann P, et al. Combined EBUS/EUS guideline. Eur Respir J. 2015;46:40–60. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26034128/

### Question  ###
A 48 year old bilateral lung transplant recipient (8 months post op) presents with dyspnea and flow volume flattening. CT shows focal narrowing at the right bronchial anastomosis; bronchoscopy confirms a fibrotic ring with mild malacia. He has already undergone four balloon dilations over 5 months with only transient relief (3–4 weeks). He is on tacrolimus and low dose prednisone, no antiplatelets/anticoagulants. Cultures are negative; there is no significant airway colonization. You plan another procedure to improve patency and durability. What is the best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Topical mitomycin C application alone
(b) Continue serial balloon dilations indefinitely
(c) Laser debulking only
(d) Photodynamic therapy as a stand alone therapy
(e) Endobronchial stent placement following dilation
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Proceed to stent placement after dilation—choice (e) correct. In post transplant anastomotic stenosis, observational series show that while balloon dilation provides short term relief, stent placement (often after dilation) is associated with longer airway patency, improved FEV₁, better symptom control, and even improved survival compared with dilation strategies alone. Many patients ultimately require stents after multiple dilations, and in comparative analyses, those receiving stents experienced superior outcomes. Mitomycin C (a), laser (c), or photodynamic therapy (d) have been used adjunctively in small series but lack robust evidence for durable superiority in this population. Continuing dilations indefinitely (b) is reasonable early on but becomes inefficient when responses are brief and frequent procedures are needed. Pitfall: Delaying stenting despite rapid restenosis after repeated dilations can prolong morbidity and procedural burden without improving durability. Figure panels and outcomes summarized in the chapter’s section on transplantation mirror the survival/pulmonary function benefit reported in the cited series. 
Balloon Dilitation
###References###
1.	Abi Jaoudeh N, et al. Endobronchial dilation after lung transplantation: effect of stent placement on survival. J Vasc Interv Radiol. 2009;20:912–920. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19555886/
2.	De Gracia J, et al. Balloon dilation for post transplant bronchial stenosis: outcomes and need for stents. Respir Med. 2007;101:27–33. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16774820/

### Question  ###
Your interventional pulmonology service is updating its mediastinal sampling algorithm to secure larger, architecture preserving tissue when EBUS TBNA is limited. The team is comfortable with miniforceps biopsy but is considering adding EBUS guided transbronchial mediastinal cryobiopsy (TBMCB) with a high frequency needle knife to open the airway wall. Before changing practice, your fellow asks which study first randomized patients to evaluate TBMCB itself (not just as an add on strategy) against standard TBNA. Identifying that trial will help frame expectations for yield and safety and inform training (e.g., probe size, window creation) for benign disease as well as uncommon tumors. Which publication provided the first randomized evaluation of EBUS TBMCB?
 ### Options### 
(a) Zhang et al., European Respiratory Journal (2021)
(b) Annema et al., JAMA (2010)
(c) Navani et al., Lancet Respir Med (2015)
(d) Eapen et al., Chest (2013; AQuIRE registry)
(e) Herth et al., Ann Thorac Surg (2008)
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Zhang et al., ERJ 2021—choice (a) correct. That randomized trial compared EBUS TBMCB to standard EBUS TBNA, showing higher overall diagnostic yield—particularly for non malignant conditions—while maintaining acceptable safety. Subsequent work, including the multicenter RCT by Fan et al., extended the concept by combining TBNA and cryobiopsy to preserve staging accuracy and improve molecular adequacy. Annema 2010 (b) randomized mediastinoscopy vs endosonography for staging and did not study TBMCB. Navani 2015 (c) was a pragmatic trial comparing EBUS TBNA–based pathways with conventional approaches, again not cryobiopsy. Eapen/AQuIRE (d) is a registry detailing EBUS TBNA practice patterns/complications; it is not a randomized TBMCB evaluation. Herth 2008 (e) evaluated EBUS guided miniforceps, not cryobiopsy. Pearl: Know two key trials: Zhang 2021 (TBMCB vs TBNA) and Fan 2023 (TBNA + TBMCB vs TBNA)—together they define where cryobiopsy fits. 
###References###
1.	Zhang J, et al. Transbronchial mediastinal cryobiopsy vs TBNA: randomized trial. Eur Respir J. 2021;58:2100055. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33958432/
2.	Fan Y, et al. TBNA combined with cryobiopsy: multicentre randomized trial. Lancet Respir Med. 2023;11:256–264. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/36279880/

### Question  ###
A 38 year old woman with idiopathic subglottic stenosis (no autoimmune markers; CT shows a 14 mm circumferential ring 8 mm below the cricoid; bronchoscopy without malacia) has undergone six balloon bronchoplasties over the last year, each with symptomatic relief lasting 4–6 weeks. She is otherwise healthy, takes no anticoagulants, and prefers to avoid a stent if possible. Spirometry again shows fixed upper airway obstruction, and she asks if another balloon dilation alone is likely to give a more durable result. What does the evidence suggest about continuing serial dilations alone at this point?
 ### Options### 
(a) Continue serial dilations indefinitely; cumulative benefit increases over time
(b) Benefits plateau after about six sessions; consider adjuncts (e.g., stenting or other modalities) if durability remains poor
(c) All patients should stop after exactly three sessions regardless of response
(d) Switch to blind bougienage because it is superior long term
(e) Increase each inflation to 5 minutes; this is proven superior to 1 minute
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Expect diminishing returns beyond ~six dilations—choice (b) correct. In a multicenter study of non malignant central airway stenosis, investigators observed no incremental improvement in efficacy beyond roughly six balloon dilation sessions, suggesting that patients with short lived responses after multiple procedures should transition to adjunctive strategies rather than perpetuating serial dilations alone. Earlier series of benign stenosis documented good immediate technical success but frequent need for additional interventions; none demonstrated that simply repeating ballooning indefinitely increases durability. Arbitrary hard stops at three sessions (c) are unsupported. Blind bougienage (d) risks shear injury and is not superior. Prolonged 5 minute inflations (e) have not been proven superior to commonly used shorter holds; comparative superiority of inflation times has not been established in peer reviewed trials. Pearl: When symptom free intervals shrink or plateau after multiple dilations, pivot to adjuncts rather than chasing durability with “one more” balloon session. Table 5 outcomes in the chapter narratively parallel these data. 
Balloon Dilitation
###References###
1.	Liang W, et al. Appropriate treatment sessions of bronchoscopic balloon dilation for non malignant central airway stenosis. Ther Adv Respir Dis. 2019;13:1753466619831966. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30795728/
2.	Sheski FD, Mathur PN. Long term results of balloon dilation in benign stenosis. Chest. 1998;114:796–800. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/9743169/
3.	Ferretti G, et al. Benign noninflammatory bronchial stenosis: treatment with balloon dilation. Radiology. 1995;196:831–834. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/7644651/

### Question  ###
A 64 year old man with weight loss and dysphagia has a 3.5 cm posterior mediastinal mass that abuts the esophagus and descends into the aortopulmonary window; the tracheal route is not feasible. Prior EUS FNA (25 gauge, 4 passes) was nondiagnostic. PET is avid but non specific. He takes clopidogrel (held 7 days) and is otherwise stable for deep sedation. You aim to obtain architecture preserving tissue suitable for immunohistochemistry and molecular testing while remaining minimally invasive. Which option is most likely to increase yield by providing larger core samples from this location?
 ### Options### 
(a) Repeat EUS FNA alone
(b) Cervical mediastinoscopy
(c) Transesophageal mediastinal cryobiopsy under endoscopic ultrasound guidance
(d) CT guided transthoracic core biopsy
(e) PET CT to further characterize the lesion
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Choose transesophageal mediastinal cryobiopsy—choice (c) correct. For posterior mediastinal targets accessible via the esophagus, case based and series data show that EUS guided transesophageal cryobiopsy can obtain larger, architecture preserving cores and achieve high diagnostic yield—even after nondiagnostic EUS FNA. In an initial report, endoscopic transesophageal cryobiopsy diagnosed nodular lymphocyte predominant Hodgkin lymphoma; a subsequent 31 patient series reported 100% yield for EUS TBMCB versus 61% with EUS FNA, with no major complications. Mediastinoscopy (b) is effective but more invasive and may not reach posterior lesions easily. Repeating FNA alone (a) risks another cytology limited result. CT guided transthoracic core (d) may be anatomically challenging and higher risk depending on trajectory. PET CT (e) cannot replace tissue diagnosis. Pearl: When EUS FNA is nondiagnostic for a posterior mediastinal target, step up to EUS guided cryobiopsy to convert cytology into core histology without surgery. Illustrative images in the chapter (Fig. 8) depict the esophageal windowing and cryoprobe trajectory for this approach. 
###References###
1.	Huang ZS, et al. Mediastinal nodular lymphocyte predominant Hodgkin lymphoma diagnosed by endoscopic transesophageal cryobiopsy. Respiration. 2022;101:190–194. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/34515245/
2.	Salcedo Lobera E, et al. Transesophageal EUS guided mediastinal cryobiopsy: 31 case experience. Arch Bronconeumol. 2024;60:587–589. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/38811320/
3.	Vilmann P, et al. Combined endosonography guideline. Eur Respir J. 2015;46:40–60. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26034128/

### Question  ###
A 66 year old man with squamous cell carcinoma of the left upper lobe is admitted with severe dyspnea. CT shows near complete occlusion of the left main bronchus from mixed intrinsic tumor and extrinsic compression; there is no massive hemoptysis. He is on room air at rest but desaturates with minimal exertion; chemoradiation is planned once his airway is stabilized. You will perform urgent bronchoscopic debulking and balloon dilation as a bridge to stenting and oncologic therapy. The lesion is easily visualized; oxygenation is adequate for the planned intervention. What immediate outcome should you realistically expect from a single dilation session?
 ### Options### 
(a) Durable cure in >90% of cases
(b) Minimal change in luminal caliber
(c) An immediate 5–10% procedure related mortality risk
(d) Luminal improvement in roughly 70–80% of cases, often sufficient to relieve symptoms or facilitate stent placement
(e) Balloon dilation is contraindicated in malignant stenosis
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Expect immediate improvement in most cases—choice (d) correct. In malignant central airway obstruction, fiberoptic balloon dilation produces rapid luminal enlargement in the majority of stenoses (≈75–80% in a large series), often enough to relieve symptoms or to facilitate adjuncts such as stent placement; however, long term control generally requires additional oncologic or endoscopic therapy. Procedure related mortality is low (c incorrect) in reported series when performed in appropriate settings. Dilation is not contraindicated in malignancy (e incorrect); it is frequently used as a bridge to stenting or definitive oncologic care. A single dilation rarely achieves durable cure (a incorrect) because tumor biology and extrinsic compression remain. Minimal change (b) is not typical when technique and case selection are appropriate. Pearl: In malignant airway obstruction, balloon dilation is a fast, low risk way to create a “working airway” that enables stenting and oncologic therapy—expect immediate benefit, not durability. Outcome figures in the chapter’s “Outcomes” section reflect these data. 
Balloon Dilitation

### Question  ###
A 59 year old woman with 25 pack years presents with B symptoms and bulky subcarinal lymphadenopathy (short axis 22 mm). CT PET is avid; there is no endobronchial tumor. Initial EBUS TBNA (four passes) is “atypical lymphoid proliferation—insufficient for architecture.” You plan repeat bronchoscopy because excisional surgery is high risk given severe COPD (FEV₁ 42% predicted). She is off clopidogrel for 7 days. Your goal is to obtain architecture preserving tissue to establish or exclude lymphoma while remaining minimally invasive. What is the best next bronchoscopic step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Repeat TBNA with a larger needle only
(b) EBUS guided mediastinal cryobiopsy alone
(c) Repeat TBNA with rapid on site cytology only
(d) Proceed directly to mediastinoscopy
(e) Add EBUS guided miniforceps biopsies in addition to TBNA
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Add miniforceps to TBNA—choice (e) correct. For suspected lymphoma or other diagnoses requiring preserved architecture, EBUS guided miniforceps biopsy added to TBNA increases diagnostic yield and provides histologic cores suitable for immunophenotyping. Early prospective work and subsequent series demonstrated improved yield over TBNA alone, particularly for sarcoidosis and lymphoma, with acceptable safety in experienced hands. While EBUS TBMCB is promising (b), the strongest architecture specific evidence for lymphoma remains with miniforceps; randomized cryobiopsy trials emphasize overall and benign disease yield and molecular adequacy, not specifically lymphoma architecture. Simply upsizing the needle or relying on ROSE (a, c) does not reliably overcome the limitation of cytology only specimens. Mediastinoscopy (d) remains an option but is more invasive; it is reasonable to maximize endosonographic tissue first. Pearl: When lymphoma is on the table, pair TBNA with miniforceps to turn “suspicious cytology” into core histology without jumping straight to mediastinoscopy. The chapter’s ###Discussion### of forceps and cryobiopsy techniques contextualizes these data. 
###References###
1.	Herth FJF, et al. EBUS guided miniforceps in subcarinal masses. Ann Thorac Surg. 2008;85:1874–1878. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/18498786/
2.	Franke KJ, et al. Diagnostic contribution of EBUS guided forceps biopsy in mediastinal/hilar lymphadenopathy. Lung. 2012;190:227–232. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/22037795/
3.	Ray AS, et al. Improved yield and specimen quality with EBUS guided forceps biopsies. Ann Thorac Surg. 2020;109:894–901. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/31593653/
### Question  ###
A 62-year-old man with severe right lower lobe bronchiectasis and a 40–pack year smoking history presents with expectoration of ~250 mL bright-red blood in the past hour. He is tachypneic and coughing up clots. Vitals: T 98.8 °F, HR 118, BP 146/84, RR 32, SpO₂ 88% on a non rebreather mask. He is alert but struggling to protect his airway. Which immediate airway step will best protect oxygenation while enabling therapeutic bronchoscopy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Place him in lateral decubitus with the suspected bleeding lung dependent and observe
(b) Orotracheal intubation with a large bore (≥8.5 mm ID) single lumen tube for subsequent bronchoscopic suctioning
(c) Proceed directly to rigid bronchoscopy in the operating room without prior intubation
(d) Obtain emergent contrast CT angiography before intervening on the airway
(e) Nebulized tranexamic acid plus high flow nasal oxygen
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In life threatening hemoptysis with hypoxemia and threatened airway, orotracheal intubation with a large bore (≥8.5 mm) single lumen tube (choice b) is the best immediate step to secure oxygenation and allow flexible bronchoscopy for suction, tamponade, and localization. Contemporary reviews recommend large ID tubes (often >8.5 mm) to accommodate therapeutic bronchoscopes and tools, with subsequent definitive control via bronchoscopic measures and/or embolization as indicated. Positioning the bleeding side down and temporary intubation are emphasized as initial stabilization measures, but definitive care requires securing the airway first when gas exchange is compromised.
Why the others are incorrect: (a) Dependent lung positioning helps limit contralateral flooding but is insufficient in this hypoxemic, actively bleeding patient who needs a protected airway. (c) Rigid bronchoscopy can be valuable, but immediate controlled orotracheal intubation is faster in most ICUs/EDs and facilitates urgent bronchoscopic suction; rigid often follows in controlled settings. (d) CT angiography is important for localization and planning embolization in stable patients; delaying airway protection in this unstable scenario risks asphyxiation. (e) Nebulized antifibrinolytics may reduce minor bleeding, but evidence is limited and this does not secure the airway in massive hemoptysis.
Pearl: Use a large ID single lumen ETT (commonly ≥8.5 mm) to allow passage of a therapeutic bronchoscope; smaller tubes impede suction and hemostatic maneuvers.
###References###
1.	Davidson K, Shojaee S. Managing Massive Hemoptysis. Chest. 2019;155(1):77 88. https://journal.chestnet.org/article/S0012-3692(19)31386-8/abstract 
2.	Ittrich H, et al. The Diagnosis and Treatment of Hemoptysis. Dtsch Arztebl Int. 2017;114:371 381. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28625277/ 
### Question  ###
A 56-year-old man with bronchiectasis and daily small-volume hemoptysis now has a single episode of ~60 mL bright-red blood. He is hemodynamically stable (HR 92, BP 132/78, SpO₂ 96% on 2 L). Labs: platelets 224 k/µL, INR 1.0. You plan to localize the bleeding and map culprit vessels for potential embolization. What is the best next diagnostic study?
 ### Options### 
(a) Immediate bedside flexible bronchoscopy without imaging
(b) Noncontrast high resolution chest CT
(c) Ventilation–perfusion scintigraphy
(d) Contrast enhanced multidetector CT angiography of the chest
(e) Sputum mycobacterial culture as the sole next step
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In a stable patient, contrast enhanced multidetector CT angiography (choice d) best localizes the bleeding site, identifies underlying pathology, and delineates bronchial/systemic collaterals for bronchial artery embolization planning. Modern algorithms emphasize CT/CTA early in stable hemoptysis because it outperforms bronchoscopy in detecting peripheral sources and vascular anatomy while still complementary to endoscopic evaluation.
(a) Bronchoscopy is crucial for airway clearance and endobronchial sources but is less sensitive for peripheral lesions and provides limited vascular mapping; in stable patients CTA first is preferred for comprehensive planning. (b) Noncontrast HRCT lacks vascular information; contrast CTA is needed to evaluate hypertrophied bronchial arteries and nonbronchial systemic collaterals. (c) V/Q scanning does not localize endobronchial bleeding or guide embolization. (e) Sputum cultures may be useful etiologically (e.g., TB) but do not localize bleeding or map vessels; they should not delay appropriate imaging.
Pitfall: Delaying CTA in a stable patient can postpone definitive therapy (e.g., embolization) by failing to reveal the culprit arterial anatomy.
###References###
1.	Ittrich H, et al. The Diagnosis and Treatment of Hemoptysis. Dtsch Arztebl Int. 2017;114:371 381. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28625277/ 
2.	Jean Baptiste E. Clinical assessment and management of massive hemoptysis. Crit Care Med. 2000;28:1642 1647. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10834728/ 
### Question  ###
In the ICU, a 48-year-old woman with cystic fibrosis on pressure control ventilation (FiO₂ 0.50) develops brisk hemoptysis. Flexible bronchoscopy via an 8.5 mm ETT reveals active bleeding from the posterior segment of the right upper lobe. After suctioning large clots, which immediate bronchoscopic maneuver is most appropriate to achieve temporary hemostasis?
 ### Options### 
(a) Wedge the bronchoscope into the bleeding subsegment and instill cold saline with dilute epinephrine
(b) Apply argon plasma coagulation over the oozing mucosa as the first step
(c) Exchange to a double lumen endotracheal tube for lung isolation
(d) Give an intravenous vasopressin bolus for systemic vasoconstriction
(e) Proceed directly to surgical lobectomy from the ICU
###Discussion###
Bottom line: The recommended first maneuver is to wedge the bronchoscope in the culprit subsegment and instill cold saline with dilute epinephrine (choice a) to tamponade the site and slow bleeding, permitting stabilization and planning for definitive therapy. Reviews of massive hemoptysis emphasize bronchoscopic wedging, iced/cold saline, and topical vasoconstrictors (e.g., epinephrine) as frontline endoscopic measures.
(b) Thermal modalities can be effective for discrete lesions but are not first in uncontrolled bleeding; visualization is poor and there is risk before stabilization. (c) Double lumen tubes hinder therapeutic bronchoscopy and suction; a large single lumen ETT is preferred for instrumented control and clot removal. (d) Systemic vasopressin is not standard for hemoptysis control and risks ischemic adverse effects. (e) Emergent surgery is reserved for select, refractory situations; bronchoscopy and/or embolization are first line.
Pearl: Simple wedging of the bronchoscope provides immediate tamponade and buys time for definitive therapy (embolization or targeted endobronchial treatment).
###References###
1.	Sakr L, Dutau H. Massive Hemoptysis: Role of Bronchoscopy. Respiration. 2010;80:38 58. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20090288/ 
2.	Davidson K, Shojaee S. Managing Massive Hemoptysis. Chest. 2019;155(1):77 88. https://journal.chestnet.org/article/S0012-3692(19)31386-8/abstract 
3.	Ittrich H, et al. Dtsch Arztebl Int. 2017;114:371 381. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28625277/ 
### Question  ###
A 58-year-old man with severe right middle lobe bronchiectasis has recurrent hemoptysis (150–200 mL/day) despite bronchoscopic tamponade during this admission. He is stable on 4 L O₂. Thin section CTA shows hypertrophied right bronchial arteries with blush in the middle lobe and no pulmonary artery pseudoaneurysm. What is the best next definitive therapy to prevent rebleeding?
 ### Options### 
(a) Prophylactic external beam radiation to the involved bronchus
(b) High frequency jet ventilation with repeat rigid bronchoscopy alone
(c) Immediate pneumonectomy during the same admission
(d) Intravenous tranexamic acid and observation
(e) Superselective bronchial artery embolization of the culprit systemic artery
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Superselective bronchial artery embolization (choice e) is recommended as first line definitive therapy for life threatening or recurrent hemoptysis from peripheral sources and is effective as definitive or bridging therapy with high initial control rates. Classic series and modern reviews show immediate control with acceptable recurrence and complication profiles, making BAE the preferred next step after stabilization.
(a) External beam radiation is not a standard treatment for hemoptysis from bronchiectasis. (b) Rigid bronchoscopy is valuable for airway control and focal endobronchial lesions but does not address pathologic systemic arterial supply driving recurrent bleeding; embolization targets the culprit vessels. (c) Emergent pneumonectomy carries high morbidity/mortality; surgery is reserved for failures of embolization or special indications. (d) Antifibrinolytics may be adjunctive but are not definitive therapy in recurrent significant bleeding.
Pearl: In stable patients, CTA followed by BAE addresses both localization and the pathologic bronchial circulation that drives rebleeding.
###References###
1.	Ittrich H, et al. The Diagnosis and Treatment of Hemoptysis. Dtsch Arztebl Int. 2017;114:371 381. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28625277/ 
2.	Mal H, et al. Immediate and long term results of bronchial artery embolization for life threatening hemoptysis. Chest. 1999;115:996 1001. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10208199/ 
3.	Davidson K, Shojaee S. Chest. 2019;155(1):77 88. https://journal.chestnet.org/article/S0012-3692(19)31386-8/abstract 
### Question  ###
A 64-year-old man undergoes bronchial artery embolization (BAE) for massive hemoptysis from aspergilloma. Two hours later he develops acute bilateral leg weakness and urinary retention. Brain CT is normal. Which BAE complication best explains this presentation?
 ### Options### 
(a) Reperfusion pulmonary edema
(b) Cerebral air embolism
(c) Anterior spinal cord ischemia from inadvertent embolization of a spinal artery
(d) Posterior reversible encephalopathy from contrast
(e) Tracheoesophageal fistula from endotracheal tube cuff pressure
###Discussion###
Bottom line: The feared complication is anterior spinal cord ischemia due to non target embolization of a spinal arterial feeder (choice c), which can cause sudden paraplegia after BAE. Reviews emphasize BAE’s efficacy but warn of rare neurologic injury from embolization of the anterior spinal artery or radiculomedullary branches; meticulous technique and superselective catheterization mitigate risk.
(a) Reperfusion edema is not typical after BAE and would not specifically cause acute paraplegia. (b) Cerebral air embolism is uncommon and would present with focal cerebral findings. (d) PRES is associated with severe hypertension and cortical imaging changes, not isolated paraplegia. (e) Tracheoesophageal fistula relates to prolonged cuff ischemia, not an acute post embolization neurologic deficit.
Pitfall: Failure to recognize early neurologic symptoms after BAE delays evaluation for spinal cord ischemia, a rare but devastating complication.
###References###
1.	Ittrich H, et al. The Diagnosis and Treatment of Hemoptysis. Dtsch Arztebl Int. 2017;114:371 381. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28625277/ 
2.	Mal H, et al. Chest. 1999;115:996 1001. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10208199/ 
### Question  ###
A 67-year-old never smoking woman has newly diagnosed metastatic lung adenocarcinoma. EBUS TBNA using a 22 gauge needle yielded two cell blocks and 8 unstained slides; PD L1 is pending. You want to maximize actionable findings while conserving tissue. Which testing strategy is best?
 ### Options### 
(a) Sequential single gene PCR for EGFR, followed by ALK FISH if EGFR negative
(b) PD L1 immunohistochemistry alone because she is a never smoker
(c) ROS1 immunohistochemistry only because ROS1 is common in never smokers
(d) Send the specimen for an FDA approved comprehensive next generation sequencing (NGS) panel
(e) Start platinum–pemetrexed now and defer molecular testing until after 2 cycles
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Use an FDA approved comprehensive NGS panel (choice d) to evaluate multiple actionable genes, fusions, and genomic signatures from limited FFPE material. FoundationOne CDx is an example of an FDA approved assay with high success on FFPE tissue, reporting substitutions/indels, copy number changes, fusions, and signatures (e.g., TMB) and directly informing therapy selection. Companion diagnostics are increasingly FDA approved across platforms (IHC, ISH, PCR, NGS), and comprehensive NGS conserves tissue compared with sequential single gene testing.
(a) Sequential testing risks tissue exhaustion and delays; NGS panels detect EGFR plus other alterations (ALK/ROS1/RET/MET/BRAF/NTRK, etc.) in a single run. (b) PD L1 alone omits targetable drivers that strongly influence first line therapy. (c) Targeting only ROS1 ignores the broader driver landscape. (e) Deferring genotyping risks starting suboptimal therapy; timely molecular profiling is central to initial management.
Pearl: Comprehensive NGS maximizes yield from small bronchoscopic samples and aligns with the companion diagnostic framework guiding targeted therapies.
###References###
1.	Takeda M, et al. Clinical Application of the FoundationOne CDx Assay. Oncologist. 2021;26:e588 e596. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33325566/ 
2.	Jørgensen JT. FDA Approved Companion Diagnostics. Transl Oncol. 2021;14:101063. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33714919/ 
### Question  ###
A 59-year-old man with metastatic EGFR mutant (L858R) lung adenocarcinoma had an initial response to erlotinib but now has systemic progression. He is on apixaban for a recent PE, has new painful bone lesions, and is a poor candidate for repeat tissue biopsy. Which test is most appropriate now to detect resistance mechanisms and guide targeted therapy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Plasma cell free DNA next generation sequencing to identify resistance mutations (e.g., EGFR T790M/C797S)
(b) Repeat PD L1 immunohistochemistry on archival tissue
(c) Bronchoalveolar lavage cytology for EGFR genotyping
(d) Serum carcinoembryonic antigen (CEA) and LDH panel
(e) Sputum KRAS PCR
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Plasma cfDNA NGS (choice a) detects actionable resistance mechanisms when tissue biopsy is risky or infeasible. Ultra deep NGS of plasma can uncover driver and resistance variants at low allele fractions and shows high concordance with orthogonal assays; it frequently identifies EGFR T790M or C797S and other mechanisms in progressing cases.
(b) PD L1 does not define TKI resistance mechanisms in EGFR mutant disease. (c) BAL cytology is unreliable for comprehensive genotyping and often insufficient for resistance profiling. (d) Tumor markers lack specificity for mechanism based decisions. (e) Sputum KRAS PCR does not address EGFR TKI resistance and has limited sensitivity.
Pearl: Liquid biopsy can obviate repeat invasive sampling, revealing targetable resistance (e.g., T790M) to direct next line therapy.
###References###
1.	Li BT, et al. Ultra deep NGS of plasma cfDNA in advanced lung cancers. Ann Oncol. 2019;30:597 603. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30891595/ 
### Question  ###
A 66-year-old man with a 50–pack year smoking history has a 1.1 cm peripheral lung nodule. A transthoracic FNA yields poorly differentiated non small cell carcinoma, but immunostains are equivocal because of scant cellularity. A microRNA based assay shows markedly increased miR 205 expression. Which histologic classification is most consistent with this result?
 ### Options### 
(a) Minimally invasive mucinous adenocarcinoma
(b) Large cell neuroendocrine carcinoma
(c) Small cell carcinoma
(d) Sarcomatoid carcinoma
(e) Lung squamous cell carcinoma
###Discussion###
Bottom line: High miR 205 expression supports lung squamous cell carcinoma (choice e). A diagnostic assay using miR 205 accurately distinguished squamous cell carcinoma from adenocarcinoma, including on small biopsies/aspirates, with near perfect concordance to conventional methods.
Other options are inconsistent with the miRNA signature used in the referenced classifier: (a) Mucinous adenocarcinoma is an adenocarcinoma subtype—miR 205 is not characteristic. (b) Large cell neuroendocrine carcinoma and (c) small cell carcinoma are neuroendocrine tumors not defined by the miR 205 squamous lineage marker. (d) Sarcomatoid carcinoma lacks a specific miR 205 based signature supporting squamous differentiation.
Pearl: When IHC is limited by scant tissue, a validated miRNA based classifier (e.g., miR 205) can accurately resolve squamous vs adenocarcinoma on small bronchoscopic or FNA samples.
###References###
1.	Bishop JA, et al. Accurate classification of NSCLC using a microRNA based approach. Clin Cancer Res. 2010;16:610 619. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20068099/ 
### Question  ###
A 58-year-old man underwent lobectomy for stage II lung adenocarcinoma. The pathology department performed an exploratory microRNA panel. High miR 21 expression is reported. Which interpretation is most accurate based on aggregated evidence?
 ### Options### 
(a) Predicts exceptional response to adjuvant platinum only
(b) Indicates an ALK fusion is present
(c) Associated with increased recurrence risk and worse survival
(d) Proves the tumor is of colorectal origin
(e) Confirms microsatellite instability–high phenotype
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Elevated miR 21 is associated with higher recurrence risk and worse survival (choice c). A meta analysis synthesizing NSCLC studies found that high miR 21 levels correlate with poorer overall and recurrence free survival; miR 155 showed similar trends for recurrence. These data position miR 21 primarily as a prognostic—not predictive—biomarker.
(a) No evidence supports miR 21 as a specific predictor of platinum sensitivity. (b) ALK fusions are not identified by miR 21 level. (d) Tissue of origin cannot be assigned to colon based on miR 21 alone. (e) MSI H status is not inferred from miR 21.
Pearl: Think of miR 21 as a negative prognostic signal in NSCLC rather than a therapy selecting biomarker.
###References###
1.	Yang M, et al. High expression of miR 21 and miR 155 predicts recurrence and unfavorable survival in NSCLC. Eur J Cancer. 2013;49:604 615. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23099007/ 
### Question  ###
A 45-year-old never smoking woman with metastatic lung adenocarcinoma has limited EBUS TBNA material. Screening immunohistochemistry for ROS1 is strongly positive. To confirm the alteration and qualify for targeted therapy, which specific assay is most appropriate?
 ### Options### 
(a) ROS1 RNA expression microarray
(b) ROS1 break apart fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH)
(c) Serum ROS1 protein ELISA
(d) DNA methylation panel of the ROS1 promoter
(e) KRAS codon 12/13 PCR
###Discussion###
Bottom line: ROS1 break apart FISH (choice b) is an FDA recognized companion diagnostic approach for detecting ROS1 rearrangements and confirming positive IHC screens for therapy eligibility. The FDA companion diagnostic landscape includes ISH/FISH, IHC, PCR, and NGS assays; for some gene fusions (e.g., ROS1), break apart FISH is the validated confirmatory method used for selection of specific TKIs. Reviews of oncogenic fusions in lung cancer similarly highlight ROS1 rearrangements as targetable events typically confirmed by FISH or detected in multiplex fusion panels.
(a) RNA expression arrays are not the standard confirmatory CDx. (c) No validated serum ELISA determines ROS1 fusion status. (d) Promoter methylation is unrelated to detecting gene fusions. (e) KRAS testing does not assess ROS1 rearrangements and may be mutually exclusive with certain driver fusions.
Pearl: Use IHC as a screen but confirm ROS1 with a validated CDx—commonly break apart FISH—when tissue is limited and targeted therapy is contemplated.
###References###
1.	Jørgensen JT. FDA Approved Companion Diagnostics. Transl Oncol. 2021;14:101063. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33714919/ 
2.	Kohno T, et al. Beyond ALK RET, ROS1 and other oncogene fusions in lung cancer. Transl Lung Cancer Res. 2015;4:156 164. https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC4384213/ 
BPF:
### Question  ###
A 63-year-old man underwent right pneumonectomy for squamous cell carcinoma 6 days ago. He now has abrupt cough with salty-tasting sputum, fever 38.1 °C, HR 112/min, BP 118/66 mm Hg, SpO₂ 92% on 3 L nasal cannula. Chest tube shows continuous vigorous bubbling to water seal. Flexible bronchoscopy reveals a gaping defect at the right main bronchial stump with purulent effusion seen through the opening. What is the most appropriate next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Instill fibrin glue via flexible bronchoscopy to seal the stump
(b) Urgent return to the operating room for stump repair/reinforcement with thorough pleural drainage
(c) Place endobronchial one way valves in right lobar bronchi to reduce flow
(d) Increase PEEP and inspiratory pressure to reduce air leak and improve oxygenation
(e) Perform bedside talc pleurodesis via the chest tube to close the space
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In an early, central postresection bronchopleural fistula (BPF) after pneumonectomy with active empyema, prompt surgical re-exploration and stump repair with pleural drainage is indicated—choice (b) is correct. Large or central postoperative BPFs carry high mortality and are not reliably cured by endoscopic sealants or valves; contemporary thoracic series emphasize early operative management when the patient can tolerate reoperation, reserving bronchoscopic measures as bridges or for nonoperative candidates. Cardillo et al. reported high success of operative repair for postresectional BPF, particularly for larger fistulas, with bronchoscopy reserved for small/viable stumps or as adjuncts if surgery fails or is unsafe. Toste and colleagues likewise stress rapid recognition and escalated definitive therapy for central BPFs after resection. Clark et al. underscore the distinct management of BPF (vs prolonged air leak), noting its association with empyema and the frequent need for operative intervention. Reference numbers below correspond to the source chapter’s reference list. 
Bronchopleural Fistula
(a) Bronchoscopic glues are best for small, central defects; efficacy is limited for large stump dehiscence with infection. 
(c) Valves reduce distal airflow but are not first line for central post pneumonectomy stump failure with empyema. 
(d) Higher pressures increase trans fistula flow and risk tension physiology; lung protective, not escalated, pressures are recommended. 
(e) Chemical pleurodesis requires an apposed, sterile pleural space; active infection and unexpanded space make success unlikely. 
Pearl: Early, central post pneumonectomy BPF with infection is a surgical disease; bronchoscopy is a bridge, not definitive therapy.
###References###
1.	Cardillo G, et al. Ann Thorac Surg. 2015;100:251 257. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26024752/
2.	Toste PA, Revels SL. Surg Clin North Am. 2021;101:911 923. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/34537151/
3.	Clark JM, Cooke DT, Brown LM. Thorac Surg Clin. 2020;30:347 358. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/32593367/

### Question  ###
A 68-year-old man (40 pack year smoker) is intubated, day 8 after left upper lobectomy. He has a large continuous air leak to water seal and subcutaneous emphysema. Thin section CT (1.0 mm) shows postoperative changes without a visible fistulous tract. During bedside bronchoscopy with FiO₂ 0.4, which maneuver is most likely to localize the culprit airway for targeted therapy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Ventilation scintigraphy with 133Xe tomorrow in nuclear medicine
(b) Instillation of methylene blue onto the stump and inspection of chest tube effluent
(c) CT bronchography in the radiology suite by injecting contrast through the bronchoscope
(d) Talc slurry via chest tube while observing for leak reduction
(e) Sequential balloon occlusion of lobar/segmental bronchi while observing the water seal chamber for cessation of bubbling
###Discussion###
Bottom line: To localize a peripheral BPF at the bedside in a ventilated patient with a chest tube, perform sequential bronchoscopic balloon occlusion with observation of the water seal chamber—choice (e). Early series describing endoscopic diagnosis of postoperative BPFs used selective bronchography and bronchoscopic maneuvers to pinpoint the leaking airway; today, sequential balloon occlusion remains a practical bedside approach to identify the segment causing the leak so that targeted endobronchial therapy can be planned. Multicenter experience with intrabronchial/endobronchial valves similarly employs balloon occlusion during bronchoscopy to map the leak before device placement. 
(a) Ventilation scintigraphy can detect BPF but is not bedside and may be inconclusive in small fistulas or in mechanically ventilated patients. 
(b) Dye testing can confirm a central stump leak but is less useful for localizing a distal segment and may complicate subsequent surgery. (Technique described historically; limited role.) 
(c) CT bronchography can localize difficult BPFs but requires coordination and imaging; not the best immediate bedside method in an intubated patient. 
(d) Talc pleurodesis is not diagnostic and is ineffective if the lung is not apposed; it also does not localize the leak. 
Pitfall: Relying on repeat standard CT when thin section CT already failed often delays care; use bronchoscopic physiology to localize the leak at the bedside.
###References###
1.	York EL, et al. Chest. 1990;97:1390 1392. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/2347224/
2.	Travaline JM, et al. Chest. 2009;136:355 360. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19349382/
3.	Raja S, et al. Eur J Nucl Med. 1999;26:215 219. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10079310/

### Question  ###
A 72-year-old woman with a prolonged air leak 9 days after right lower lobectomy remains dependent on a chest tube. She is hemodynamically stable and would prefer to avoid reoperation. You are planning bronchoscopic one way valve therapy. Which pre procedural step best predicts procedural success and should guide targeting?
 ### Options### 
(a) Apply −40 cm H₂O suction for 24 hours before bronchoscopy
(b) Instill fibrin glue at the bronchial stump during the same session
(c) Schedule VATS decortication before valve placement
(d) Assess fissure integrity/collateral ventilation with thin section CT and/or intra procedural Chartis to choose a low CV target lobe
(e) Place valves in multiple lobes empirically to ensure coverage
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Before intrabronchial/endobronchial valve placement for prolonged air leak, evaluate fissure integrity/collateral ventilation—patients with intact fissures/low collateral ventilation are most likely to respond to lobar occlusion (choice d). In a multicenter analysis, Majid et al. found that fissure completeness (a surrogate for absent collateral ventilation) predicted successful resolution of prolonged air leak with intrabronchial valves. An expert panel best practice statement for persistent air leak likewise recommends confirming low collateral ventilation (by CT fissure analysis and/or Chartis) and then placing valves to isolate the leaking lobe. 
(a) Deep suction does not predict valve success and can perpetuate flow through the fistula. 
(b) Stump glues do not address distal alveolar/leak physiology and are not a prerequisite for valves. 
(c) Decortication may be needed for trapped lung, but it is not a predictor of valve efficacy. 
(e) Empiric multilevel valve placement increases risk/cost; targeting should be informed by collateral ventilation assessment. 
Pearl: Incomplete fissures (robust collateral ventilation) predict valve failure; confirm fissure integrity or low CV before deploying valves.
###References###
1.	Majid A, et al. Ann Thorac Surg. 2019;107:407 411. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30315804/
2.	Smesseim I, et al. Respiration. 2024;103:544 562. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/38870914/

### Question  ###
A 66-year-old man is scheduled for pneumonectomy for centrally located non–small cell lung cancer after multimodality therapy. Which factor most increases his postoperative risk of bronchopleural fistula?
 ### Options### 
(a) Neoadjuvant radiotherapy to the hilum
(b) Neoadjuvant chemotherapy alone
(c) Left-sided pneumonectomy
(d) Age >70 years with COPD but no radiotherapy
(e) Early extubation after surgery
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Neoadjuvant radiotherapy is a major risk factor for postresectional BPF—choice (a). A meta analysis of 14,912 patients found that preoperative radiotherapy increased BPF risk after lung cancer surgery, whereas chemotherapy alone did not show the same magnitude of risk signal. Right sided pneumonectomy (not left sided) also carries higher BPF risk, reflecting anatomic and vascular factors. 
(b) Chemotherapy alone is not as strongly associated with BPF as radiotherapy in pooled data. 
(c) Left pneumonectomy has a lower risk compared with right. 
(d) Advanced age/COPD contribute perioperative risk but are not as predictive as prior radiotherapy in multivariable analyses. 
(e) Early extubation is generally favorable; prolonged positive pressure ventilation is more problematic. 
Pearl: Right pneumonectomy and preoperative radiotherapy are classic high risk settings for BPF—anticipate and mitigate aggressively.
###References###
1.	Li S, et al. Jpn J Clin Oncol. 2016;46:534 546. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27161492/
2.	Darling GE, et al. Ann Thorac Surg. 2005;79:433 437. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15680866/
