### Question  ###
A 58-year-old woman with emphysema is intubated for pneumonia and develops a large bronchopleural fistula with continuous bubbling to water seal. Ventilator: volume control, VT 6 mL/kg predicted body weight, PEEP 8 cm H₂O, FiO₂ 0.6, plateau 28 cm H₂O. ABG shows permissive hypercapnia but tolerable pH. Which ventilator adjustment best reduces fistula flow while maintaining safety?
 ### Options### 
(a) Increase PEEP from 8 to 14 cm H₂O to splint the airways
(b) Increase inspiratory time to 1.5 s to improve oxygenation
(c) Reduce PEEP and tidal volume to the lowest tolerable settings and accept permissive hypercapnia
(d) Switch to high mean airway pressure ventilation (e.g., HFOV)
(e) Convert to APRV with high PHigh to enhance recruitment
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Minimize driving pressure across the fistula—lower PEEP and VT with permissive hypercapnia is preferred (choice c). Reviews on postoperative air leaks and BPF emphasize strategies that reduce airway pressures to limit flow through the defect, using lung protective volumes and the lowest PEEP compatible with oxygenation. Surgical/critical care reviews similarly advocate pressure minimization and individualized rescue strategies (e.g., independent lung ventilation) when needed. 
(a), (d), (e) Increasing mean airway pressure typically worsens trans fistula flow and can perpetuate the leak. 
(b) Prolonging inspiratory time increases mean airway pressure, often counterproductive for a BPF. 
Pearl: For BPFs, think “pressure sparing”: the lowest practical VT and PEEP, with permissive hypercapnia, mitigate ongoing leak while definitive therapy is arranged.
###References###
1.	Clark JM, Cooke DT, Brown LM. Thorac Surg Clin. 2020;30:347 358. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/32593367/
2.	Toste PA, Revels SL. Surg Clin North Am. 2021;101:911 923. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/34537151/

### Question  ###
A 61-year-old man with COPD has a persistent air leak 2 weeks after necrotizing pneumonia. Thin section CT (1.0 mm) shows bronchiectasis and a loculated effusion but no definite tract. He is stable and extubated. Which diagnostic study is most likely to localize a small peripheral BPF to guide therapy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Repeat standard chest CT with 5 mm slices
(b) Bronchoscopy with dye on the stump only
(c) Ventilation scintigraphy with 99mTc DTPA
(d) CT bronchography by instilling contrast through the bronchoscope
(e) Pleural manometry during thoracentesis
###Discussion###
Bottom line: CT bronchography can delineate small/difficult BPFs when routine CT is nondiagnostic—choice (d). Westcott and Volpe showed that even thin section CT visualized postoperative/peripheral BPFs inconsistently, particularly when the tract is tiny. CT bronchography, in which contrast is instilled via bronchoscope and imaged under CT/fluoroscopy, has been reported to localize otherwise occult BPFs and to inform management. Ventilation scintigraphy can detect BPFs but may lack precise anatomic localization and is less practical if the goal is segmental targeting. 
(a) Repeating standard CT with thicker sections reduces sensitivity. 
(b) Dye on the stump detects central leaks, not distal segmental fistulas. 
(c) Scintigraphy detects presence more than exact segment; it’s not the best for precise localization. 
(e) Pleural manometry does not localize a bronchial communication.
Pearl: When thin section CT is nondiagnostic, CT bronchography offers actionable anatomic detail for targeted bronchoscopy or surgery.
###References###
1.	Westcott JL, Volpe JP. Radiology. 1995;196:175 181. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/7784563/
2.	Sarkar P, et al. J Thorac Imaging. 2010;25:W10 W13. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20160584/
3.	Raja S, et al. Eur J Nucl Med. 1999;26:215 219. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/10079310/

### Question  ###
A 70-year-old man with poor performance status develops a 10 mm circular bronchial stump defect 3 weeks after right pneumonectomy. He is afebrile but malnourished and deemed prohibitive risk for thoracotomy. Pleural space is adequately drained. Which bronchoscopic strategy is most appropriate as definitive therapy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Deploy an Amplatzer type occluder device across the stump defect sized to slightly exceed the orifice
(b) Place endobronchial valves in right lobar bronchi to reduce flow
(c) Instill fibrin glue onto the defect via flexible bronchoscopy
(d) Use a Fogarty catheter and Surgicel to plug the defect temporarily
(e) Place a straight silicone stent across the carina to bridge the defect
###Discussion###
Bottom line: For a large, discrete central stump defect in a nonoperative candidate, bronchoscopic closure with an Amplatzer occluder has the best supportive series—choice (a). Case series demonstrate successful closure of central BPFs (often ≥8–10 mm) with septal/vascular occluders placed under bronchoscopic/fluoroscopic guidance, with durable follow up. Long term outcomes from the same group support sustained closure in most patients. Additional reports confirm feasibility for central, round defects. 
(b) Valves are suited to distal/prolonged air leaks by isolating a lobe; they do not physically close a large stump hole. 
(c) Sealants are more effective for small (e.g., <5 mm) defects; large dehiscence has poor glue durability. 
(d) Temporary tamponade is not definitive and is prone to displacement.
(e) Stents may palliate selected central fistulas, but customizing/anchoring for a solitary stump defect is challenging; migration and secretion issues limit use. 
Pearl: Amplatzer type devices are particularly suited to round, central defects that are too large for glues and not amenable to lobar isolation.
###References###
1.	Fruchter O, et al. Chest. 2011;139:682 687. https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC3241098/
2.	Fruchter O, et al. Respiration. 2014;87:227 233. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24281307/
3.	Klotz LV, et al. J Thorac Dis. 2015;7:1478 1482. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26380735/
4.	Chae EY, et al. Ann Thorac Surg. 2010;89:293 296. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20103263/

### Question  ###
A 64-year-old man with cachexia (BMI 16) and diabetes develops post pneumonectomy empyema with a small BPF 18 days after surgery. He is afebrile but frail, with persistent purulent drainage and a large postpneumonectomy space. Which procedure best provides durable, dependent drainage while he undergoes nutritional rehabilitation before later definitive closure?
 ### Options### 
(a) VATS talc pleurodesis
(b) Repeat bronchoscopy with sealant application
(c) Percutaneous pigtail drainage alone
(d) Creation of a modified Eloesser thoracostomy (open window thoracostomy)
(e) Immediate thoracoplasty
###Discussion###
Bottom line: A modified Eloesser thoracostomy provides continuous dependent pleural drainage in chronically ill patients with post pneumonectomy empyema and BPF—choice (d). Large institutional series over 26 years describe the modified Eloesser flap as a safe, durable option that allows ongoing drainage and stabilization, particularly useful as part of staged management in debilitated patients. Early PPE often features polymicrobial flora and worse outcomes than delayed cases, reinforcing the need for robust drainage and control of sepsis before definitive closure. 
(a) Chemical pleurodesis is ineffective in an unapposed, infected space.
(b) Sealants do not address the infected postpneumonectomy cavity and are unlikely to succeed without source control.
(c) A single percutaneous drain is usually inadequate for a large postpneumonectomy empyema cavity.
(e) Thoracoplasty may be considered later; it is not the initial stabilization strategy in a cachectic patient.
Pearl: For PPE with BPF in frail patients, staged therapy starting with an Eloesser window can be lifesaving by achieving reliable drainage.
###References###
1.	Thourani VH, et al. Ann Thorac Surg. 2003;76:401 406. https://www.annalsthoracicsurgery.org/article/S0003-4975(03)00470-3/fulltext
2.	Stern JB, et al. Clin Respir J. 2018;12:1753 1761. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/29114998/

### Question  ###
A 73-year-old man with COPD has a prolonged air leak after necrotizing pneumonia. Endoscopic bronchial occlusion is performed using endobronchial Watanabe spigots (EWS) in the involved bronchi. Which complication should the team most anticipate and mitigate during early follow up?
 ### Options### 
(a) Extensive granulation tissue overgrowth causing severe stenosis
(b) Spigot migration/dislodgement requiring repositioning or replacement
(c) Massive hemoptysis from bronchial arterial erosion
(d) High grade atrioventricular block during insertion
(e) Refractory hypercapnia from diffuse air trapping
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Dislocation/migration of silicone spigots is the most frequent adverse event after EWS placement—choice (b). A Japanese series of 21 patients reported favorable chest tube removal rates but highlighted spigot dislocation as a common issue; antitussives are often recommended post implantation. A nationwide analysis of >1,000 EWS cases confirmed substantial treatment failure with notable in hospital mortality, underscoring the need for careful selection and vigilant follow up (with migration among practical complications). 
(a) Granulation can occur but is less frequent than migration. 
(c) Major bleeding is uncommon with EWS.
(d) AV block is not a recognized EWS complication.
(e) Regional atelectasis may occur, but refractory hypercapnia is not the predominant complication profile.
Pearl: Plan for migration: educate staff, use imaging confirmation, and consider cough suppression in the early post placement period.
###References###
1.	Himeji D, et al. Intern Med. 2020;59:1835 1839. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/32378659/
2.	Taniguchi J, et al. Respir Investig. 2024;62:449 454. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/38617861/

### Question  ###
A 60-year-old man, 3 weeks after right middle lobectomy, has a 2 mm central bronchial stump fistula visible on bronchoscopy. He is afebrile, pleural space is drained, and he is a poor surgical candidate. Which bronchoscopic technique has supportive series data for closing small central BPFs?
 ### Options### 
(a) Deploying an Amplatzer septal occluder across the stump
(b) Endobronchial Watanabe spigot placement in segmental bronchi
(c) Submucosal injections of absolute ethanol (0.1 mL aliquots) circumferentially around the fistula
(d) Straight silicone stent across the mainstem bronchus
(e) Lobar endobronchial one way valve placement
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Submucosal absolute ethanol injection around small central BPFs has been reported to achieve closure in case series—choice (c). Takaoka et al. described successful closure of central, bronchoscopy visible postoperative fistulas <3 mm using 0.1 mL aliquots of absolute ethanol injected circumferentially, sometimes over multiple sessions. A contemporary review also describes ethanol as a sclerosing option for selected small, central fistulas when surgery is high risk. 
(a) Occluder devices are more appropriate for larger central defects (often ≥8–10 mm). 
(b) Spigots are designed to occlude segmental bronchi and are used more commonly for peripheral leaks or persistent pneumothorax. 
(d) Stents can palliate central fistulas but are not typically used for tiny, discrete stump defects. 
(e) Valves mitigate distal alveolar leaks; they do not directly seal a small central stump hole. 
Pearl: For tiny, central, bronchoscopy visible fistulas in high risk patients, sclerosing injections (e.g., absolute ethanol) can be effective with careful technique and patience.
###References###
1.	Takaoka K, Inoue S, Ohira S. Chest. 2002;122:374 378. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12171839/
2.	Abramian O, et al. Ther Adv Respir Dis. 2021;15:17534666211044411. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/34637469/
3.	Fruchter O, et al. Chest. 2011;139:682 687. https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC3241098/
Conventional Bronchoscopy
### Question  ###
A 67 year old man (55 pack years, quit 5 years ago) undergoes diagnostic flexible bronchoscopy for hemoptysis and weight loss. On room air he maintains SpO₂ 96%. CT with 1 mm slices shows a 2 cm exophytic mass arising from the right mainstem bronchus without distal atelectasis. He takes no anticoagulants or antiplatelet agents; platelets are 220,000/µL and INR 1.0. During bronchoscopy you see a friable, polypoid endobronchial lesion with contact bleeding. You plan conventional forceps biopsies and want to balance diagnostic yield against bleeding risk and procedure time. What is the optimal routine number of forceps biopsies to obtain from this visible endobronchial tumor?
 ### Options### 
(a) Three to four biopsies
(b) One biopsy is sufficient
(c) Six to eight biopsies are needed
(d) Ten biopsies minimize false negatives
(e) The number of biopsies does not influence yield
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Diagnostic yield for visible endobronchial cancer plateaus after three to four forceps biopsies (choice A correct). Classic prospective series show marked gains from the second and third specimens, with only marginal improvement beyond the fourth; taking more increases bleeding and time without meaningful benefit. Gellert et al. and Shure & Astarita independently demonstrated that three or four samples optimize sensitivity for endobronchial malignancy, informing modern practice. Pearl: Stop after three to four good bites unless specific circumstances warrant more (e.g., necrosis precluding viable tissue). Options (c) and (d) reflect a common misconception that “more is always better”; data do not support routine ≥6 biopsies in visible lesions. Option (b) risks under sampling, especially when crush artifact or necrosis is present. Option (e) is contradicted by studies correlating yield with the number of specimens. Practical implications: prioritize quality (target viable, non necrotic tissue) over quantity; if repeated biopsies still produce necrotic or “white” tissue, consider switching tools (e.g., TBNA) rather than simply increasing the count. 
###References###
1.	Gellert AR, Rudd RM, Sinha G, et al. Effect of multiple bronchial biopsies on diagnostic yield in bronchogenic carcinoma. Thorax. 1982;37:684 687. https://doi.org/10.1136/thx.37.9.684
2.	Shure D, Astarita RW. Bronchogenic carcinoma presenting as an endobronchial mass: optimal number of biopsy specimens. Chest. 1983;83:865 867. https://doi.org/10.1378/chest.83.6.865

### Question  ###
A 59 year old woman (30 pack years) presents with cough and recurrent right upper lobe collapse. Thin section CT (1 mm) shows an endobronchial lesion at the right upper lobe orifice. PET is pending. During bronchoscopy on FiO₂ 0.30, you see a friable, exophytic tumor with superficial necrosis. You plan to perform both bronchial brushing and forceps biopsy in the same session and wish to maximize cytologic yield while minimizing blood contamination of smears. Which sequencing strategy is best?
 ### Options### 
(a) Brushing after biopsy improves sensitivity
(b) Brushing before biopsy improves sensitivity
(c) Sequence does not affect sensitivity
(d) Avoid brushing if biopsy is planned
(e) Brush only after bronchial washing
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Perform brushing before forceps biopsy to maximize cytologic sensitivity (choice B correct). In a randomized study of visible exophytic tumors, pre biopsy brushing produced higher diagnostic yield than post biopsy brushing—likely because biopsy introduces blood and crush artifact that degrade cytology. Adding brushing to biopsy modestly increases overall diagnostic yield in central tumors; the incremental value is small but real. Pearl: Collect brushings early—before bleeding obscures the field. Option (a) is inferior based on the trial results. Option (c) is incorrect; sequence matters when bleeding is expected. Option (d) discards a complementary modality; while histology often suffices, cytology can be diagnostic in a measurable minority. Option (e) is unnecessary; washing can be done opportunistically, but its standalone sensitivity is lower and timing relative to biopsy or brushing does not consistently change yield. In practice, pre biopsy brushing → forceps biopsy is a sensible order when dealing with friable lesions that bleed. 
###References###
1.	Hou G, Miao Y, Hu XJ, et al. Optimal sequence for bronchial brushing and forceps biopsy in lung cancer diagnosis: randomized study. J Thorac Dis. 2016;8:520 526. https://doi.org/10.21037/jtd.2016.02.12
2.	Mak VHF, Johnston IDA, Hetzel MR, et al. Value of washings and brushings at fiberoptic bronchoscopy in lung cancer. Thorax. 1990;45:373 376. https://doi.org/10.1136/thx.45.5.373

### Question  ###
A 70 year old man with COPD (FEV₁ 58% predicted) and 40 pack years undergoes bronchoscopy for a central lesion seen on CT. The tumor elevates the mucosa of the bronchus intermedius; the surface appears firm and intact with minimal irregularity. CT suggests a thick submucosal component with suspected central necrosis. He is on low dose aspirin alone; platelets 190,000/µL. You want to maximize diagnostic material while minimizing bleeding, given his marginal pulmonary reserve and the lesion’s appearance. Which single conventional sampling method is most likely to be diagnostic in this scenario?
 ### Options### 
(a) Endobronchial forceps biopsy
(b) Bronchial brushing
(c) Transbronchial needle aspiration (TBNA)
(d) Bronchial washing
(e) Curette sampling
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Use TBNA to sample suspected submucosal or peribronchial disease through intact mucosa (choice C correct). TBNA penetrates beyond the mucosal surface, bypassing superficial necrosis to retrieve deeper viable cells with less bleeding than forceps. Shure & Fedullo’s series highlighted TBNA’s value in submucosal/peribronchial bronchogenic carcinoma, where forceps, brushing, or washing—each limited to superficial layers—often miss tumor. Pearl: A firm, intact mucosal dome over a CT bulky wall lesion is a TBNA cue. Option (a) risks nondiagnostic superficial bites and more bleeding in hypervascular tissue. Option (b) and (d) collect only exfoliated surface cells and perform poorly when the surface is uninvolved. Option (e) remains a surface tool and is less commonly used; evidence of superiority over TBNA is lacking. The schematic on page 6, Fig. 6 of the chapter visually demonstrates how a needle accesses the deeper, viable component of a necrotic submucosal lesion, explaining the diagnostic advantage in this setting. 
###References###
1.	Shure D, Fedullo PF. TBNA in submucosal and peribronchial bronchogenic carcinoma. Chest. 1985;88:49 51. https://doi.org/10.1378/chest.88.1.49
2.	Mazzone P, Jain P, Arroliga AC, et al. Bronchoscopy and needle biopsy techniques for lung cancer. Clin Chest Med. 2002;23:137 158. https://doi.org/10.1016/S0272-5231(03)00065-0

### Question  ###
During forceps biopsy of a right main bronchus tumor in a 63 year old man (CAD on no antithrombotics), brisk bleeding obscures the field. He is breathing spontaneously (FiO₂ 0.40 via nasal cannula integrated with bronchoscopy) and becomes acutely hypoxemic to SpO₂ 82%. Blood is pooling and threatens the left lung. What is the first maneuver that most effectively limits contralateral flooding and buys time for definitive hemostasis (e.g., topical vasoconstrictors, balloon tamponade, thermal tools at safe FiO₂) during flexible bronchoscopy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Instill large aliquots of ice cold saline
(b) Instill epinephrine 1:10,000 in small aliquots
(c) Inflate a Fogarty balloon at the segmental orifice
(d) Turn the patient into lateral decubitus with the bleeding lung dependent
(e) Proceed immediately to rigid bronchoscopy under general anesthesia
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Immediately place the patient bleeding side down in lateral decubitus to protect the contralateral lung (choice D correct). Large series and expert reviews emphasize dependent positioning as the first, rapid maneuver during major endobronchial hemorrhage; it limits spillage to the non bleeding lung and allows time for topical agents, tamponade, or escalation. Pearl: Position first, then pharmacology and tamponade—airway protection trumps everything. Options (a) and (b) are commonly used but should follow airway protection; evidence for their standalone efficacy is limited, and they do not prevent cross contamination early. Option (c) is effective once the bleeding segment is localized and a catheter is in position, but setup takes time. Option (e) may be necessary if bleeding persists or airway control fails, yet immediate conversion delays the most time critical protective step and may not be feasible. After positioning, keep the scope in the airway, suction continuously, consider cold saline/epinephrine, and advance to balloon tamponade or thermal coagulation as needed; Fig. 3 on page 4 illustrates balloon use. 
###References###
1.	Cordasco EM, Mehta AC, Ahmad M. Bronchoscopically induced bleeding: 9 year experience and literature review. Chest. 1991;100:1141 1147. https://doi.org/10.1378/chest.100.4.1141
2.	Lee P, Mehta AC, Mathur PN. Management of complications from bronchoscopy. Respirology. 2009;14:940 953. https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1440-1843.2009.01617.x

### Question  ###
A 72 year old woman with a vascular appearing endobronchial renal cell carcinoma metastasis bleeds diffusely after forceps biopsy despite cold saline and three 2 mL aliquots of epinephrine (1:10,000). She remains spontaneously breathing with FiO₂ 0.30. Suction clears the airway intermittently, but oozing from a segmental bronchus persists and visualization is poor. You wish to tamponade the culprit airway quickly through the flexible bronchoscope to prevent distal flooding while planning definitive hemostasis. Which device is specifically designed for endobronchial tamponade through the working channel?
 ### Options### 
(a) Sengstaken–Blakemore tube
(b) Bougie dilator
(c) Laryngeal mask airway cuff
(d) Double lumen endotracheal tube
(e) Fogarty balloon catheter
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Use a Fogarty balloon catheter passed through the bronchoscope to tamponade the bleeding segment (choice E correct). The Fogarty balloon (typically 4–7 Fr) can be positioned in the targeted segmental orifice and gently inflated to compress the source, limit blood spillage, and stabilize oxygenation while additional measures are prepared. Pearl: Have a Fogarty balloon immediately available in your bronchoscopy suite. Options (a), (c), and (d) are not designed for selective segmental tamponade via a bronchoscope (GI device, supraglottic airway, and lung isolation tube, respectively). Option (b) is an airway adjunct, not a hemostatic device. Reports and reviews describe the practicality and safety of Fogarty assisted tamponade during bronchoscopy for significant bleeding, often in combination with dependent positioning and topical vasoconstrictors. This stepwise approach can avert emergent conversion to rigid bronchoscopy or surgery. 
###References###
1.	Kshettry VR, Bassett HFM. The Fogarty balloon technique for control of endobronchial bleeding. Thorax. 1983;38:640. https://doi.org/10.1136/thx.38.8.640
2.	Lee P, Mehta AC, Mathur PN. Management of complications from bronchoscopy. Respirology. 2009;14:940 953. https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1440-1843.2009.01617.x
________________________________________
### Question  ###
A 68 year old man (40 pack years) has a new 2.8 cm, lobulated left upper lobe mass. Thin section CT shows a bronchus sign coursing into the lesion; there is no adjacent emphysema. Biplane fluoroscopy is available; EBUS miniprobe is not. You plan a conventional bronchoscopy under moderate sedation (FiO₂ 0.28) with the goal of securing malignancy diagnosis on the first pass and minimizing repeats. Which initial sampling strategy best maximizes sensitivity for malignancy in fluoroscopy visible peripheral lesions?
 ### Options### 
(a) TBNA first, then add forceps biopsy if needed
(b) Bronchoalveolar lavage alone
(c) Brushing alone
(d) Forceps biopsy alone
(e) Washing plus lavage
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Start with TBNA for fluoroscopy guided sampling of peripheral lesions, then add forceps if needed (choice A correct). Systematic review data show TBNA has higher sensitivity for malignancy than forceps or brushing under fluoroscopy; combining methods further improves overall yield. Needles can sample lesions not breaking the mucosal surface or located peribronchiolarly, explaining superior performance in cancer. Pearl: In peripheral lesions, needle first—biopsy/brushing can blood contaminate subsequent aspirations. Options (b), (e) have poor standalone sensitivity for focal nodules/masses and add little when used alone. Option (c) underperforms TBNA and is more vulnerable to bleeding related smear degradation. Option (d) is reasonable for benign processes but often yields less malignant diagnostic material than TBNA. Practical tip: when multiple tools are used, perform TBNA before forceps/brushing to avoid blood filled aspirations; this sequencing is emphasized in conventional approaches. 
###References###
1.	Mondoni M, Sotgiu G, Bonifazi M, et al. TBNA in peripheral pulmonary lesions: systematic review and meta analysis. Eur Respir J. 2016;48:196 204. https://doi.org/10.1183/13993003.00051-2016
2.	Wang KP, Haponik EF, Britt EJ, et al. TBNA of peripheral pulmonary nodules. Chest. 1984;86:819 823. https://doi.org/10.1378/chest.86.6.819
3.	Gasparini S, Ferretti M, Bichi Secchi E, et al. Integrated transbronchial/percutaneous approach for peripheral nodules. Chest. 1995;108:131 137. https://doi.org/10.1378/chest.108.1.131

### Question  ###
A 61 year old woman (never smoker) is referred for bronchoscopic sampling of a 1.6 cm left upper lobe nodule discovered on lung cancer screening. She is a potential surgical candidate (PFTs: FEV₁ 92% predicted, DLCO 85%). High resolution CT with 1 mm slices demonstrates a tubular airway leading directly into the nodule, which is part solid with a 9 mm solid core. No calcification is present; the lesion lies 4.8 cm from the hilum. PET has not yet been performed. You plan conventional bronchoscopy with fluoroscopy. Which radiographic feature most strongly predicts successful transbronchial sampling?
 ### Options### 
(a) Spiculated margins
(b) Presence of a “bronchus sign” leading into the lesion
(c) Distance >3 cm from hilum
(d) Popcorn calcification
(e) Pure ground glass attenuation
###Discussion###
Bottom line: The bronchus sign on thin section CT is the strongest predictor of bronchoscopic sampling success (choice B correct). When a bronchus leads into or lies within a lesion, instruments can reach the target airway more reliably, improving diagnostic yield, even for small nodules. Pearl: Always scrutinize the thin section CT for a bronchus sign before planning tools and approach. Option (a) may raise malignancy probability but does not by itself predict bronchoscopic access. Option (c) (distance from hilum) has mixed predictive value and is less important than airway lesion continuity. Option (d) suggests hamartoma but is not a predictor of sampling success. Option (e) (pure ground glass) is associated with lower bronchoscopic yields compared with solid/part solid lesions; reliance on bronchoscopy may be misplaced for pure GGNs. Thin section correlation studies (including EBUS GS contexts) consistently identify the bronchus sign as a key predictor for successful transbronchial diagnosis. 
###References###
1.	Minezawa T, Okamura T, Yatsuya H, et al. Bronchus sign on thin section CT predicts successful transbronchial biopsy. BMC Med Imaging. 2015;15:21. https://doi.org/10.1186/s12880-015-0060-5
2.	Naidich DP, Sussman R, Kutcher WL, et al. Solitary pulmonary nodules: CT–bronchoscopic correlation. Chest. 1988;93:595 598. https://doi.org/10.1378/chest.93.3.595

### Question  ###
A 62 year old man with a prior drug eluting coronary stent (placed 2 years ago) is scheduled for elective flexible bronchoscopy with endobronchial forceps biopsies of a right mainstem tumor seen on CT. He takes aspirin 81 mg daily for secondary prevention; no other antiplatelets or anticoagulants. Labs: platelets 175,000/µL; INR 1.0; creatinine normal. He has no bleeding history. You want to minimize bleeding risk without increasing cardiovascular risk. What is the most appropriate antiplatelet management?
 ### Options### 
(a) Transfuse platelets to >100,000/µL before biopsy
(b) Stop aspirin 7 days prior to biopsy
(c) Continue low dose aspirin without interruption
(d) Stop aspirin and substitute clopidogrel
(e) Proceed only if INR ≤ 1.0 and aspirin is withheld
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Continue low dose aspirin for diagnostic flexible bronchoscopy with planned forceps biopsies (choice C correct). Contemporary guidelines support proceeding without stopping aspirin when platelet count and coagulation parameters are acceptable; withholding aspirin increases cardiovascular risk without clearly reducing clinically significant bleeding. Pearl: Aspirin is generally continued; clopidogrel is not. Option (a) is unnecessary; his platelet count is adequate. Option (b) is not recommended for routine diagnostic bronchoscopy and exposes him to stent related risk. Option (d) worsens bleeding risk and provides no benefit. Option (e) conflates warfarin management (INR) with aspirin; INR is already normal and aspirin need not be withheld. In contrast, agents like clopidogrel are typically interrupted for biopsy, and anticoagulants require drug specific management. These recommendations align with BTS guidance for bronchoscopy and ACCP guidance for peri procedural antithrombotic management. 
###References###
1.	Du Rand IA, Blaikley J, Chaudhuri N, et al. BTS guideline for diagnostic flexible bronchoscopy in adults. Thorax. 2013;68:i1 i44. https://doi.org/10.1136/thoraxjnl-2013-203618
2.	Douketis JD, Spyropoulos AC, Murad MH, et al. Perioperative management of antithrombotic therapy (ACCP). Chest. 2022;162:e207 e243. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.chest.2022.07.025

### Question  ###
A 66 year old woman with a 3.2 cm fluoroscopy visible right upper lobe mass is scheduled for peripheral TBNA and adjunctive sampling. The oncologist requests sufficient material for reflex next generation sequencing (EGFR/ALK/ROS1 and others) and PD L1 immunohistochemistry if cancer is confirmed. Rapid on site evaluation is unavailable today. To maximize downstream adequacy for comprehensive genotyping from TBNA, how should you process the first needle passes?
 ### Options### 
(a) Air dry all smears; discard residual material
(b) Rinse needle in heparinized saline; avoid formalin
(c) Prepare alcohol fixed smears only; no cell block
(d) Prepare direct smears and flush residual needle contents into 10% neutral buffered formalin to create a cell block
(e) Spray fix smears and place residual in RBC lysis buffer
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Combine direct smears with formalin fixed cell block processing of TBNA material to support comprehensive genotyping (choice D correct). In a contemporary series of peripheral lung cancers sampled by TBNA under fluoroscopic guidance, smear cytology plus cell block (by flushing residual material into 10% neutral buffered formalin) enabled complete molecular profiling—including PD L1—in the great majority of cases. Pearl: Always save residual TBNA material in formalin for a cell block—don’t waste it. Option (a) squanders cellular material and compromises IHC/molecular tests. Option (b) avoids formalin needed for robust IHC/PD L1 and many NGS workflows. Option (c) omits the cell block that laboratories rely on for ancillary studies. Option (e) uses a non validated buffer for comprehensive pathology; downstream performance is uncertain. While newer tools (e.g., transbronchial cryobiopsy) can yield large tissue fragments, TBNA with proper processing remains adequate for most genotyping needs when performed thoughtfully. 
###References###
1.	Zuccatosta L, Latini L, Belleggia B, et al. Suitability of TBNA for genotyping peripheral pulmonary tumors. Front Med. 2023;9:1087028. https://doi.org/10.3389/fmed.2022.1087028
2.	Mazzone P, Jain P, Arroliga AC, et al. Bronchoscopy and needle biopsy techniques for lung cancer. Clin Chest Med. 2002;23:137 158. https://doi.org/10.1016/S0272-5231(03)00065-0

### Question  ###
A 58 year old man with a 4.5 cm right upper lobe primary mass has CT enlarged mediastinal nodes: subcarinal 2.2 cm (station 7), right paratracheal 1.8 cm (4R), and left paratracheal 1.0 cm (4L). PET shows uptake in the primary and both 7 and 4R. EBUS is unavailable today. Under moderate sedation (SpO₂ 95% on FiO₂ 0.28), you plan conventional TBNA (cTBNA) to stage him before definitive therapy. To maximize first pass yield and minimize contamination, which target should you sample first?
 ### Options### 
(a) 4L (1.0 cm)
(b) 4R (1.8 cm)
(c) Alternate punctures of 4L and 4R
(d) Sample the primary tumor before any nodes
(e) 7 (2.2 cm)
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Sample the subcarinal (station 7) 2.2 cm node first (choice E correct). cTBNA sensitivity increases with node size and is generally higher at subcarinal and right paratracheal sites; larger targets yield more diagnostic aspirates. Meta analytic and staging data report robust accuracy for cTBNA when nodes are ≥2 cm, with diminishing gains beyond ~2.5 cm. Pearl: In cTBNA, big and central first—subcarinal or 4R—before smaller or left sided nodes. Option (a) begins with the smallest, least favorable site, lowering first pass success. Option (b) is reasonable but smaller than 7; starting at 7 better maximizes yield. Option (c) adds no advantage and complicates workflow. Option (d) risks tumor cell contamination of the needle; guidance emphasizes sampling mediastinal nodes before the primary to preserve specificity. Practically, perform ≥4 passes per station as yield rises through the fourth pass; if negative at 4L and 4R after adequate sampling, proceed to alternative modalities when available. 
###References###
1.	Harrow EM, Abi Saleh W, Blum J, et al. Utility of TBNA in staging bronchogenic carcinoma. Am J Respir Crit Care Med. 2000;161:601 607. https://doi.org/10.1164/ajrccm.161.2.9902040
2.	Holty JE, Kuschner WG, Gould MK. Accuracy of TBNA for mediastinal staging: meta analysis. Thorax. 2005;60:949 955. https://doi.org/10.1136/thx.2005.041525
3.	Dasgupta A, Mehta AC. Transbronchial needle aspiration: an underused diagnostic technique. Clin Chest Med. 1999;20:39 51. https://doi.org/10.1016/S0272-5231(05)70125-8

Perc Trach:
### Question  ###
A 58 year old man with maxillofacial trauma after a motor vehicle collision arrives obtunded (GCS 6) and hypoxic despite bag mask ventilation. Two attempts at video laryngoscopy fail; supraglottic airway placement yields poor chest rise. Saturation is 78% on 100% FiO₂. The anterior neck is accessible and landmarks are palpable. What is the single best next step?
 ### Options### 
(a) Perform a surgical cricothyroidotomy immediately
(b) Attempt needle cricothyroidotomy with transtracheal jet ventilation
(c) Place a second supraglottic airway and retry laryngoscopy
(d) Proceed to emergent open surgical tracheostomy at the bedside
(e) Administer paralytic reversal and attempt awake fiber optic intubation
###Discussion###
Bottom line: When both intubation and ventilation fail (“can’t intubate/can’t ventilate”), the recommended rescue is a surgical cricothyroidotomy (choice a). The American Society of Anesthesiologists’ difficult airway guideline identifies surgical airway as the endpoint of failed noninvasive airway control in emergencies. All cited studies in this set are drawn from the chapter reference lists. 
Cricothyroidotomy
Why a is correct: A surgical cricothyroidotomy rapidly establishes oxygenation and ventilation through the cricothyroid membrane, is technically straightforward with palpable landmarks, and is prioritized in guidelines once mask ventilation and intubation attempts fail. 
Why the others are incorrect: b) Needle cricothyroidotomy can temporize oxygenation but provides inadequate CO₂ clearance in adults and is generally a bridge when surgical cricothyroidotomy is not immediately achievable. c) Repeated failed attempts delay definitive oxygenation and risk worsening hypoxemia. d) Emergent tracheostomy is slower and more morbid in this context than cricothyroidotomy. e) Reversing paralysis and attempting awake fiber optic intubation in a hypoxemic, uncooperative trauma patient is unsafe and delays definitive rescue. 
Pearl: In an adult CICO scenario with palpable landmarks, a scalpel bougie (surgical) cricothyroidotomy is the fastest, most reliable path to oxygenation.
###References###
1.	American Society of Anesthesiologists Task Force. Practice guidelines for management of the difficult airway. Anesthesiology. 2003;98:1269 1277. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12717151/
2.	Scrase I, Woollard M. Needle vs surgical cricothyroidotomy: a short cut to effective ventilation. Anaesthesia. 2006;61:962 974. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16978312/

### Question  ###
A 6 year old boy (20 kg) with rapidly progressive facial angioedema from a peanut exposure is apneic. Two mask ventilation attempts fail; the epiglottis cannot be visualized. His neck is short and landmarks are difficult to palpate. What is the best immediate airway strategy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Open surgical cricothyroidotomy with a 5.0 cuffed tube
(b) Needle cricothyroidotomy with transtracheal ventilation as a bridge
(c) Emergent low tracheostomy through the second tracheal ring
(d) Repeat laryngoscopy with a curved blade and external laryngeal manipulation
(e) Retrograde wire guided oral intubation via the cricothyroid membrane
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In children <10–12 years, the preferred emergency surgical airway is needle cricothyroidotomy with transtracheal ventilation (choice b), not open surgical cricothyroidotomy, because the pediatric cricothyroid membrane is very small and at higher risk for laryngeal injury. All cited articles derive from the chapter’s reference list. 
Cricothyroidotomy
Why b is correct: Literature reviews of prehospital/ED pediatric airway rescue emphasize that percutaneous/needle cricothyroidotomy allows rapid oxygenation when surgical cricothyrotomy is technically difficult and hazardous in small airways. 
Why the others are incorrect: a) Open surgical cricothyroidotomy in small children risks extensive laryngeal trauma and subglottic stenosis. c) Emergent tracheostomy is slower and often not feasible as an immediate rescue. d) Repeated laryngoscopy in a CICO scenario delays oxygenation. e) Retrograde intubation is time consuming and technically demanding in a crashing child.
Pitfall: Attempting an adult style surgical cricothyroidotomy in a young child can catastrophically injure the larynx—use needle cricothyroidotomy as the bridge.
###References###
1.	Scrase I, Woollard M. Needle vs surgical cricothyroidotomy: a short cut to effective ventilation. Anaesthesia. 2006;61:962 974. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16978312/
2.	Hsiao J, Pacheco Fowler V. Videos in Clinical Medicine: Cricothyroidotomy. N Engl J Med. 2008;358:e25. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/18579802/

### Question  ###
A 64 year old woman with severe COPD has been orally intubated for 8 days for hypercapnic failure. Anticipating continued ventilator dependence, the ICU team asks whether to perform an elective cricothyroidotomy instead of tracheostomy because her thyroid isthmus is enlarged. Vitals are stable; platelets 230 × 10⁹/L, INR 1.0. What is the best approach?
 ### Options### 
(a) Elective cricothyroidotomy using a percutaneous kit
(b) Delay any surgical airway until day 14 to minimize laryngeal injury
(c) Proceed with tracheostomy rather than cricothyroidotomy
(d) Maintain endotracheal intubation and wean sedation only
(e) Perform needle cricothyroidotomy as a bridge to elective cricothyrotomy
###Discussion###
Bottom line: In patients with antecedent prolonged endotracheal intubation, elective cricothyroidotomy is associated with higher laryngeal complication rates; tracheostomy is preferred (choice c). 
Why c is correct: A classic study found a high complication rate for elective cricothyroidotomy in patients who had already been intubated, concluding antecedent intubation is a contraindication to elective cricothyroidotomy. Subsequent otolaryngology perspectives have reinforced avoiding elective cricothyroidotomy in patients with preexisting laryngeal inflammation/trauma from the ETT. All supporting articles are from the chapter reference list. 
Cricothyroidotomy
Why the others are incorrect: a) Percutaneous cricothyroidotomy as an elective, long term airway in an intubated patient increases risk of subglottic injury. b) Prolonging translaryngeal intubation adds risk without changing the basic contraindication. d) Continued intubation is not optimal given anticipated prolonged ventilation. e) Needle cricothyroidotomy is a temporizing emergency maneuver, not an elective plan.
Pearl: Elective cricothyroidotomy is generally avoided in patients intubated for several days—choose tracheostomy for long term access.
###References###
1.	Weymuller EA Jr, Cummings CW. Cricothyroidotomy: the impact of antecedent endotracheal intubation. Ann Otol Rhinol Laryngol. 1982;91:437 439. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/7114727/
2.	Cole RR, Aguilar EA. Cricothyroidotomy versus tracheotomy: an otolaryngologist’s perspective. Laryngoscope. 1988;98:131 135. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/3336274/

### Question  ###
A 45 year old man underwent emergent surgical cricothyroidotomy for maxillofacial trauma 18 hours ago. He is stable on pressure support ventilation and is likely to need 24–48 more hours of airway access. ENT asks whether to convert the cricothyroidotomy to a tracheostomy now. What is the best course?
 ### Options### 
(a) Immediate conversion to tracheostomy to prevent subglottic stenosis
(b) Conversion within 24 hours to reduce pneumonia risk
(c) Removal of the cricothyroidotomy tube and return to oral intubation
(d) Continue the cricothyroidotomy and defer routine conversion
(e) Perform bronchoscopy guided exchange to a larger cricothyrotomy tube now
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Contemporary evidence does not support routine conversion of emergency cricothyroidotomy to tracheostomy in all patients; if short term access is anticipated, it is reasonable to continue the cricothyroidotomy (choice d). 
Why d is correct: A 2022 systematic review and meta analysis found no evidence that routine conversion reduces complications; subglottic stenosis after cricothyroidotomy appeared rare in modern series. These studies come from the chapter’s reference list. 
Cricothyroidotomy
Why the others are incorrect: a) The historical recommendation for universal, immediate conversion was based on early century concerns that are not borne out by more recent data. b) No high quality evidence shows earlier conversion decreases infection risk. c) Exchanging to oral intubation risks losing the airway in a trauma patient with difficult anatomy. e) Upsizing without indication risks additional laryngeal injury.
Pitfall: Assuming all cricothyroidotomies must be converted within 48–72 hours—modern data suggest selective, not routine, conversion.
###References###
1.	Choi J, Anderson TN, Sheira D, et al. The need to routinely convert emergency cricothyroidotomy to tracheostomy: a systematic review and meta analysis. J Am Coll Surg. 2022;234:947 952. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/35426409/
2.	Talving P, DuBose J, Inaba K, et al. Conversion of emergent cricothyroidotomy to tracheotomy in trauma patients. Arch Surg. 2010;145:87 91. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20083758/

### Question  ###
During an ED airway course, learners practice two surgical cricothyroidotomy techniques on cadavers: the standard technique (vertical skin incision, horizontal membrane incision, hook traction, dilator, then tube) and the rapid four step technique (RFST; horizontal stab through skin and membrane, then hook traction below the incision). Which statement best reflects current evidence from comparative studies?
 ### Options### 
(a) RFST provides similar success with fewer complications than standard technique
(b) RFST and standard technique have identical complication profiles in cadavers
(c) Standard technique is faster but at higher risk of cricoid fracture than RFST
(d) RFST is preferred for elective surgical airways because it uses fewer instruments
(e) Standard technique is safer, whereas RFST is faster but associated with more complications
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Comparative trials in cadaver models show the RFST is faster but has more complications (notably cricoid fractures) than standard technique; thus standard technique is safer (choice e). All studies cited are from the chapter’s reference list. 
Cricothyroidotomy
Why e is correct: Randomized crossover studies demonstrated that while RFST reduced time to airway entry, it increased structural laryngeal injuries compared with the standard approach. 
Why the others are incorrect: a) Complication rates were higher, not lower, with RFST. b) Profiles differed, with RFST causing more fractures. c) The standard technique is generally slower, not faster. d) RFST is an emergency simplification, not an elective preference.
Pearl: Speed should not trump safety—when feasible, favor the standard, stepwise surgical cricothyroidotomy technique.
###References###
1.	Davis DP, Bramwell KJ, Vilke GM, et al. Cricothyrotomy technique: standard versus the rapid four step technique. J Emerg Med. 1999;17:17 21. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/9950380/
2.	Holmes JF, Panacek EA, Sakles JC, et al. Comparison of two cricothyroidotomy techniques. Ann Emerg Med. 1998;32:442 446. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/9774939/
3.	Brofeldt BT, Panacek EA, Richards JR. An easy cricothyrotomy approach: the rapid four step technique. Acad Emerg Med. 1996;3:1060 1063. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/8922017/

### Question  ###
A 52 year old woman with morbid obesity (BMI 45 kg/m²) and neck edema from fluid resuscitation is deteriorating with stridor. Landmarks are poorly palpable. Which immediate step best increases safety before a potential emergency cricothyroidotomy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Use handheld point of care ultrasound to identify and mark the cricothyroid membrane
(b) Prep for percutaneous tracheostomy at the bedside using Seldinger technique
(c) Obtain a contrast neck CT to delineate vascular anatomy
(d) Attempt retrograde wire assisted oral intubation through the cricothyroid membrane
(e) Administer high dose steroids and defer any invasive airway plan
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Point of care ultrasound (POCUS) can quickly identify the cricothyroid membrane when landmarks are difficult, improving procedural accuracy and potentially reducing complications; marking the membrane with POCUS (choice a) is best. This approach appears in the chapter’s reference list for cricothyroidotomy. 
Cricothyroidotomy
Why a is correct: A CHest review supports handheld POCUS to localize the cricothyroid membrane during emergency airway management, especially in obese or edematous necks, to expedite and guide cricothyroidotomy. 
Why the others are incorrect: b) Percutaneous tracheostomy is not the initial rescue for evolving upper airway obstruction. c) CT imaging delays care and may be unobtainable in instability. d) Retrograde techniques are time consuming in a decompensating patient. e) Steroids may help edema but do not address imminent airway failure.
Pearl: Mark the cricothyroid membrane early with POCUS in difficult neck patients; the mark can be lifesaving if a CICO scenario unfolds.
###References###
1.	Austin DR, Chang MG, Bittner EA. Use of handheld point of care ultrasound in emergency airway management. Chest. 2021;159:1155 1165. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/32971075/

### Question  ###
A 71 year old man (BMI 29 kg/m²) with severe systolic heart failure is ventilated for cardiogenic shock. He has a drug eluting stent placed 10 days ago and remains on aspirin and clopidogrel; he also receives a low dose unfractionated heparin infusion. Platelets 165 × 10⁹/L, INR 1.1. He needs a long term airway. What is the best plan regarding percutaneous tracheostomy (PT)?
 ### Options### 
(a) Defer PT until dual antiplatelet therapy (DAPT) can be stopped for ≥5 days
(b) Proceed with bronchoscopy guided PT while continuing DAPT, holding heparin peri procedurally
(c) Convert to open surgical tracheostomy in the OR because bleeding risk is prohibitive
(d) Stop all antithrombotics for 24 hours and proceed with PT without bronchoscopy
(e) Place a surgical tracheostomy now and restart antithrombotics tomorrow
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Evidence suggests PT can be performed safely in patients on antiplatelet therapy, including DAPT, with careful technique and peri procedural heparin interruption; proceed with bronchoscopy guided PT while continuing DAPT and holding heparin (choice b). This plan and sources come from the chapter’s PT reference list. 
Perc Trach
Why b is correct: Retrospective series and prospective cohorts show acceptable bleeding with PT on antiplatelets; a study of patients on DAPT/anticoagulation reported low major bleeding with brief heparin holds. Bleeding risk may be higher vs. no antithrombotics, but most events are minor and manageable. 
Why the others are incorrect: a) Deferral may be unsafe given airway needs; many centers proceed under DAPT when stopping is high risk. c) Open tracheostomy is not proven safer for bleeding in this context. d) Abrupt cessation of all agents for 24 h is inadequate for DAPT washout and omits bronchoscopy that reduces complications. e) Immediate surgical tracheostomy does not mitigate antiplatelet related bleeding risk and adds transport/OR risks.
Pearl: For necessary PT under DAPT, hold heparin briefly, use bronchoscopic guidance, and plan meticulous hemostasis—most bleeding is minor.
###References###
1.	Abouzgheib W, Meena N, Jagtap P, et al. Percutaneous dilational tracheostomy in patients receiving antiplatelet therapy: is it safe? J Bronchology Interv Pulmonol. 2013;20:322 325. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24162115/
2.	Lüsebrink E, Stark K, Bertlich M, et al. Safety of percutaneous dilatational tracheotomy in patients on dual antiplatelet therapy and anticoagulation. Crit Care Explor. 2019;1:e0050. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/32166231/

4.	Shen G, Yin H, Cao Y, et al. FOB guided vs unguided PT: randomized trial. Ir J Med Sci. 2019;188:675 681. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30099716/

### Question  ###
A 59 year old woman with ARDS (PaO₂/FiO₂ 130) on pressure control ventilation requires prolonged mechanical ventilation. Ventilator settings: FiO₂ 0.7, PEEP 16 cm H₂O, Vt 6 mL/kg. She is hemodynamically stable. The ICU team considers bedside bronchoscopy guided PT. What is the best recommendation?
 ### Options### 
(a) Decrease PEEP to ≤8 cm H₂O before PT to avoid desaturation
(b) Avoid PT; transfer to the OR for open tracheostomy under general anesthesia
(c) Proceed with bronchoscopy guided PT at the bedside without routine PEEP reduction
(d) Delay tracheostomy until FiO₂ <0.4 and PEEP <10 cm H₂O
(e) Switch to high frequency oscillatory ventilation and perform PT in the OR
###Discussion###
Bottom line: High PEEP alone is not a contraindication to bedside PT; studies report stable oxygenation with bronchoscopy guided PT even at elevated PEEP. Proceed with PT without routine PEEP reduction (choice c). The evidence cited is included in the tracheostomy chapter’s reference list. 
Perc Trach
Why c is correct: An ICU series demonstrated that bronchoscopic, dilatational tracheostomy did not jeopardize oxygenation at 1 and 24 hours in patients ventilated with high PEEP, arguing against categorical deferral. 
Why the others are incorrect: a) Routine PEEP reduction risks derecruitment and hypoxemia. b) Open tracheostomy is not required solely due to high PEEP. d) Waiting for arbitrary thresholds delays potential benefits of tracheostomy. e) HFOV is unnecessary; PT has even been reported during HFOV when needed, but this is not the standard approach. 
Pearl: With experienced operators and bronchoscopic guidance, bedside PT can be safely performed in patients requiring substantial PEEP.
###References###
1.	Beiderlinden M, Groeben H, Peters J. Safety of PDT in patients ventilated with high PEEP. Intensive Care Med. 2003;29:944 948. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12589531/
2.	Shah S, Morgan P. Percutaneous dilational tracheostomy during high frequency oscillatory ventilation. Crit Care Med. 2002;30:1762 1764. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12163773/

### Question  ###
A 67 year old woman with morbid obesity and a short neck requires PT for anticipated prolonged ventilation. The team debates whether any adjunct is necessary. Which adjunct most reliably reduces posterior tracheal wall trauma and false passage during PT?
 ### Options### 
(a) Fluoroscopy to confirm wire position and tube depth
(b) Capnography alone to confirm airway placement
(c) Neck ultrasound only to identify the tracheal rings
(d) Fiber optic bronchoscopy during the procedure to visualize puncture and guidewire
(e) No adjunct is necessary if landmarks are palpable and the operator is experienced
###Discussion###
Bottom line: Concurrent fiber optic bronchoscopy during PT increases first pass success, allows precise puncture between rings, and reduces complications (including posterior wall trauma and subcutaneous emphysema); bronchoscopy is preferred (choice d). These data and comparators are drawn from the PT chapter’s reference list. 
Perc Trach
Why d is correct: An RCT demonstrated improved first attempt success and fewer complications when PT was performed with bronchoscopy compared with landmark guided PT alone. Other trials suggest ultrasound can match bronchoscopy for some outcomes, but ultrasound cannot visualize the posterior wall during dilation. 
Why the others are incorrect: a) Fluoroscopy is impractical at bedside and does not show the posterior wall. b) Capnography confirms intratracheal placement after the fact; it does not prevent injury. c) Ultrasound helps mark anatomy but cannot prevent posterior wall laceration during dilation. e) Even experienced operators benefit from real time airway visualization in difficult anatomy.
Pearl: Bronchoscopy is the only bedside adjunct that lets you see the needle, wire, and dilator relative to the posterior wall during PT.
###References###
1.	Shen G, Yin H, Cao Y, et al. FOB guided vs unguided PT in critically ill patients: RCT. Ir J Med Sci. 2019;188:675 681. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30099716/
2.	Gobatto ALN, Besen BAMP, Tierno PFGMM, et al. Ultrasound guided vs bronchoscopy guided PT (TRACHUS): noninferiority RCT. Intensive Care Med. 2016;42:342 351. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26831676/
________________________________________
### Question  ###
Five weeks after an uncomplicated PT placed between the 3rd and 4th rings, a 62 year old man on the ward develops brisk bleeding around the tracheostomy with pulsatile blood in the cannula after a brief “sentinel” ooze earlier in the day. BP 88/56 mm Hg, HR 120/min. What is the most effective immediate bedside maneuver while arranging definitive therapy?
 ### Options### 
(a) Remove the tracheostomy tube and compress the stoma externally
(b) Replace the tracheostomy with a smaller uncuffed tube to improve visualization
(c) Pack the stoma with gauze and raise the head of bed to 60°
(d) Deflate the cuff and place a nasopharyngeal airway to optimize oxygenation
(e) Hyperinflate the cuff and apply finger pressure through the stoma against the anterior wall while activating emergency surgery
###Discussion###
Bottom line: This is classic for tracheo innominate artery fistula (TIF)—a rare, often fatal late complication. Immediate cuff hyperinflation with digital compression of the innominate against the sternum through the stoma is the best temporizing maneuver (choice e) while urgent operative control is arranged. Evidence and complication data are included in the PT chapter’s reference list. 
Perc Trach
Why e is correct: TIF carries high mortality; bedside temporization includes overinflating the cuff to tamponade bleeding and inserting a finger through the stoma to compress the anterior wall/innominate artery until surgical control. 
Why the others are incorrect: a) Removing the tube sacrifices the ability to tamponade bleeding and can worsen hemorrhage. b) Downsizing reduces tamponade and delays control. c) Packing alone is inadequate for arterial bleeding. d) Deflating the cuff increases hemorrhage risk and worsens oxygenation. Tracheostomy complication series underscore hemorrhage as a leading cause of fatality after PT. 
Pearl: A “sentinel bleed” weeks after tracheostomy demands immediate cuff hyperinflation and digital compression—then straight to the OR.
###References###
1.	Scalise P, Prunk SR, Healy D, Votto J. The incidence of tracheoarterial fistula in chronic tracheostomy tubes. Chest. 2005;128:3906 3909. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/16354862/
2.	Simon M, Metschke M, Braune SA, et al. Death after percutaneous dilatational tracheostomy: systematic review. Crit Care. 2013;17:R258. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24168826/
3.	Goldenberg D, Ari EG, Golz A, et al. Tracheotomy complications: a retrospective study of 1130 cases. Otolaryngol Head Neck Surg. 2000;123:495 500. https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/11020193/
